biological sex and gender identity", "standfirst": "For too long, language and statistics have blurred the lines between the two", "trailText": "For too long, language and statistics have blurred the February 2023.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Women's rights groups demand that prisons be kept single-sex, in a protest in Edinburgh in February 2023.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Ken Jack/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>", "body": "<p>Thank you so much for your article (\u201c<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/apr/09/at-last-consensus-emerging-on-protecting-women-only-spaces\" title=\"\">At last, a consensus is emerging on protecting women-only spaces</a>\u201d, Comment). As a left-leaning feminist, doctor and trainee psychotherapist, I wept with joy and relief to see an article that finally seemed to voice that there is a difference between biological sex and gender identity, and sometimes this difference matters.</p> <p>It matters most to the most vulnerable women who are also more likely to be conditioned not to speak up, including female inmates in prison, where a huge percentage have been on the receiving end of male violence and sexual assaults. I have been so upset in response to some of the confluence between sex and gender identity in language and statistics. I feel this serves neither biological women or men, nor trans women or trans men.</p> <p>I desperately want protection for biological women, trans women and trans men from male violence, abuse and discrimination. We shouldn\u2019t have to wait until women are getting their necks broken on sports pitches or more women are raped by males in female-only prisons before we as a society wake up, use our common sense and listen to science.</p> <p>Sexual dimorphism is a biological fact of human existence. This doesn\u2019t inhibit anyone from identifying or expressing themselves however they like and diversity should be celebrated, but sometimes sex matters. Sex-protected rights were fought for and granted for important reasons and we can\u2019t see them erased.<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"><strong>Kate Newman</strong><br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\">Brighton<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"></p> <p>As a trans person, I won\u2019t pretend that Sonia Sodha\u2019s article didn\u2019t deeply wound me. To claim an \u201cemerging consensus\u201d around Gender Recognition Act reform is to construct a public and to draw a line \u2013 right-thinking people on one side, gender ideologues on the other.</p> <p>Only a small number of trans people bother to apply for a gender recognition certificate. The process is frustrating, dehumanising, bureaucratic, but, even if it were not, a GRC has no bearing on your day-to-day life, which toilets you use, how others perceive you. Trans women have been using women\u2019s spaces, especially public toilets, for years, with or without GRCs, but usually without. A GRC enables death and marriage certificates to be filed correctly. That\u2019s all. My status as a trans woman is legally protected under the Equality Act, without a GRC.</p> <p>The government has issued guidance in the last few months advising women\u2019s refuges and similar charities that they may exclude trans women if they believe there are reasonable grounds to do so for others\u2019 benefit. Given the paucity of options for trans women fleeing domestic violence, this is a bad state of affairs. Sodha suggests that GRA reform is all that stands between us and a world where trans women can use whatever bathroom they please. That world is already here, and has been for decades.</p> <p>Finally, I think the assertion that \u201csomeone female with a GRC who gets pregnant would not be covered by Equality Act protections against pregnancy and maternity discrimination\u201d is bogus. Trans men have made clear that the compassionate solution is to reform the Equality Act to extend such protections, rather than removing existing ones.<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"><strong>Ell Johnson</strong><br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\">Norwich<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"></p> <h2>Distorting the truth</h2> <p>Will Hutton rightly warns of the corrosive impact that \u201ctruth denial\u201d will have on democracy (\u201c<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/apr/09/culture-truth-denial-wilting-us-democracy-britain-following-fast\" title=\"\">A culture of truth denial is wilting US democracy and Britain is fast following suit</a>\u201d, Comment). I hope he would agree that Labour\u2019s \u201cattack strategy\u201d demonstrates that this equally applies to grotesque truth distortion.<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"><strong>Richard de Friend</strong><br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\">London NW5<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"></p> <h2>Not all gloom for churches</h2> <p>Peter Stanford\u2019s article regretting the demise of rural church buildings was informative, but struck a rather gloomy note (\u201c<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/culture/2023/apr/08/is-the-uk-prepared-to-let-its-rural-churches-rot-or-can-they-be-saved\" title=\"\">Is the melancholy fate off derelict rural churches sealed \u2013 or can we change it?</a>\u201d, Focus). Much can be done by communities to retain their churches if they campaign energetically and innovatively.</p> <p>The Romney Marsh Historic Churches Trust is a good example of how this can be done. For more than 40 years, the trust has provided funds for the maintenance of 12 medieval churches across the large marsh area of Kent, having secured support from art, entertainment, politics and journalism.</p> <p> It now has significant resources that are directed towards these beautiful and historic churches in their sparsely populated villages and hamlets. What has been done on Romney Marsh can be replicated elsewhere.<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"><strong>Shaun Leavey</strong><br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\">Sherborne, Dorset<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"></p> <h2>The SNP is where I want to be</h2> <p>Andrew Rawnsley suggests that Labour must seize the opportunity offered by troubles in the SNP (\u201c<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/apr/09/as-the-snp-loses-its-iron-grip-on-scotland-labour-must-seize-this-golden-opportunity\" title=\"\">As the SNP loses its iron grip on Scotland, Labour must seize this golden opportunity</a>\u201d, Comment). He may be right, but Labour has treated Scotland badly in the past and folk have long memories. Second, Labour is no longer a left-of-centre party. Its stance on most issues is in tune with the Tories and nowhere near where I want to be.</p> <p>The SNP has offered Scottish voters policies and strategies that are progressive and based on the wellbeing of the nation. It has had to mitigate the worst that Westminster has thrown at us.<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"><strong>Donald MacKay</strong><br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\">Crossford, Fife<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"></p> <h2>Eat like it\u2019s 1950</h2> <p>The news that low-carb diets can be a cure for obesity and diabetes would not have seemed so surprising in the 1950s (\u201c<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/society/2023/apr/09/british-doctor-pioneers-low-carb-diet-as-cure-for-obesity-and-type-2-diabetes\" title=\"\">Obesity, diabetes\u2026 has this GP found the drug-free treatment?</a>\u201d, News). The classic textbook <em>Human Nutrition and Dietetics</em> (1959) stated: \u201cThe intake of foods rich in carbohydrate should be drastically reduced since over-indulgence in such foods is the most common cause of obesity.\u201d This was common-sense advice at the time.</p> <p>But since then, we have been obsessed with reducing consumption of animal fats, replacing them with manufactured margarines, until the trans-fats that they contained were found to be too dangerous to eat. We have eaten sugar with abandon because throughout the second half of the 20th century we were assured that it was harmless. Had we stuck to the earlier views about diet, we could have avoided this epidemic of chronic ill-health.<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"><strong>Maggie Watson</strong><br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\">London E9<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"></p> <h2>Thanks for nothing, Nigel</h2> <p>So one of the reasons Nigel Lawson retired to France was the \u201cold-fashioned way of life\u201d there (\u201c<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/apr/08/labour-still-in-thrall-to-low-tax-regime-championed-by-my-friend-nigel-lawson\" title=\"\">Labour is still in thrall to the low tax regime championed by my friend Nigel Lawson</a>\u201d, Comment). I can still remember the old-fashioned way of life in this country: the river into which the water board did not dump tons of sewage, the time when the man from the gas board did visit, the city with the grand post office building. All gone, of course, thanks to Nigel\u2019s programme of privatisation and deregulation.<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"><strong>Ivor Morgan</strong><br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\">Lincoln<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"></p>", "newspaperPageNumber": "48", "wordcount": "1074", "commentable": "false", "firstPublicationDate": "2023-04-16T05:01:01Z", "isInappropriateForSponsorship": "false", "isPremoderated": "false", "lastModified": "2023-04-16T05:01:01Z", "newspaperEditionDate": "2023-04-16T00:00:00Z", "productionOffice": "UK", "publication": "The Observer", "shortUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/p/nmmqc", "shouldHideAdverts": "false", "showInRelatedContent": "true", "thumbnail": "https://media.guim.co.uk/e62d4393ffe8bb2e1ae4bee517e8ddb21a0dfca4/0_0_6048_3628/500.jpg", "legallySensitive": "false", "lang": "en", "isLive": "true", "bodyText": "Thank you so much for your article (\u201cAt last, a consensus is emerging on protecting women-only spaces\u201d, Comment). Sodha suggests that GRA reform is all that stands between us and a world where trans women can use whatever bathroom they please. That world is already here, and has been for decades.</p> <p>Finally, I think the assertion that \u201csomeone female with a GRC who gets pregnant would not be covered by Equality Act protections against pregnancy and maternity discrimination\u201d is bogus. Trans men have made clear that the compassionate solution is to reform the Equality Act to extend such protections, rather than removing existing ones.<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"><strong>Ell Johnson</strong><br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\">Norwich<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"></p> <h2>Distorting the truth</h2> <p>Will Hutton rightly warns of the corrosive impact that \u201ctruth denial\u201d will have on democracy (\u201c<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/apr/09/culture-truth-denial-wilting-us-democracy-britain-following-fast\" title=\"\">A culture of truth denial is wilting US democracy and Britain is fast following suit</a>\u201d, Comment). I hope he would agree that Labour\u2019s \u201cattack strategy\u201d demonstrates that this equally applies to grotesque truth distortion.<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"><strong>Richard de Friend</strong><br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\">London NW5<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"></p> <h2>Not all gloom for churches</h2> <p>Peter Stanford\u2019s article regretting the demise of rural church buildings was informative, but struck a rather gloomy note (\u201c<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/culture/2023/apr/08/is-the-uk-prepared-to-let-its-rural-churches-rot-or-can-they-be-saved\" title=\"\">Is the melancholy fate off derelict rural churches sealed \u2013 or can we change it?</a>\u201d, Focus). Much can be done by communities to retain their churches if they campaign energetically and innovatively.</p> <p>The Romney Marsh Historic Churches Trust is a good example of how this can be done. For more than 40 years, the trust has provided funds for the maintenance of 12 medieval churches across the large marsh area of Kent, having secured support from art, entertainment, politics and journalism.</p> <p> It now has significant resources that are directed towards these beautiful and historic churches in their sparsely populated villages and hamlets. What has been done on Romney Marsh can be replicated elsewhere.<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"><strong>Shaun Leavey</strong><br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\">Sherborne, Dorset<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"></p> <h2>The SNP is where I want to be</h2> <p>Andrew Rawnsley suggests that Labour must seize the opportunity offered by troubles in the SNP (\u201c<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/apr/09/as-the-snp-loses-its-iron-grip-on-scotland-labour-must-seize-this-golden-opportunity\" title=\"\">As the SNP loses its iron grip on Scotland, Labour must seize this golden opportunity</a>\u201d, Comment). He may be right, but Labour has treated Scotland badly in the past and folk have long memories. Second, Labour is no longer a left-of-centre party. Its stance on most issues is in tune with the Tories and nowhere near where I want to be.</p> <p>The SNP has offered Scottish voters policies and strategies that are progressive and based on the wellbeing of the nation. It has had to mitigate the worst that Westminster has thrown at us.<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"><strong>Donald MacKay</strong><br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\">Crossford, Fife<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"></p> <h2>Eat like it\u2019s 1950</h2> <p>The news that low-carb diets can be a cure for obesity and diabetes would not have seemed so surprising in the 1950s (\u201c<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/society/2023/apr/09/british-doctor-pioneers-low-carb-diet-as-cure-for-obesity-and-type-2-diabetes\" title=\"\">Obesity, diabetes\u2026 has this GP found the drug-free treatment?</a>\u201d, News). The classic textbook <em>Human Nutrition and Dietetics</em> (1959) stated: \u201cThe intake of foods rich in carbohydrate should be drastically reduced since over-indulgence in such foods is the most common cause of obesity.\u201d This was common-sense advice at the time.</p> <p>But since then, we have been obsessed with reducing consumption of animal fats, replacing them with manufactured margarines, until the trans-fats that they contained were found to be too dangerous to eat. We have eaten sugar with abandon because throughout the second half of the 20th century we were assured that it was harmless. Had we stuck to the earlier views about diet, we could have avoided this epidemic of chronic ill-health.<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"><strong>Maggie Watson</strong><br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\">London E9<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"></p> <h2>Thanks for nothing, Nigel</h2> <p>So one of the reasons Nigel Lawson retired to France was the \u201cold-fashioned way of life\u201d there (\u201c<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/apr/08/labour-still-in-thrall-to-low-tax-regime-championed-by-my-friend-nigel-lawson\" title=\"\">Labour is still in thrall to the low tax regime championed by my friend Nigel Lawson</a>\u201d, Comment). I can still remember the old-fashioned way of life in this country: the river into which the water board did not dump tons of sewage, the time when the man from the gas board did visit, the city with the grand post office building. All gone, of course, thanks to Nigel\u2019s programme of privatisation and deregulation.<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"><strong>Ivor Morgan</strong><br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\">Lincoln<br incopy-break-type=\"webbreak\"></p>", "bodyTextSummary": "Thank you so much for your article (\u201cAt last, a consensus is emerging on protecting women-only spaces\u201d, Comment). As a left-leaning feminist, doctor and trainee psychotherapist, I wept with joy and relief to see an article that finally seemed to voice that there is a difference between biological sex and gender identity, and sometimes this difference matters. It matters most to the most vulnerable women who are also more likely to be conditioned not to speak up, including female inmates in prison, where a huge percentage have been on the receiving end of male violence and sexual assaults. I have been so upset in response to some of the confluence between sex and gender identity in language and statistics. I feel this serves neither biological women or men, nor trans women or trans men. I desperately want protection for biological women, trans women and trans men from male violence, abuse and discrimination. We shouldn\u2019t have to wait until women are getting their necks broken on sports pitches or more women are raped by males in female-only prisons before we as a society wake up, use our common sense and listen to science. Sexual dimorphism is a biological fact of human existence. This doesn\u2019t inhibit anyone from identifying or expressing themselves however they like and diversity should be celebrated, but sometimes sex matters. Sex-protected rights were fought for and granted for important reasons and we can\u2019t see them erased. Kate Newman Brighton As a trans person, I won\u2019t pretend that Sonia Sodha\u2019s article didn\u2019t deeply wound me. To claim an \u201cemerging consensus\u201d around Gender Recognition Act reform is to construct a public and to draw a line \u2013 right-thinking people on one side, gender ideologues on the other. Only a small number of trans people bother to apply for a gender recognition certificate. The process is frustrating, dehumanising, bureaucratic, but, even if it were not, a GRC has no bearing on your day-to-day life, which toilets you use, how others perceive you. Trans women have been using women\u2019s spaces, especially public toilets, for years, with or without GRCs, but usually without. A GRC enables death and marriage certificates to be filed correctly. That\u2019s all. My status as a trans woman is legally protected under the Equality Act, without a GRC. The government has issued guidance in the last few months advising women\u2019s refuges and similar charities that they may exclude trans women if they believe there are reasonable grounds to do so for others\u2019 benefit. Given the paucity of options for trans women fleeing domestic violence, this is a bad state of affairs. Sodha suggests that GRA reform is all that stands between us and a world where trans women can use whatever bathroom they please. That world is already here, and has been for decades. Finally, I think the assertion that \u201csomeone female with a GRC who gets pregnant would not be covered by Equality Act protections against pregnancy and maternity discrimination\u201d is bogus. Trans men have made clear that the compassionate solution is to reform the Equality Act to extend such protections, rather than removing existing ones. What has been done on Romney Marsh can be replicated elsewhere. Donald MacKay Crossford, Fife Eat like it\u2019s 1950 The news that low-carb diets can be a cure for obesity and diabetes would not have seemed so surprising in the 1950s (\u201cObesity, diabetes\u2026 has this GP found the drug-free treatment?\u201d, News). A look back at six months of Musk\u2019s Twitter reign", "webUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2023/apr/16/elon-musk-twitter-takeover-six-months", "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/technology/2023/apr/16/elon-musk-twitter-takeover-six-months", "fields": {"headline": "Is the bird really freed? A look back at six months of Musk\u2019s Twitter reign", "standfirst": "<p>As we approach half a year of Musk\u2019s acquisition of the social media firm, this is how key areas of the site\u2019s business have performed</p>", "trailText": "As we approach half a year of Musk\u2019s acquisition of the social media firm, this is how key areas of the site\u2019s business have performed", "byline": "Dan Milmo Global technology editor", "main": "<figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"cac584cb9c538eff4f85b86777b803e2f0ac8b37\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/cac584cb9c538eff4f85b86777b803e2f0ac8b37/0_88_4000_2401/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Elon Musk's photo seen through a Twitter logo\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Elon Musk\u2019s decision making style at the media company: erratic, and sometimes, inscrutable in motivation.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Dado Ruvi\u0107/Reuters</span> </figcaption> </figure>", "body": "<p>On 28 October 2022 Elon Musk tweeted \u201c<a href=\"https://twitter.com/elonmusk/status/1585841080431321088?lang=en\">the bird is freed</a>\u201d as he marked his $44bn acquisition of Twitter.</p> <p>Nearly six months later, it became a dog. The distinctive avian logo the Tesla CEO had referenced in October had been replaced by a picture of a Shiba Inu canine \u2013 the face of the Dogecoin cryptocurrency.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2023/apr/11/former-twitter-executives-sue-company-parag-agrawal\">Twitter\u2019s ex-CEO sues company after legal turmoil</a> </p> </aside>  <p>There was speculation over what was behind the change. Was it linked to a recent attempt to dismiss a $258bn lawsuit filed against Musk by Dogecoin investors over an alleged pyramid scheme? Was it a <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2023/apr/04/twitter-changes-logo-to-dogecoin-cryptocurrency-image-in-apparent-late-april-fools-day-gag\">delayed April Fool\u2019s gag</a>?</p> <p>Whatever the reason, the dog has now gone. But the episode was exemplary of Musk\u2019s decision-making style at the social media company: erratic, and sometimes, inscrutable in motivation.</p> <p>As we approach six months of Musk\u2019s acquisition of Twitter, it remains far from business as usual at the company. In the past week alone, there has been <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/media/2023/apr/11/row-between-twitter-and-substack-ends-with-uneasy-truce\">a spat</a> with Substack, further problems with the site\u2019s infrastructure and NPR, the US radio network, has <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/media/2023/apr/12/npr-leaves-twitter-elon-musk-state-media\">quit the platform</a>. Musk himself admitted in an <a href=\"https://www.bbc.com/news/business-65248196\">interview</a> with the BBC that running Twitter had been \u201cpainful\u201d and that buying it had come with \u201cemotional strain\u201d.</p> <p>Here\u2019s a look at how key areas of Twitter\u2019s business have performed.</p> <h2>Debt and company value</h2> <p>The end of the month does not just mark six months since Musk\u2019s purchase, it is also the due date for the second quarterly instalment on paying down Twitter\u2019s $13bn debt burden (a payment of about $300m).</p> <p>When the first payment approached in January, it was against a backdrop of doom-laden warnings from Musk, who had cautioned shortly after the takeover that \u201cbankruptcy isn\u2019t out of the question\u201d.</p> <p>True, Musk had paid $44bn for a business that had been largely loss-making since its launch in 2006. But his own decisions at the platform had led to an advertiser boycott that had caused significant damage to the platform\u2019s main source of revenue (more than $5bn in 2021).</p> <p>Last month it emerged that Twitter is now <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2023/mar/26/elon-musk-twitter-value-leaked-memo-less-than-half-paid\">worth less than half</a> of what <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/elon-musk\">Musk</a> paid for it, having lost more than $20bn (\u00a316.4bn) in value, according to calculations based on a leaked memo.</p> <h2>Financial performance</h2> <p>Once at the helm, Musk slashed costs and, last month, the CEO said he thought Twitter had a chance of <a href=\"https://www.theinformation.com/briefings/musk-twitter-has-a-shot-at-becoming-cash-flow-positive-in-second-quarter?rc=fnnefa\">turning cashflow positive</a> \u2013 broadly, spending less cash to run the business than it takes in \u2013 in the current quarter. He repeated the claim in the BBC interview. In March he told a Morgan Stanley conference in San Francisco that Twitter\u2019s revenue was due to drop to less than $3bn this year but expenditure had dropped from $4.5bn to $1.5bn.</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--inline\"> <blockquote> <p>Musk, as is his wont, has been a chaos agent, unfocused and unpredictable \u2026</p> <footer> <cite>Aswath Damodaran</cite> </footer> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>Aswath Damodaran, a professor of finance at the Stern School of Business at New York University, says Musk\u2019s high-risk approach could pay off for a platform that was struggling for financial stability even before the chaotic takeover.</p> <p>\u201cTwitter has always been a basket case as a business, unable to monetize its users into advertising revenues and the status quo was getting them nowhere,\u201d he says. \u201cMusk, as is his wont, has been a chaos agent, unfocused and unpredictable, but I would think that Twitter stands a chance of making it out of the forest, precisely because of this chaos.\u201d</p> <p>Indeed, Musk recently folded Twitter into an entity called X Corp. Musk has referred to Twitter as being an \u201caccelerant\u201d for creating X, his \u201ceverything app\u201d akin to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2012/dec/07/wechat-chinese-social-media-app\" title=\"\">WeChat in China</a>.</p> <h2>User base</h2> <p>A self-declared\u201cfree speech absolutist\u201d, Musk early on chose to reinstate accounts that had been banned under the platform\u2019s previous owners. Those included the accounts for Donald Trump, misogynist influencer Andrew Tate and the personal account of the far-right US congresswoman Marjorie Taylor Greene.</p> <p>According to Musk, users have not fled the platform in response. Musk said in November that Twitter\u2019s user numbers had crossed 250 million, up from 238 million before the takeover.</p> <p>The controversy around account reinstatement, the threat of losing blue-check status and Musk\u2019s dislike of mainstream media, including banning some prominent reporters, also do not seem to have deterred the journalists and influential pundits who use the platform regularly (see the Gary Lineker tweet that <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/media/2023/mar/11/assault-on-free-speech-gary-linekers-defenders-and-detractors\">drove a whole news cycle</a> recently).</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"f065ba430b6111db11b1b815606fe5bd5c7380de\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/f065ba430b6111db11b1b815606fe5bd5c7380de/167_0_2833_1700/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Elon Musk carrying a sink in to the Twitter HQ on 26 October 2022.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Elon Musk carrying a sink in to the Twitter HQ on 26 October 2022.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Twitter account of Elon Musk/AFP/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>\u201cDespite all the talk that journalists would be leaving Twitter, in practice very few have done so because it remains an important part of workflows, with networks that have been built up and carefully curated over the years \u2013 as well as being a compelling and amusing distraction,\u201d says Nic Newman, a senior research associate at the Reuters Institute for the Study Journalism.</p> <p>\u201cOur data suggests that there has been no substantial drop-off in the number of people that use it weekly \u2013 though that may well come.\u201d</p> <h2>Staff</h2> <p>In an effort to dramatically cut costs, Musk axed half of the company\u2019s 7,500-strong workforce <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2022/nov/04/twitter-layoffs-elon-musk-revenue-drop\">within days of sealing the takeover</a> and reduced it further through a series of cuts including a bizarre redundancy program where staff were asked to commit to being \u201chardcore\u201d or leave. Such was the chaotic reaction to that ultimatum, Twitter was forced to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2022/nov/17/elon-musk-twitter-closes-offices-loyalty-oath-resignations\">shut offices temporarily</a>.</p> <p>According to one former employee who was made redundant following the takeover, the platform has gone backwards. \u201cFor many of us former tweeps, seeing what Musk is doing to Twitter is heartbreaking,\u201d said the former staff member, speaking on condition of anonymity. \u201cThe platform has regressed in terms of toxicity, safety and nuance. The hard work of dedicated teams and colleagues over the years has been erased, including efforts to combat misinformation and create trustworthy safety policies and curation standards.\u201d</p> <h2>Advertisers</h2> <p>Concerns over Musk\u2019s erratic decision making, approach to content moderation and a botched relaunch of Twitter\u2019s Blue subscription service underlined Musk\u2019s revenue projections last month. At one point in January, the company\u2019s daily revenue was down 40% year-over-year, according to the tech newsletter <a href=\"https://twitter.com/ZoeSchiffer/status/1615519936805482496\">the Platformer</a> and some boycotts remain in place. Pharmaceutical firms Eli Lilly and Pfizer, retailer Macy\u2019s and Cadbury owner Mond\u0113lez spent no money on Twitter in the first two months of the year, according to the advertising data firm MediaRadar. However, MediaRadar said there were 3,700 companies advertising on Twitter in February \u2013 8% more than the same period last year (although some, like Volkswagen, General Motors and Chipotle, have reduced their spending significantly).</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--inline\"> <blockquote> <p>The platform has regressed in terms of toxicity, safety and nuance. The hard work of dedicated teams and colleagues over the years has been erased \u2026</p> <footer> <cite>Former Twitter employee</cite> </footer> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>Nonetheless, there are signs of life.</p> <p>\u201cJust this past couple of weeks we\u2019ve seen the first signs of recovery, as a few advertisers are now talking about Twitter ads again,\u201d says Farhad Divecha, the managing director of the UK digital marketing agency AccuraCast. \u201cThey\u2019re now coming to us asking about it, rather than us asking them if they\u2019re ready to reconsider Twitter \u2013 and them declining.</p> <h2>Twitter Blue relaunch</h2> <p>Determined to reduce Twitter\u2019s reliance on advertising, Musk has invested big in the relaunch of Twitter\u2019s subscription services. The first relaunch of Twitter Blue, in November, was paused after it led to a slew of fake \u201cverified\u201d accounts.</p> <p>The company tried again in December, offering subscribers perks such as a blue tick \u2013 giving your account more prominence on the For You feed \u2013 and fewer adverts for $8 a month on the web or $11 on iOS or Android.</p> <p>According to Travis Brown, a software developer who has been tracking Twitter\u2019s subscription service, the new-look Blue has about 550-585,000 subscribers which equates to $4m+ a month in revenue. Twitter will need many more sign-ups to offset the advertising loss.</p> <p>Meanwhile, the 400,000 legacy blue checks face a loss of status. Musk told the BBC that these checks will be phased out by next week, although a deadline has been missed before.</p> <h2>Site performance and regulators</h2> <p>Twitter has been hit by a series of outages since it was bought. In the latest breach, users reported that Twitter\u2019s privacy-friendly Circles feature was leaking <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/media/2023/apr/11/row-between-twitter-and-substack-ends-with-uneasy-truce\">private user data</a>.</p> <p>The US Federal Trade Commission is investigating the company amid concerns that Twitter\u2019s ability to protect users, and comply with a <a href=\"https://www.ftc.gov/business-guidance/blog/2022/05/twitter-pay-150-million-penalty-allegedly-breaking-its-privacy-promises-again\">2022 deal</a> with the FTC to maintain high data security standards, might have been affected by redundancies and cost cuts.</p> <p>\u201cThe FTC\u2019s investigation is quite serious,\u201d says David Vladeck, a professor at Georgetown University Law Center. \u201cThere is deep concern within the FTC as to whether Twitter is, in fact, making a concerted effort to bring it into compliance with the May 2022 consent decree.\u201d</p> <p>Vladeck says that unless Twitter \u201cups its game\u201d another enforcement action, including \u201csubstantial civil penalties\u201d, looms.</p> <p>An emailed request for comment sent to Twitter\u2019s press office was met, as is now standard practice at the company, with a poop emoji.</p>", "wordcount": "1419", "firstPublicationDate": "2023-04-16T05:00:03Z", "isInappropriateForSponsorship": "false", "isPremoderated": "false", "lastModified": "2023-04-16T11:07:42Z", "productionOffice": "US", "publication": "theguardian.com", "shortUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/p/nydfq", "shouldHideAdverts": "false", "showInRelatedContent": "true", "thumbnail": "https://media.guim.co.uk/cac584cb9c538eff4f85b86777b803e2f0ac8b37/0_88_4000_2401/500.jpg", "legallySensitive": "false", "lang": "en", "isLive": "true", "bodyText": "On 28 October 2022 Elon Musk tweeted \u201cthe bird is freed\u201d as he marked his $44bn acquisition of Twitter. Nearly six months later, it became a dog. The distinctive avian logo the Tesla CEO had referenced in October had been replaced by a picture of a Shiba Inu canine \u2013 the face of the Dogecoin cryptocurrency. There was speculation over what was behind the change. Was it linked to a recent attempt to dismiss a $258bn lawsuit filed against Musk by Dogecoin investors over an alleged pyramid scheme? Was it a delayed April Fool\u2019s gag? Whatever the reason, the dog has now gone. But the episode was exemplary of Musk\u2019s decision-making style at the social media company: erratic, and sometimes, inscrutable in motivation. As we approach six months of Musk\u2019s acquisition of Twitter, it remains far from business as usual at the company. In the past week alone, there has been a spat with Substack, further problems with the site\u2019s infrastructure and NPR, the US radio network, has quit the platform. Musk himself admitted in an interview with the BBC that running Twitter had been \u201cpainful\u201d and that buying it had come with \u201cemotional strain\u201d. Here\u2019s a look at how key areas of Twitter\u2019s business have performed. Debt and company value The end of the month does not just mark six months since Musk\u2019s purchase, it is also the due date for the second quarterly instalment on paying down Twitter\u2019s $13bn debt burden (a payment of about $300m). When the first payment approached in January, it was against a backdrop of doom-laden warnings from Musk, who had cautioned shortly after the takeover that \u201cbankruptcy isn\u2019t out of the question\u201d. True, Musk had paid $44bn for a business that had been largely loss-making since its launch in 2006. But his own decisions at the platform had led to an advertiser boycott that had caused significant damage to the platform\u2019s main source of revenue (more than $5bn in 2021). Last month it emerged that Twitter is now worth less than half of what Musk paid for it, having lost more than $20bn (\u00a316.4bn) in value, according to calculations based on a leaked memo. Financial performance Once at the helm, Musk slashed costs and, last month, the CEO said he thought Twitter had a chance of turning cashflow positive \u2013 broadly, spending less cash to run the business than it takes in \u2013 in the current quarter. He repeated the claim in the BBC interview. In March he told a Morgan Stanley conference in San Francisco that Twitter\u2019s revenue was due to drop to less than $3bn this year but expenditure had dropped from $4.5bn to $1.5bn. Aswath Damodaran, a professor of finance at the Stern School of Business at New York University, says Musk\u2019s high-risk approach could pay off for a platform that was struggling for financial stability even before the chaotic takeover. \u201cTwitter has always been a basket case as a business, unable to monetize its users into advertising revenues and the status quo was getting them nowhere,\u201d he says. \u201cMusk, as is his wont, has been a chaos agent, unfocused and unpredictable, but I would think that Twitter stands a chance of making it out of the forest, precisely because of this chaos.\u201d Indeed, Musk recently folded Twitter into an entity called X Corp. Musk has referred to Twitter as being an \u201caccelerant\u201d for creating X, his \u201ceverything app\u201d akin to WeChat in China. User base A self-declared\u201cfree speech absolutist\u201d, Musk early on chose to reinstate accounts that had been banned under the platform\u2019s previous owners. Those included the accounts for Donald Trump, misogynist influencer Andrew Tate and the personal account of the far-right US congresswoman Marjorie Taylor Greene. According to Musk, users have not fled the platform in response. Musk said in November that Twitter\u2019s user numbers had crossed 250 million, up from 238 million before the takeover. The controversy around account reinstatement, the threat of losing blue-check status and Musk\u2019s dislike of mainstream media, including banning some prominent reporters, also do not seem to have deterred the journalists and influential pundits who use the platform regularly (see the Gary Lineker tweet that drove a whole news cycle recently). \u201cDespite all the talk that journalists would be leaving Twitter, in practice very few have done so because it remains an important part of workflows, with networks that have been built up and carefully curated over the years \u2013 as well as being a compelling and amusing distraction,\u201d says Nic Newman, a senior research associate at the Reuters Institute for the Study Journalism. \u201cOur data suggests that there has been no substantial drop-off in the number of people that use it weekly \u2013 though that may well come.\u201d Staff In an effort to dramatically cut costs, Musk axed half of the company\u2019s 7,500-strong workforce within days of sealing the takeover and reduced it further through a series of cuts including a bizarre redundancy program where staff were asked to commit to being \u201chardcore\u201d or leave. Such was the chaotic reaction to that ultimatum, Twitter was forced to shut offices temporarily. According to one former employee who was made redundant following the takeover, the platform has gone backwards. \u201cFor many of us former tweeps, seeing what Musk is doing to Twitter is heartbreaking,\u201d said the former staff member, speaking on condition of anonymity. \u201cThe platform has regressed in terms of toxicity, safety and nuance. The hard work of dedicated teams and colleagues over the years has been erased, including efforts to combat misinformation and create trustworthy safety policies and curation standards.\u201d Advertisers Concerns over Musk\u2019s erratic decision making, approach to content moderation and a botched relaunch of Twitter\u2019s Blue subscription service underlined Musk\u2019s revenue projections last month. At one point in January, the company\u2019s daily revenue was down 40% year-over-year, according to the tech newsletter the Platformer and some boycotts remain in place. Pharmaceutical firms Eli Lilly and Pfizer, retailer Macy\u2019s and Cadbury owner Mond\u0113lez spent no money on Twitter in the first two months of the year, according to the advertising data firm MediaRadar. However, MediaRadar said there were 3,700 companies advertising on Twitter in February \u2013 8% more than the same period last year (although some, like Volkswagen, General Motors and Chipotle, have reduced their spending significantly). Nonetheless, there are signs of life. \u201cJust this past couple of weeks we\u2019ve seen the first signs of recovery, as a few advertisers are now talking about Twitter ads again,\u201d says Farhad Divecha, the managing director of the UK digital marketing agency AccuraCast. \u201cThey\u2019re now coming to us asking about it, rather than us asking them if they\u2019re ready to reconsider Twitter \u2013 and them declining. Twitter Blue relaunch Determined to reduce Twitter\u2019s reliance on advertising, Musk has invested big in the relaunch of Twitter\u2019s subscription services. The first relaunch of Twitter Blue, in November, was paused after it led to a slew of fake \u201cverified\u201d accounts. The company tried again in December, offering subscribers perks such as a blue tick \u2013 giving your account more prominence on the For You feed \u2013 and fewer adverts for $8 a month on the web or $11 on iOS or Android. According to Travis Brown, a software developer who has been tracking Twitter\u2019s subscription service, the new-look Blue has about 550-585,000 subscribers which equates to $4m+ a month in revenue. Twitter will need many more sign-ups to offset the advertising loss. Meanwhile, the 400,000 legacy blue checks face a loss of status. Musk told the BBC that these checks will be phased out by next week, although a deadline has been missed before. Site performance and regulators Twitter has been hit by a series of outages since it was bought. In the latest breach, users reported that Twitter\u2019s privacy-friendly Circles feature was leaking private user data. The US Federal Trade Commission is investigating the company amid concerns that Twitter\u2019s ability to protect users, and comply with a 2022 deal with the FTC to maintain high data security standards, might have been affected by redundancies and cost cuts. \u201cThe FTC\u2019s investigation is quite serious,\u201d says David Vladeck, a professor at Georgetown University Law Center. \u201cThere is deep concern within the FTC as to whether Twitter is, in fact, making a concerted effort to bring it into compliance with the May 2022 consent decree.\u201d Vladeck says that unless Twitter \u201cups its game\u201d another enforcement action, including \u201csubstantial civil penalties\u201d, looms. height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Elon Musk carrying a sink in to the Twitter HQ on 26 October 2022.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Twitter account of Elon Musk/AFP/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>\u201cDespite all the talk that journalists would be leaving Twitter, in practice very few have done so because it remains an important part of workflows, with networks that have been built up and carefully curated over the years \u2013 as well as being a compelling and amusing distraction,\u201d says Nic Newman, a senior research associate at the Reuters Institute for the Study Journalism.</p> <p>\u201cOur data suggests that there has been no substantial drop-off in the number of people that use it weekly \u2013 though that may well come.\u201d</p> <h2>Staff</h2> <p>In an effort to dramatically cut costs, Musk axed half of the company\u2019s 7,500-strong workforce <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2022/nov/04/twitter-layoffs-elon-musk-revenue-drop\">within days of sealing the takeover</a> and reduced it further through a series of cuts including a bizarre redundancy program where staff were asked to commit to being \u201chardcore\u201d or leave. Such was the chaotic reaction to that ultimatum, Twitter was forced to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2022/nov/17/elon-musk-twitter-closes-offices-loyalty-oath-resignations\">shut offices temporarily</a>.</p> <p>According to one former employee who was made redundant following the takeover, the platform has gone backwards. \u201cFor many of us former tweeps, seeing what Musk is doing to Twitter is heartbreaking,\u201d said the former staff member, speaking on condition of anonymity. \u201cThe platform has regressed in terms of toxicity, safety and nuance. The hard work of dedicated teams and colleagues over the years has been erased, including efforts to combat misinformation and create trustworthy safety policies and curation standards.\u201d</p> <h2>Advertisers</h2> <p>Concerns over Musk\u2019s erratic decision making, approach to content moderation and a botched relaunch of Twitter\u2019s Blue subscription service underlined Musk\u2019s revenue projections last month. At one point in January, the company\u2019s daily revenue was down 40% year-over-year, according to the tech newsletter <a href=\"https://twitter.com/ZoeSchiffer/status/1615519936805482496\">the Platformer</a> and some boycotts remain in place. Pharmaceutical firms Eli Lilly and Pfizer, retailer Macy\u2019s and Cadbury owner Mond\u0113lez spent no money on Twitter in the first two months of the year, according to the advertising data firm MediaRadar. However, MediaRadar said there were 3,700 companies advertising on Twitter in February \u2013 8% more than the same period last year (although some, like Volkswagen, General Motors and Chipotle, have reduced their spending significantly).</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--inline\"> <blockquote> <p>The platform has regressed in terms of toxicity, safety and nuance. The hard work of dedicated teams and colleagues over the years has been erased \u2026</p> <footer> <cite>Former Twitter employee</cite> </footer> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>Nonetheless, there are signs of life.</p> <p>\u201cJust this past couple of weeks we\u2019ve seen the first signs of recovery, as a few advertisers are now talking about Twitter ads again,\u201d says Farhad Divecha, the managing director of the UK digital marketing agency AccuraCast. \u201cThey\u2019re now coming to us asking about it, rather than us asking them if they\u2019re ready to reconsider Twitter \u2013 and them declining.</p> <h2>Twitter Blue relaunch</h2> <p>Determined to reduce Twitter\u2019s reliance on advertising, Musk has invested big in the relaunch of Twitter\u2019s subscription services. The first relaunch of Twitter Blue, in November, was paused after it led to a slew of fake \u201cverified\u201d accounts.</p> <p>The company tried again in December, offering subscribers perks such as a blue tick \u2013 giving your account more prominence on the For You feed \u2013 and fewer adverts for $8 a month on the web or $11 on iOS or Android.</p> <p>According to Travis Brown, a software developer who has been tracking Twitter\u2019s subscription service, the new-look Blue has about 550-585,000 subscribers which equates to $4m+ a month in revenue. Twitter will need many more sign-ups to offset the advertising loss.</p> <p>Meanwhile, the 400,000 legacy blue checks face a loss of status. Musk told the BBC that these checks will be phased out by next week, although a deadline has been missed before.</p> <h2>Site performance and regulators</h2> <p>Twitter has been hit by a series of outages since it was bought. In the latest breach, users reported that Twitter\u2019s privacy-friendly Circles feature was leaking <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/media/2023/apr/11/row-between-twitter-and-substack-ends-with-uneasy-truce\">private user data</a>.</p> <p>The US Federal Trade Commission is investigating the company amid concerns that Twitter\u2019s ability to protect users, and comply with a <a href=\"https://www.ftc.gov/business-guidance/blog/2022/05/twitter-pay-150-million-penalty-allegedly-breaking-its-privacy-promises-again\">2022 deal</a> with the FTC to maintain high data security standards, might have been affected by redundancies and cost cuts.</p> <p>\u201cThe FTC\u2019s investigation is quite serious,\u201d says David Vladeck, a professor at Georgetown University Law Center. \u201cThere is deep concern within the FTC as to whether Twitter is, in fact, making a concerted effort to bring it into compliance with the May 2022 consent decree.\u201d</p> <p>Vladeck says that unless Twitter \u201cups its game\u201d another enforcement action, including \u201csubstantial civil penalties\u201d, looms.</p> <p>An emailed request for comment sent to Twitter\u2019s press office was met, as is now standard practice at the company, with a poop emoji.</p>", "bodyTextSummary": "On 28 October 2022 Elon Musk tweeted \u201cthe bird is freed\u201d as he marked his $44bn acquisition of Twitter. Nearly six months later, it became a dog. The distinctive avian logo the Tesla CEO had referenced in October had been replaced by a picture of a Shiba Inu canine \u2013 the face of the Dogecoin cryptocurrency.\nThere was speculation over what was behind the change. Was it linked to a recent attempt to dismiss a $258bn lawsuit filed against Musk by Dogecoin investors over an alleged pyramid scheme? Was it a delayed April Fool\u2019s gag? Whatever the reason, the dog has now gone. But the episode was exemplary of Musk\u2019s decision-making style at the social media company: erratic, and sometimes, inscrutable in motivation. As we approach six months of Musk\u2019s acquisition of Twitter, it remains far from business as usual at the company. In the past week alone, there has been a spat with Substack, further problems with the site\u2019s infrastructure and NPR, the US radio network, has quit the platform. Musk himself admitted in an interview with the BBC that running Twitter had been \u201cpainful\u201d and that buying it had come with \u201cemotional strain\u201d. Here\u2019s a look at how key areas of Twitter\u2019s business have performed. Debt and company value The end of the month does not just mark six months since Musk\u2019s purchase, it is also the due date for the second quarterly instalment on paying down Twitter\u2019s $13bn debt burden (a payment of about $300m). When the first payment approached in January, it was against a backdrop of doom-laden warnings from Musk, who had cautioned shortly after the takeover that \u201cbankruptcy isn\u2019t out of the question\u201d. True, Musk had paid $44bn for a business that had been largely loss-making since its launch in 2006. But his own decisions at the platform had led to an advertiser boycott that had caused significant damage to the platform\u2019s main source of revenue (more than $5bn in 2021). Last month it emerged that Twitter is now worth less than half of what Musk paid for it, having lost more than $20bn (\u00a316.4bn) in value, according to calculations based on a leaked memo. Financial performance Once at the helm, Musk slashed costs and, last month, the CEO said he thought Twitter had a chance of turning cashflow positive \u2013 broadly, spending less cash to run the business than it takes in \u2013 in the current quarter. He repeated the claim in the BBC interview. In March he told a Morgan Stanley conference in San Francisco that Twitter\u2019s revenue was due to drop to less than $3bn this year but expenditure had dropped from $4.5bn to $1.5bn.\nAswath Damodaran, a professor of finance at the Stern School of Business at New York University, says Musk\u2019s high-risk approach could pay off for a platform that was struggling for financial stability even before the chaotic takeover. \u201cTwitter has always been a basket case as a business, unable to monetize its users into advertising revenues and the status quo was getting them nowhere,\u201d he says. \u201cMusk, as is his wont, has been a chaos agent, unfocused and unpredictable, but I would think that Twitter stands a chance of making it out of the forest, precisely because of this chaos.\u201d Indeed, Musk recently folded Twitter into an entity called X Corp. Musk has referred to Twitter as being an \u201caccelerant\u201d for creating X, his \u201ceverything app\u201d akin to WeChat in China. User base A self-declared\u201cfree speech absolutist\u201d, Musk early on chose to reinstate accounts that had been banned under the platform\u2019s previous owners. Those included the accounts for Donald Trump, misogynist influencer Andrew Tate and the personal account of the far-right US congresswoman Marjorie Taylor Greene. According to Musk, users have not fled the platform in response. Musk said in November that Twitter\u2019s user numbers had crossed 250 million, up from 238 million before the takeover. The controversy around account reinstatement, the threat of losing blue-check status and Musk\u2019s dislike of mainstream media, including banning some prominent reporters, also do not seem to have deterred the journalists and influential pundits who use the platform regularly (see the Gary Lineker tweet that drove a whole news cycle recently).\n\u201cDespite all the talk that journalists would be leaving Twitter, in practice very few have done so because it remains an important part of workflows, with networks that have been built up and carefully curated over the years \u2013 as well as being a compelling and amusing distraction,\u201d says Nic Newman, a senior research associate at the Reuters Institute for the Study Journalism. \u201cOur data suggests that there has been no substantial drop-off in the number of people that use it weekly \u2013 though that may well come.\u201d Staff In an effort to dramatically cut costs, Musk axed half of the company\u2019s 7,500-strong workforce within days of sealing the takeover and reduced it further through a series of cuts including a bizarre redundancy program where staff were asked to commit to being \u201chardcore\u201d or leave. Such was the chaotic reaction to that ultimatum, Twitter was forced to shut offices temporarily. According to one former employee who was made redundant following the takeover, the platform has gone backwards. \u201cFor many of us former tweeps, seeing what Musk is doing to Twitter is heartbreaking,\u201d said the former staff member, speaking on condition of anonymity. \u201cThe platform has regressed in terms of toxicity, safety and nuance. The hard work of dedicated teams and colleagues over the years has been erased, including efforts to combat misinformation and create trustworthy safety policies and curation standards.\u201d Advertisers Concerns over Musk\u2019s erratic decision making, approach to content moderation and a botched relaunch of Twitter\u2019s Blue subscription service underlined Musk\u2019s revenue projections last month. At one point in January, the company\u2019s daily revenue was down 40% year-over-year, according to the tech newsletter the Platformer and some boycotts remain in place. Pharmaceutical firms Eli Lilly and Pfizer, retailer Macy\u2019s and Cadbury owner Mond\u0113lez spent no money on Twitter in the first two months of the year, according to the advertising data firm MediaRadar. However, MediaRadar said there were 3,700 companies advertising on Twitter in February \u2013 8% more than the same period last year (although some, like Volkswagen, General Motors and Chipotle, have reduced their spending significantly).\nNonetheless, there are signs of life. \u201cJust this past couple of weeks we\u2019ve seen the first signs of recovery, as a few advertisers are now talking about Twitter ads again,\u201d says Farhad Divecha, the managing director of the UK digital marketing agency AccuraCast. \u201cThey\u2019re now coming to us asking about it, rather than us asking them if they\u2019re ready to reconsider Twitter \u2013 and them declining. Twitter Blue relaunch Determined to reduce Twitter\u2019s reliance on advertising, Musk has invested big in the relaunch of Twitter\u2019s subscription services. The first relaunch of Twitter Blue, in November, was paused after it led to a slew of fake \u201cverified\u201d accounts. The company tried again in December, offering subscribers perks such as a blue tick \u2013 giving your account more prominence on the For You feed \u2013 and fewer adverts for $8 a month on the web or $11 on iOS or Android. According to Travis Brown, a software developer who has been tracking Twitter\u2019s subscription service, the new-look Blue has about 550-585,000 subscribers which equates to $4m+ a month in revenue. Twitter will need many more sign-ups to offset the advertising loss. Meanwhile, the 400,000 legacy blue checks face a loss of status. Musk told the BBC that these checks will be phased out by next week, although a deadline has been missed before. Site performance and regulators Twitter has been hit by a series of outages since it was bought. In the latest breach, users reported that Twitter\u2019s privacy-friendly Circles feature was leaking private user data. The US Federal Trade Commission is investigating the company amid concerns that Twitter\u2019s ability to protect users, and comply with a 2022 deal with the FTC to maintain high data security standards, might have been affected by redundancies and cost cuts. \u201cThe FTC\u2019s investigation is quite serious,\u201d says David Vladeck, a professor at Georgetown University Law Center. \u201cThere is deep concern within the FTC as to whether Twitter is, in fact, making a concerted effort to bring it into compliance with the May 2022 consent decree.\u201d Vladeck says that unless Twitter \u201cups its game\u201d another enforcement action, including \u201csubstantial civil penalties\u201d, looms. "Life and style", "webPublicationDate": "2023-04-16T05:00:03Z", "webTitle": "Is it fair to put my abusive, elderly father into a home? | Ask Philippa", "webUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/lifeandstyle/2023/apr/16/is-it-fair-to-put-my-abusive-elderly-father-into-a-home-ask-philippa-perry", "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/lifeandstyle/2023/apr/16/is-it-fair-to-put-my-abusive-elderly-father-into-a-home-ask-philippa-perry", "fields": {"headline": "Is it fair to put my abusive, elderly father into a home?", "standfirst": "A home is the best place for him now \u2013 and it\u2019s time to talk to friends or a therapist so you can hear and have compassion for yourself without fearing your father", "trailText": "A home is the best place for him now, Says Philippa Perry. And it\u2019s time to talk to friends or a therapist so you can hear and have compassion for yourself without fearing your father", "byline": "Philippa Perry", "main": "<figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"ae171d6e25d51266cb4d22e1205b7193619d4aed\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/ae171d6e25d51266cb4d22e1205b7193619d4aed/0_0_5138_3084/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Nurse pushing patient in wheelchair in a plush care home\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u2018He won\u2019t have to worry about his meals and setting fire to the kitchen, he will be looked after, there\u2019ll be company for him and a clean bed.\u2019</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>", "body": "<p><em><strong>The dilemma</strong></em><strong> </strong><em>I\u2019ve never known, despite asking both my parents, why, for the first four years of my life, I lived with my grandmother. Then she died and I moved in with my parents. Like my grandmother, my parents were strict and </em><em>beat me. I was often bruised and </em><em>occasionally bloodied</em><em>. They demanded complete obedience. I would be hit if I got a less than perfect grade for any subject at school. It taught me to be a hard worker, so I am pretty successful now. I am in my 40s, know some nice people and could have a good life if every spare minute wasn\u2019t spent either with my father or fretting about him.</em></p> <p><em>My mother died five years ago and my elderly father\u2019s health and dementia require</em><em> a lot of care. The past two years were super-difficult and the last six months were closer to hell. I have appointed a care agency to come in, which he hates, and he needs more supervision than they are able to provide</em><em>.</em></p> <p><em>He wants me to live in and be his sole carer. </em><em>He cannot see why I have my own flat. </em><em>He regards single, childless women as having nothing to do. He has never been interested in my career and says I don\u2019t need it as he could give me housekeeping! He can put on a front of charming sociability </em><em>and everything ugly is happening behind closed doors, similar to when I was a child </em><em>and, as when I was a child, I don\u2019t tell anyone about his bullying. I feel ashamed as if it is my fault. I know it isn\u2019t, but these feelings defy reason.</em></p> <p><em>I</em><em>\u2019ve </em><em>found him a nice place in an expensive home. I </em><em>broached the subject and he tried to make me feel guilty for even mentioning it. His dementia is taking hold now</em><em> \u2013 he recently caused a small fire in his kitchen</em><em>; he isn\u2019t safe at home</em><em>. I could get him in</em><em>to the home if I said it was for a holiday. I\u2019m shaking even as I write this</em><em>. I feel </em><em>if I do this against his will, I\u2019m doing something dreadfully wrong. How can I square it with myself?</em></p> <p><em><strong>Philippa\u2019s answer </strong></em>I expect there is still a small voice inside you which believes that if only you could get it right for him then he\u2019d be a loving father. It is too scary when we are children to feel that the people responsible for us don\u2019t love us and aren\u2019t safe people, and so it is easier to believe that, if only we could get it right for them, everything would be OK. And, paradoxically, often the worse we were treated the more ingrained and lingering this irrational belief becomes.</p> <p>You are an adult now, but a part of you, the traumatised part of you, is still that poor child cowering from blows and thinking she must be bad otherwise this wouldn\u2019t be happening. Please look at that undefended, vulnerable little girl inside you and give her the love and protection now that she never had then. She and you will never get anything right for your father. The time has come to get it right for you.</p> <p>I believe you will feel less ashamed of your past and ongoing mistreatment when you talk about it more. Talk to your friends or a therapist. To start to do this will feel like a giant leap, but it will get easier. When you say things out loud about how you were treated and of your legacy of shame and fear, you will be able to hear yourself and feel for yourself more, and experience fear and shame less.</p> <p>I can understand your shaking as you write, because even though you are now a responsible, independent adult with a good career, a part of you is still expecting a physical and mental beating for going against your father\u2019s wishes. It\u2019s time to act, despite your fear. So, take him \u201con holiday\u201d, and take a friend with you. Get support: everything is better with a sympathetic witness. He\u2019ll also behave better if someone else is there. This isn\u2019t punishing him, it is giving him the best possible care, even if he can\u2019t realise that.</p> <p>I endorse the \u201con holiday\u201d approach because having advanced dementia means he\u2019ll be more comfortable with a familiar idea like a holiday, and \u201cmoving to a care home\u201d would be difficult to take in. And it will be like a holiday. He won\u2019t have to worry about his meals and setting fire to the kitchen, he will be looked after, there\u2019ll be company for him and a clean bed. At my father\u2019s home, they gave them all sherry before lunch. You can square it with yourself because his current situation is unsafe and unsustainable \u2013 and he doesn\u2019t even sound happy.</p> <p>There\u2019s one point I want to pick you up on \u2013 your career success now is despite being beaten, not because of it. Had your father been protective, loving and kind, this might all feel different. Then he would not want you to sacrifice your life to be his full-time carer, and you would have been far more willing to spend more time with him.</p> <p>Recommended books: <em><a href=\"https://guardianbookshop.com/contented-dementia-9780091901813\" title=\"\">Contented Dementia</a></em> by Oliver James; <em><a href=\"https://guardianbookshop.com/toxic-parents-9780553814828\" title=\"\">Toxic Parents</a></em> by Susan Forward. </p> <p><em>If you have a question, send a brief email to <a href=\"mailto:askphilippa@observer.co.uk\">askphilippa@observer.co.uk</a><br>Submissions are subject to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/info/2011/oct/06/submitting-content-to-print-publications\" title=\"\">our terms and conditions</a></em></p>", "newspaperPageNumber": "38", "wordcount": "904", "commentCloseDate": "2023-04-16T12:00:00Z", "commentable": "true", "firstPublicationDate": "2023-04-16T05:00:03Z", "isInappropriateForSponsorship": "false", "isPremoderated": "true", "lastModified": "2023-04-16T11:47:29Z", "newspaperEditionDate": "2023-04-16T00:00:00Z", "productionOffice": "UK", "publication": "The Observer", "shortUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/p/nm5dj", "shouldHideAdverts": "false", "showInRelatedContent": "true", "thumbnail": "https://media.guim.co.uk/ae171d6e25d51266cb4d22e1205b7193619d4aed/0_0_5138_3084/500.jpg", "legallySensitive": "false", "lang": "en", "isLive": "true", "bodyText": "The dilemma I\u2019ve never known, despite asking both my parents, why, for the first four years of my life, I lived with my grandmother. Then she died and I moved in with my parents. Like my grandmother, my parents were strict and beat me. I was often bruised and occasionally bloodied. They demanded complete obedience. I would be hit if I got a less than perfect grade for any subject at school. It taught me to be a hard worker, so I am pretty successful now. I am in my 40s, know some nice people and could have a good life if every spare minute wasn\u2019t spent either with my father or fretting about him. My mother died five years ago and my elderly father\u2019s health and dementia require a lot of care. The past two years were super-difficult and the last six months were closer to hell. I have appointed a care agency to come in, which he hates, and he needs more supervision than they are able to provide. He wants me to live in and be his sole carer. He cannot see why I have my own flat. He regards single, childless women as having nothing to do. He has never been interested in my career and says I don\u2019t need it as he could give me housekeeping! He can put on a front of charming sociability and everything ugly is happening behind closed doors, similar to when I was a child and, as when I was a child, I don\u2019t tell anyone about his bullying. I feel ashamed as if it is my fault. I know it isn\u2019t, but these feelings defy reason. I\u2019ve found him a nice place in an expensive home. I broached the subject and he tried to make me feel guilty for even mentioning it. His dementia is taking hold now \u2013 he recently caused a small fire in his kitchen; he isn\u2019t safe at home. I could get him into the home if I said it was for a holiday. I\u2019m shaking even as I write this. I feel if I do this against his will, I\u2019m doing something dreadfully wrong. How can I square it with myself? Philippa\u2019s answer I expect there is still a small voice inside you which believes that if only you could get it right for him then he\u2019d be a loving father. It is too scary when we are children to feel that the people responsible for us don\u2019t love us and aren\u2019t safe people, and so it is easier to believe that, if only we could get it right for them, everything would be OK. And, paradoxically, often the worse we were treated the more ingrained and lingering this irrational belief becomes. You are an adult now, but a part of you, the traumatised part of you, is still that poor child cowering from blows and thinking she must be bad otherwise this wouldn\u2019t be happening. Please look at that undefended, vulnerable little girl inside you and give her the love and protection now that she never had then. She and you will never get anything right for your father. The time has come to get it right for you. I believe you will feel less ashamed of your past and ongoing mistreatment when you talk about it more. Talk to your friends or a therapist. To start to do this will feel like a giant leap, but it will get easier. When you say things out loud about how you were treated and of your legacy of shame and fear, you will be able to hear yourself and feel for yourself more, and experience fear and shame less. I can understand your shaking as you write, because even though you are now a responsible, independent adult with a good career, a part of you is still expecting a physical and mental beating for going against your father\u2019s wishes. It\u2019s time to act, despite your fear. So, take him \u201con holiday\u201d, and take a friend with you. Get support: everything is better with a sympathetic witness. He\u2019ll also behave better if someone else is there. This isn\u2019t punishing him, it is giving him the best possible care, even if he can\u2019t realise that. I endorse the \u201con holiday\u201d approach because having advanced dementia means he\u2019ll be more comfortable with a familiar idea like a holiday, and \u201cmoving to a care home\u201d would be difficult to take in. And it will be like a holiday. He won\u2019t have to worry about his meals and setting fire to the kitchen, he will be looked after, there\u2019ll be company for him and a clean bed. At my father\u2019s home, they gave them all sherry before lunch. You can square it with yourself because his current situation is unsafe and unsustainable \u2013 and he doesn\u2019t even sound happy. There\u2019s one point I want to pick you up on \u2013 your career success now is despite being beaten, not because of it. Had your father been protective, loving and kind, this might all feel different. Then he would not want you to sacrifice your life to be his full-time carer, and you would have been far more willing to spend more time with him. Recommended books: Contented Dementia by Oliver James; Toxic Parents by Susan Forward. It\u2019s time to act, despite your fear. So, take him \u201con holiday\u201d, and take a friend with you. Get support: everything is better with a sympathetic witness. He\u2019ll also behave better if someone else is there. This isn\u2019t punishing him, it is giving him the best possible care, even if he can\u2019t realise that.</p> <p>I endorse the \u201con holiday\u201d approach because having advanced dementia means he\u2019ll be more comfortable with a familiar idea like a holiday, and \u201cmoving to a care home\u201d would be difficult to take in. And it will be like a holiday. He won\u2019t have to worry about his meals and setting fire to the kitchen, he will be looked after, there\u2019ll be company for him and a clean bed. At my father\u2019s home, they gave them all sherry before lunch. You can square it with yourself because his current situation is unsafe and unsustainable \u2013 and he doesn\u2019t even sound happy.</p> <p>There\u2019s one point I want to pick you up on \u2013 your career success now is despite being beaten, not because of it. Had your father been protective, loving and kind, this might all feel different. Then he would not want you to sacrifice your life to be his full-time carer, and you would have been far more willing to spend more time with him.</p> <p>Recommended books: <em><a href=\"https://guardianbookshop.com/contented-dementia-9780091901813\" title=\"\">Contented Dementia</a></em> by Oliver James; <em><a href=\"https://guardianbookshop.com/toxic-parents-9780553814828\" title=\"\">Toxic Parents</a></em> by Susan Forward. </p> <p><em>If you have a question, send a brief email to <a href=\"mailto:askphilippa@observer.co.uk\">askphilippa@observer.co.uk</a><br>Submissions are subject to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/info/2011/oct/06/submitting-content-to-print-publications\" title=\"\">our terms and conditions</a></em></p>", "bodyTextSummary": "The dilemma I\u2019ve never known, despite asking both my parents, why, for the first four years of my life, I lived with my grandmother. Then she died and I moved in with my parents. Like my grandmother, my parents were strict and beat me. I was often bruised and occasionally bloodied. They demanded complete obedience. I would be hit if I got a less than perfect grade for any subject at school. It taught me to be a hard worker, so I am pretty successful now. I am in my 40s, know some nice people and could have a good life if every spare minute wasn\u2019t spent either with my father or fretting about him. My mother died five years ago and my elderly father\u2019s health and dementia require a lot of care. The past two years were super-difficult and the last six months were closer to hell. I have appointed a care agency to come in, which he hates, and he needs more supervision than they are able to provide. He wants me to live in and be his sole carer. He cannot see why I have my own flat. He regards single, childless women as having nothing to do. He has never been interested in my career and says I don\u2019t need it as he could give me housekeeping! He can put on a front of charming sociability and everything ugly is happening behind closed doors, similar to when I was a child and, as when I was a child, I don\u2019t tell anyone about his bullying. I feel ashamed as if it is my fault. I know it isn\u2019t, but these feelings defy reason. I\u2019ve found him a nice place in an expensive home. I broached the subject and he tried to make me feel guilty for even mentioning it. His dementia is taking hold now \u2013 he recently caused a small fire in his kitchen; he isn\u2019t safe at home. I could get him into the home if I said it was for a holiday. I\u2019m shaking even as I write this. I feel if I do this against his will, I\u2019m doing something dreadfully wrong. How can I square it with myself? Philippa\u2019s answer I expect there is still a small voice inside you which believes that if only you could get it right for him then he\u2019d be a loving father. It is too scary when we are children to feel that the people responsible for us don\u2019t love us and aren\u2019t safe people, and so it is easier to believe that, if only we could get it right for them, everything would be OK. And, paradoxically, often the worse we were treated the more ingrained and lingering this irrational belief becomes. You are an adult now, but a part of you, the traumatised part of you, is still that poor child cowering from blows and thinking she must be bad otherwise this wouldn\u2019t be happening. Please look at that undefended, vulnerable little girl inside you and give her the love and protection now that she never had then. She and you will never get anything right for your father. The time has come to get it right for you. I believe you will feel less ashamed of your past and ongoing mistreatment when you talk about it more. Talk to your friends or a therapist. To start to do this will feel like a giant leap, but it will get easier. When you say things out loud about how you were treated and of your legacy of shame and fear, you will be able to hear yourself and feel for yourself more, and experience fear and shame less. I can understand your shaking as you write, because even though you are now a responsible, independent adult with a good career, a part of you is still expecting a physical and mental beating for going against your father\u2019s wishes. It\u2019s time to act, despite your fear. So, take him \u201con holiday\u201d, and take a friend with you. Get support: everything is better with a sympathetic witness. He\u2019ll also behave better if someone else is there. This isn\u2019t punishing him, it is giving him the best possible care, even if he can\u2019t realise that. I endorse the \u201con holiday\u201d approach because having advanced dementia means he\u2019ll be more comfortable with a familiar idea like a holiday, and \u201cmoving to a care home\u201d would be difficult to take in. And it will be like a holiday. He won\u2019t have to worry about his meals and setting fire to the kitchen, he will be looked after, there\u2019ll be company for him and a clean bed. At my father\u2019s home, they gave them all sherry before lunch. You can square it with yourself because his current situation is unsafe and unsustainable \u2013 and he doesn\u2019t even sound happy. There\u2019s one point I want to pick you up on \u2013 your career success now is despite being beaten, not because of it. Had your father been protective, loving and kind, this might all feel different. Then he would not want you to sacrifice your life to be his full-time carer, and you would have been far more willing to spend more time with him. Recommended books: Contented Dementia by Oliver James; Toxic Parents by Susan Forward.
