"food/2023/apr/16/jay-rayner-restaurant-review-the-kolossi-attica-london-much-better-than-revelatory", "type": "article", "sectionId": "food", "sectionName": "Food", "webPublicationDate": "2023-04-16T05:00:02Z", "webTitle": "The Kolossi/Attica, London: \u2018It\u2019s not revelatory, it\u2019s much better than that\u2019 \u2013 restaurant review", "webUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/food/2023/apr/16/jay-rayner-restaurant-review-the-kolossi-attica-london-much-better-than-revelatory", "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/food/2023/apr/16/jay-rayner-restaurant-review-the-kolossi-attica-london-much-better-than-revelatory", "fields": {"headline": "The Kolossi/Attica, London: \u2018It\u2019s not revelatory, it\u2019s much better than that\u2019 \u2013 restaurant review", "standfirst": "They say you can\u2019t revisit past times, but at this much-loved old haunt, it\u2019s not quite true", "trailText": "They say you can\u2019t revisit past times, but at this much-loved old haunt, it\u2019s not quite true, writes Jay Rayner", "byline": "Jay Rayner", "main": "<figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"d56bef5f6dd4c447f945c35f71b184a77ed839fa\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/d56bef5f6dd4c447f945c35f71b184a77ed839fa/0_270_5523_3314/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Greek revival: The Kolossi/Attica. \" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Greek revival: the bright and welcoming interior of Kolossi/Attica.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Sophia Evans/The Observer</span> </figcaption> </figure>", "body": "<p><strong><a href=\"https://thekolossi.com/\">The Kolossi/Attica</a>, 50-60 Rosebery Avenue, London EC1R 4RR. Small plates, starters \u00a33.80-\u00a39.80, larger dishes \u00a315.80-\u00a321.50, desserts \u00a37.50, wines from \u00a327</strong></p> <p>Let\u2019s pull back the velvet curtain on this restaurant reviewing lark. Because readers of the <em>Observer</em> and our daily sibling, the <em>Guardian</em>, would be unimpressed if both myself and the saintly <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/profile/gracedent\" title=\"\">Grace Dent</a> reviewed the same restaurants, we quietly co-ordinate what we are going to write about. I let a <em>Guardian</em> colleague know where I\u2019m planning to go and they return the compliment. Mostly these emails contain just the name of a restaurant from one side and a reply with the single word \u201cyours\u201d from the other.</p> <p>When I emailed to say I was planning to review <a href=\"https://thekolossi.com/menu\" title=\"\">the Kolossi grill</a> on Rosebery Avenue in London\u2019s Clerkenwell, my famously lugubrious colleague\u2019s response was the word \u201cblimey\u201d followed by a sentence questioning the sanity of everyone involved. I understood why. The Kolossi first opened in 1966, a short stumble from what in <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/gnm-archive/2002/jun/11/1\" title=\"\">1976</a> became the <em>Guardian</em> and later the <em>Observer</em>\u2019s home, until 2008. It has a kitsch Palladian frontage, as if it were a Poundland offshoot of the Acropolis. It would be unfair to say it was actively bad. It was part of a generation of mostly Greek Cypriot restaurants scattered across the UK that offered a narrow, reliably robust version of the Greek repertoire.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"f34c97a28544fbc33d5f4b64160026b47f6121b4\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/f34c97a28544fbc33d5f4b64160026b47f6121b4/0_187_5616_3370/1000.jpg\" alt=\"\u2018Whipped and frothy\u2019: tarama and pitta.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u2018Whipped and frothy\u2019: tarama and pitta.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Sophia Evans/The Observer</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>For the newspapers\u2019 staff, however, the Kolossi\u2019s greatest virtue was simply that it was there. You went there after the pub, guided by instinct. With the searing wit of headline writers everywhere, it was nicknamed the Colossal Bill, perhaps in celebration of its cheapness. Or it was called something else, which was hilariously funny in 1992 and really isn\u2019t now. Back then the walls were panelled in orange-varnished wood. There was plastic ivy dangling from the beamed ceiling and, on occasion, a belly dancer. A paid one, not a subeditor who had got carried away, two bottles of caustic retsina to the bad. Although that might have happened. It could all get very messy. The lovely staff were forgiving.</p> <p>So no, not an obvious candidate for the once over. But then I learned that the owners these past 34 years had moved on. The lease had been taken over by a businessman named David Lonsdale, who is involved with the nearby <a href=\"https://www.thesekforde.com/\" title=\"\">Sekforde</a>. He had restored that Georgian pub to its former glory. Now he was doing the same with the Kolossi. I checked out the menu, which used to be long. It is now short and to the point and rather attractive.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"eb1c5b395b922cb60ad53078c40e5f3820655c73\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/eb1c5b395b922cb60ad53078c40e5f3820655c73/0_117_5172_3102/1000.jpg\" alt=\"\u2018Baked with olive oil, feta, oregano and the sweetest of cherry tomatoes\u2019: prawns saganaki.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u2018Baked with olive oil, feta, oregano and the sweetest of cherry tomatoes\u2019: prawns saganaki.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Sophia Evans/The Observer</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>So here I am once more, clambering out of a cab at the scene of so many lost nights. The faux Palladian fa\u00e7ade is still in place, but it has been repainted from a light olive green to a deep Aegean blue. And hang on. What\u2019s this? The name seems to have changed. It is now called Attica, presumably after the Greek peninsula. Although at the time of writing it\u2019s still called the Kolossi on its website and elsewhere online. We\u2019re modern. We can handle this two names thing. Inside, the wood panelling has been stripped and repainted in shades of cream. The plastic ivy on the ceiling has been replaced by a fairy-lit canopy of twigs. The paper tablecloths have gone. It\u2019s now all solid marble.</p> <p>But it\u2019s still that room. It\u2019s still that place where I remember putting the world to rights and filling up my glass again mid-anecdote, even though I knew I\u2019d pay for it the next morning, and then filling the glass once more. I can still hear the echoes of when it operated as an unofficial club room for a bunch of people with a shared endeavour. In those spaces the quality of the cooking matters far less than the fact that you have all chosen to sit in it together.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"d4aa518dfa1153453470551c370b96d05da75f78\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/d4aa518dfa1153453470551c370b96d05da75f78/0_137_5616_3370/1000.jpg\" alt=\"\u2018Served on a bed of nutty new potatoes with its own lemon-boosted sauce\u2019: seabass.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u2018Served on a bed of nutty new potatoes with its own lemon-boosted sauce\u2019: seabass.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Sophia Evans/The Observer</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>And then the food started to arrive and I knew we were somewhere else entirely. Once upon a time restaurants like the original Kolossi were the only place you\u2019d find tarama and it would be bright pink and grainy and acidic. Now it\u2019s become a part of menus in so many other ambitious restaurants, as a mark of good taste. The bar has been raised. This version, from the short list of dips with which the menu opens, is a match for any of them. It\u2019s whipped and frothy and comes dressed with a shiny puddle of olive oil, lots of chopped dill and a single salty kalamata olive. The accompanying pitta is warm and soft and oil-slicked. There are deep-fried zucchini balls, creamy inside and crisp outside, with a coarse and garlicky tzatziki. There are three fat prawns \u201csaganaki\u201d \u2013 baked with olive oil, feta, oregano and the sweetest of cherry tomatoes now bursting from their skins. Hold back some of the pitta for mopping duties.</p> <p>The choice of larger dishes is concise: three seafood dishes, a few souvlaki and a couple of baked things, including moussaka. A skewer of lamb souvlaki brings salty, charred meat that has clearly been grilled at speed to save it from being ruined, with a rugged salad and a dish of a lemony tahini dressing. A fat fillet of seabass, its skin crisped, slouches louchely on a bed of nutty new potatoes with its own lemon-boosted sauce. Only a Cretan salad, made with hefty chunks of barley rusk, is a little heavy-going. It may well be exactly as it\u2019s meant to be. In which case what it\u2019s meant to be turns out to be a little heavy-going.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"034ebca89b62c5279ace7c0737382c517f714e43\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/034ebca89b62c5279ace7c0737382c517f714e43/0_193_5158_3094/1000.jpg\" alt=\"\u2018Soft, fragrant\u2019: orange cake and mastic ice-cream.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u2018Soft, fragrant\u2019: orange cake and mastic ice-cream.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Sophia Evans/The Observer</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>This is not cooking that redefines the very notion of Greek food. It\u2019s not revelatory. It\u2019s so much better than that. It\u2019s the essentials done with due care and attention. Service is run by one lovely bearded man from Athens, who doesn\u2019t miss a beat and seems delighted to see his customers enjoying themselves. In the half-open kitchen, there\u2019s one older chap, grilling the meats and dribbling the olive oil in all the right places. Desserts are syrup-drenched pastries with a scoop of mastic ice-cream, with that slightly bouncy, rubbery texture, familiar to anyone who has ever bought a cornet in the hottest of climes. We have a slab of soft, fragrant orange cake and another made with walnuts. The short, entirely Greek wine list, currently boasting just one retsina, helps it all on its way. The Kolossi Grill AKA Attica grill is that rare thing; a venerable restaurant that has found a way to avoid decline and start afresh. I used to know you very well, old friend. I look forward to getting to know you all over again.</p> <h2><strong>News bites</strong></h2> <p>Two restaurants, both very much enjoyed by this column, have announced they are closing, and both attribute the decision to rising costs and generally appalling trading conditions. In Worthing, <em>MasterChef</em> winner Kenny Tutt closes Bayside Social today, but will continue to focus on <a href=\"https://www.pitchrestaurant.co.uk/\">Pitch</a>, his other restaurant in the town. Meanwhile in Ramsbottom, Levanter Fine Foods has now closed. However, its sister restaurant, the Basque-influenced Baratxuri, which recently relocated to Manchester city centre, is very much alive and well (<a href=\"http://baratxuri.co.uk/\">baratxuri.co.uk</a>).</p> <p>There\u2019s money in pizza. Fulham Shore, the company that operates the Franco Manca chain as well as the Real Greek group, looks set to accept a cash takeover offer from a Japanese restaurant business called Toridoll Holdings. The offer values Fulham Shore at just shy of \u00a395m and would benefit chairman and co-founder David Page to the tune of \u00a311m. Franco Manca, which started with one site in Brixton market in 2008, now has just over 70 outlets across the UK (<a href=\"http://francomanca.co.uk/\">francomanca.co.uk</a>).</p> <p>We started with news of closures so let\u2019s finish with news of an opening. Josh and Victoria Overington, who closed their York restaurant Le Cochon Aveugle late last year, have announced the opening in June of My\u0301se, which is pronounced meez and comes from the Anglo Saxon for \u201ceating at the table\u201d. It\u2019s a restaurant with rooms in the North Yorkshire village of Hovingham and as with so many ambitious new openings at the moment, will offer an evening tasting menu. It will cost \u00a3110 a head and will include a duck liver and walnut pie, baked scallop with sea urchin, and goats milk ice-cream with caramelised honey and raspberry juice.</p> <p><em>Email Jay at jay.rayner@observer.co.uk or follow him on Twitter <a href=\"https://twitter.com/jayrayner1?ref_src=twsrc%5Egoogle%7Ctwcamp%5Eserp%7Ctwgr%5Eauthor\">@jayrayner1</a></em></p>", "newspaperPageNumber": "26", "wordcount": "1391", "commentCloseDate": "2023-04-16T20:45:00Z", "commentable": "true", "firstPublicationDate": "2023-04-16T05:00:02Z", "isInappropriateForSponsorship": "false", "isPremoderated": "false", "lastModified": "2023-04-16T20:29:27Z", "newspaperEditionDate": "2023-04-16T00:00:00Z", "productionOffice": "UK", "publication": "The Observer", "shortUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/p/nmac8", "shouldHideAdverts": "false", "showInRelatedContent": "true", "thumbnail": "https://media.guim.co.uk/d56bef5f6dd4c447f945c35f71b184a77ed839fa/0_270_5523_3314/500.jpg", "legallySensitive": "false", "lang": "en", "isLive": "true", "bodyText": "The Kolossi/Attica, 50-60 Rosebery Avenue, London EC1R 4RR. Small plates, starters \u00a33.80-\u00a39.80, larger dishes \u00a315.80-\u00a321.50, desserts \u00a37.50, wines from \u00a327 Let\u2019s pull back the velvet curtain on this restaurant reviewing lark. Because readers of the Observer and our daily sibling, the Guardian, would be unimpressed if both myself and the saintly Grace Dent reviewed the same restaurants, we quietly co-ordinate what we are going to write about. I let a Guardian colleague know where I\u2019m planning to go and they return the compliment. Mostly these emails contain just the name of a restaurant from one side and a reply with the single word \u201cyours\u201d from the other. When I emailed to say I was planning to review the Kolossi grill on Rosebery Avenue in London\u2019s Clerkenwell, my famously lugubrious colleague\u2019s response was the word \u201cblimey\u201d followed by a sentence questioning the sanity of everyone involved. I understood why. The Kolossi first opened in 1966, a short stumble from what in 1976 became the Guardian and later the Observer\u2019s home, until 2008. It has a kitsch Palladian frontage, as if it were a Poundland offshoot of the Acropolis. It would be unfair to say it was actively bad. It was part of a generation of mostly Greek Cypriot restaurants scattered across the UK that offered a narrow, reliably robust version of the Greek repertoire. For the newspapers\u2019 staff, however, the Kolossi\u2019s greatest virtue was simply that it was there. You went there after the pub, guided by instinct. With the searing wit of headline writers everywhere, it was nicknamed the Colossal Bill, perhaps in celebration of its cheapness. Or it was called something else, which was hilariously funny in 1992 and really isn\u2019t now. Back then the walls were panelled in orange-varnished wood. There was plastic ivy dangling from the beamed ceiling and, on occasion, a belly dancer. A paid one, not a subeditor who had got carried away, two bottles of caustic retsina to the bad. Although that might have happened. It could all get very messy. The lovely staff were forgiving. So no, not an obvious candidate for the once over. But then I learned that the owners these past 34 years had moved on. The lease had been taken over by a businessman named David Lonsdale, who is involved with the nearby Sekforde. He had restored that Georgian pub to its former glory. Now he was doing the same with the Kolossi. I checked out the menu, which used to be long. It is now short and to the point and rather attractive. So here I am once more, clambering out of a cab at the scene of so many lost nights. The faux Palladian fa\u00e7ade is still in place, but it has been repainted from a light olive green to a deep Aegean blue. And hang on. What\u2019s this? The name seems to have changed. It is now called Attica, presumably after the Greek peninsula. Although at the time of writing it\u2019s still called the Kolossi on its website and elsewhere online. We\u2019re modern. We can handle this two names thing. Inside, the wood panelling has been stripped and repainted in shades of cream. The plastic ivy on the ceiling has been replaced by a fairy-lit canopy of twigs. The paper tablecloths have gone. It\u2019s now all solid marble. But it\u2019s still that room. It\u2019s still that place where I remember putting the world to rights and filling up my glass again mid-anecdote, even though I knew I\u2019d pay for it the next morning, and then filling the glass once more. I can still hear the echoes of when it operated as an unofficial club room for a bunch of people with a shared endeavour. In those spaces the quality of the cooking matters far less than the fact that you have all chosen to sit in it together. And then the food started to arrive and I knew we were somewhere else entirely. Once upon a time restaurants like the original Kolossi were the only place you\u2019d find tarama and it would be bright pink and grainy and acidic. Now it\u2019s become a part of menus in so many other ambitious restaurants, as a mark of good taste. The bar has been raised. This version, from the short list of dips with which the menu opens, is a match for any of them. It\u2019s whipped and frothy and comes dressed with a shiny puddle of olive oil, lots of chopped dill and a single salty kalamata olive. The accompanying pitta is warm and soft and oil-slicked. There are deep-fried zucchini balls, creamy inside and crisp outside, with a coarse and garlicky tzatziki. There are three fat prawns \u201csaganaki\u201d \u2013 baked with olive oil, feta, oregano and the sweetest of cherry tomatoes now bursting from their skins. Hold back some of the pitta for mopping duties. The choice of larger dishes is concise: three seafood dishes, a few souvlaki and a couple of baked things, including moussaka. A skewer of lamb souvlaki brings salty, charred meat that has clearly been grilled at speed to save it from being ruined, with a rugged salad and a dish of a lemony tahini dressing. A fat fillet of seabass, its skin crisped, slouches louchely on a bed of nutty new potatoes with its own lemon-boosted sauce. Only a Cretan salad, made with hefty chunks of barley rusk, is a little heavy-going. It may well be exactly as it\u2019s meant to be. In which case what it\u2019s meant to be turns out to be a little heavy-going. This is not cooking that redefines the very notion of Greek food. It\u2019s not revelatory. It\u2019s so much better than that. It\u2019s the essentials done with due care and attention. Service is run by one lovely bearded man from Athens, who doesn\u2019t miss a beat and seems delighted to see his customers enjoying themselves. In the half-open kitchen, there\u2019s one older chap, grilling the meats and dribbling the olive oil in all the right places. Desserts are syrup-drenched pastries with a scoop of mastic ice-cream, with that slightly bouncy, rubbery texture, familiar to anyone who has ever bought a cornet in the hottest of climes. We have a slab of soft, fragrant orange cake and another made with walnuts. The short, entirely Greek wine list, currently boasting just one retsina, helps it all on its way. The Kolossi Grill AKA Attica grill is that rare thing; a venerable restaurant that has found a way to avoid decline and start afresh. I used to know you very well, old friend. I look forward to getting to know you all over again. News bites Two restaurants, both very much enjoyed by this column, have announced they are closing, and both attribute the decision to rising costs and generally appalling trading conditions. In Worthing, MasterChef winner Kenny Tutt closes Bayside Social today, but will continue to focus on Pitch, his other restaurant in the town. Meanwhile in Ramsbottom, Levanter Fine Foods has now closed. However, its sister restaurant, the Basque-influenced Baratxuri, which recently relocated to Manchester city centre, is very much alive and well (baratxuri.co.uk). There\u2019s money in pizza. Fulham Shore, the company that operates the Franco Manca chain as well as the Real Greek group, looks set to accept a cash takeover offer from a Japanese restaurant business called Toridoll Holdings. The offer values Fulham Shore at just shy of \u00a395m and would benefit chairman and co-founder David Page to the tune of \u00a311m. Franco Manca, which started with one site in Brixton market in 2008, now has just over 70 outlets across the UK (francomanca.co.uk). We started with news of closures so let\u2019s finish with news of an opening. Josh and Victoria Overington, who closed their York restaurant Le Cochon Aveugle late last year, have announced the opening in June of My\u0301se, which is pronounced meez and comes from the Anglo Saxon for \u201ceating at the table\u201d. It\u2019s a restaurant with rooms in the North Yorkshire village of Hovingham and as with so many ambitious new openings at the moment, will offer an evening tasting menu. It will cost \u00a3110 a head and will include a duck liver and walnut pie, baked scallop with sea urchin, and goats milk ice-cream with caramelised honey and raspberry juice. Only a Cretan salad, made with hefty chunks of barley rusk, is a little heavy-going. It may well be exactly as it\u2019s meant to be. In which case what it\u2019s meant to be turns out to be a little heavy-going.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"034ebca89b62c5279ace7c0737382c517f714e43\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/034ebca89b62c5279ace7c0737382c517f714e43/0_193_5158_3094/1000.jpg\" alt=\"\u2018Soft, fragrant\u2019: orange cake and mastic ice-cream.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u2018Soft, fragrant\u2019: orange cake and mastic ice-cream.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Sophia Evans/The Observer</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>This is not cooking that redefines the very notion of Greek food. It\u2019s not revelatory. It\u2019s so much better than that. It\u2019s the essentials done with due care and attention. Service is run by one lovely bearded man from Athens, who doesn\u2019t miss a beat and seems delighted to see his customers enjoying themselves. In the half-open kitchen, there\u2019s one older chap, grilling the meats and dribbling the olive oil in all the right places. Desserts are syrup-drenched pastries with a scoop of mastic ice-cream, with that slightly bouncy, rubbery texture, familiar to anyone who has ever bought a cornet in the hottest of climes. We have a slab of soft, fragrant orange cake and another made with walnuts. The short, entirely Greek wine list, currently boasting just one retsina, helps it all on its way. The Kolossi Grill AKA Attica grill is that rare thing; a venerable restaurant that has found a way to avoid decline and start afresh. I used to know you very well, old friend. I look forward to getting to know you all over again.</p> <h2><strong>News bites</strong></h2> <p>Two restaurants, both very much enjoyed by this column, have announced they are closing, and both attribute the decision to rising costs and generally appalling trading conditions. In Worthing, <em>MasterChef</em> winner Kenny Tutt closes Bayside Social today, but will continue to focus on <a href=\"https://www.pitchrestaurant.co.uk/\">Pitch</a>, his other restaurant in the town. Meanwhile in Ramsbottom, Levanter Fine Foods has now closed. However, its sister restaurant, the Basque-influenced Baratxuri, which recently relocated to Manchester city centre, is very much alive and well (<a href=\"http://baratxuri.co.uk/\">baratxuri.co.uk</a>).</p> <p>There\u2019s money in pizza. Fulham Shore, the company that operates the Franco Manca chain as well as the Real Greek group, looks set to accept a cash takeover offer from a Japanese restaurant business called Toridoll Holdings. The offer values Fulham Shore at just shy of \u00a395m and would benefit chairman and co-founder David Page to the tune of \u00a311m. Franco Manca, which started with one site in Brixton market in 2008, now has just over 70 outlets across the UK (<a href=\"http://francomanca.co.uk/\">francomanca.co.uk</a>).</p> <p>We started with news of closures so let\u2019s finish with news of an opening. Josh and Victoria Overington, who closed their York restaurant Le Cochon Aveugle late last year, have announced the opening in June of My\u0301se, which is pronounced meez and comes from the Anglo Saxon for \u201ceating at the table\u201d. It\u2019s a restaurant with rooms in the North Yorkshire village of Hovingham and as with so many ambitious new openings at the moment, will offer an evening tasting menu. It will cost \u00a3110 a head and will include a duck liver and walnut pie, baked scallop with sea urchin, and goats milk ice-cream with caramelised honey and raspberry juice.</p> <p><em>Email Jay at jay.rayner@observer.co.uk or follow him on Twitter <a href=\"https://twitter.com/jayrayner1?ref_src=twsrc%5Egoogle%7Ctwcamp%5Eserp%7Ctwgr%5Eauthor\">@jayrayner1</a></em></p>", "bodyTextSummary": "The Kolossi/Attica, 50-60 Rosebery Avenue, London EC1R 4RR. Small plates, starters \u00a33.80-\u00a39.80, larger dishes \u00a315.80-\u00a321.50, desserts \u00a37.50, wines from \u00a327 Let\u2019s pull back the velvet curtain on this restaurant reviewing lark. Because readers of the Observer and our daily sibling, the Guardian, would be unimpressed if both myself and the saintly Grace Dent reviewed the same restaurants, we quietly co-ordinate what we are going to write about. I let a Guardian colleague know where I\u2019m planning to go and they return the compliment. Mostly these emails contain just the name of a restaurant from one side and a reply with the single word \u201cyours\u201d from the other. When I emailed to say I was planning to review the Kolossi grill on Rosebery Avenue in London\u2019s Clerkenwell, my famously lugubrious colleague\u2019s response was the word \u201cblimey\u201d followed by a sentence questioning the sanity of everyone involved. I understood why. The Kolossi first opened in 1966, a short stumble from what in 1976 became the Guardian and later the Observer\u2019s home, until 2008. It has a kitsch Palladian frontage, as if it were a Poundland offshoot of the Acropolis. It would be unfair to say it was actively bad. It was part of a generation of mostly Greek Cypriot restaurants scattered across the UK that offered a narrow, reliably robust version of the Greek repertoire.\nFor the newspapers\u2019 staff, however, the Kolossi\u2019s greatest virtue was simply that it was there. You went there after the pub, guided by instinct. With the searing wit of headline writers everywhere, it was nicknamed the Colossal Bill, perhaps in celebration of its cheapness. Or it was called something else, which was hilariously funny in 1992 and really isn\u2019t now. Back then the walls were panelled in orange-varnished wood. There was plastic ivy dangling from the beamed ceiling and, on occasion, a belly dancer. A paid one, not a subeditor who had got carried away, two bottles of caustic retsina to the bad. Although that might have happened. It could all get very messy. The lovely staff were forgiving. So no, not an obvious candidate for the once over. But then I learned that the owners these past 34 years had moved on. The lease had been taken over by a businessman named David Lonsdale, who is involved with the nearby Sekforde. He had restored that Georgian pub to its former glory. Now he was doing the same with the Kolossi. I checked out the menu, which used to be long. It is now short and to the point and rather attractive.\nSo here I am once more, clambering out of a cab at the scene of so many lost nights. The faux Palladian fa\u00e7ade is still in place, but it has been repainted from a light olive green to a deep Aegean blue. And hang on. What\u2019s this? The name seems to have changed. It is now called Attica, presumably after the Greek peninsula. Although at the time of writing it\u2019s still called the Kolossi on its website and elsewhere online. We\u2019re modern. We can handle this two names thing. Inside, the wood panelling has been stripped and repainted in shades of cream. The plastic ivy on the ceiling has been replaced by a fairy-lit canopy of twigs. The paper tablecloths have gone. It\u2019s now all solid marble. But it\u2019s still that room. It\u2019s still that place where I remember putting the world to rights and filling up my glass again mid-anecdote, even though I knew I\u2019d pay for it the next morning, and then filling the glass once more. I can still hear the echoes of when it operated as an unofficial club room for a bunch of people with a shared endeavour. In those spaces the quality of the cooking matters far less than the fact that you have all chosen to sit in it together.\nAnd then the food started to arrive and I knew we were somewhere else entirely. Once upon a time restaurants like the original Kolossi were the only place you\u2019d find tarama and it would be bright pink and grainy and acidic. Now it\u2019s become a part of menus in so many other ambitious restaurants, as a mark of good taste. The bar has been raised. This version, from the short list of dips with which the menu opens, is a match for any of them. It\u2019s whipped and frothy and comes dressed with a shiny puddle of olive oil, lots of chopped dill and a single salty kalamata olive. The accompanying pitta is warm and soft and oil-slicked. There are deep-fried zucchini balls, creamy inside and crisp outside, with a coarse and garlicky tzatziki. There are three fat prawns \u201csaganaki\u201d \u2013 baked with olive oil, feta, oregano and the sweetest of cherry tomatoes now bursting from their skins. Hold back some of the pitta for mopping duties. The choice of larger dishes is concise: three seafood dishes, a few souvlaki and a couple of baked things, including moussaka. A skewer of lamb souvlaki brings salty, charred meat that has clearly been grilled at speed to save it from being ruined, with a rugged salad and a dish of a lemony tahini dressing. A fat fillet of seabass, its skin crisped, slouches louchely on a bed of nutty new potatoes with its own lemon-boosted sauce. Only a Cretan salad, made with hefty chunks of barley rusk, is a little heavy-going. It may well be exactly as it\u2019s meant to be. In which case what it\u2019s meant to be turns out to be a little heavy-going.\nThis is not cooking that redefines the very notion of Greek food. It\u2019s not revelatory. It\u2019s so much better than that. It\u2019s the essentials done with due care and attention. Service is run by one lovely bearded man from Athens, who doesn\u2019t miss a beat and seems delighted to see his customers enjoying themselves. In the half-open kitchen, there\u2019s one older chap, grilling the meats and dribbling the olive oil in all the right places. Desserts are syrup-drenched pastries with a scoop of mastic ice-cream, with that slightly bouncy, rubbery texture, familiar to anyone who has ever bought a cornet in the hottest of climes. We have a slab of soft, fragrant orange cake and another made with walnuts. The short, entirely Greek wine list, currently boasting just one retsina, helps it all on its way. The Kolossi Grill AKA Attica grill is that rare thing; a venerable restaurant that has found a way to avoid decline and start afresh. I used to know you very well, old friend. I look forward to getting to know you all over again. [], "textTypeData": {"html": "<p>This is not cooking that redefines the very notion of Greek food. It\u2019s not revelatory. It\u2019s so much better than that. It\u2019s the essentials done with due care and attention. Service is run by one lovely bearded man from Athens, who doesn\u2019t miss a beat and seems delighted to see his customers enjoying themselves. In the half-open kitchen, there\u2019s one older chap, grilling the meats and dribbling the olive oil in all the right places. Desserts are syrup-drenched pastries with a scoop of mastic ice-cream, with that slightly bouncy, rubbery texture, familiar to anyone who has ever bought a cornet in the hottest of climes. We have a slab of soft, fragrant orange cake and another made with walnuts. The short, entirely Greek wine list, currently boasting just one retsina, helps it all on its way. The Kolossi Grill AKA Attica grill is that rare thing; a venerable restaurant that has found a way to avoid decline and start afresh. I used to know you very well, old friend. I look forward to getting to know you all over again.</p> \n<h2><strong>News bites</strong></h2> \n<p>Two restaurants, both very much enjoyed by this column, have announced they are closing, and both attribute the decision to rising costs and generally appalling trading conditions. In Worthing, <em>MasterChef</em> winner Kenny Tutt closes Bayside Social today, but will continue to focus on <a href=\"https://www.pitchrestaurant.co.uk/\">Pitch</a>, his other restaurant in the town. Meanwhile in Ramsbottom, Levanter Fine Foods has now closed. However, its sister restaurant, the Basque-influenced Baratxuri, which recently relocated to Manchester city centre, is very much alive and well (<a href=\"http://baratxuri.co.uk/\">baratxuri.co.uk</a>).</p> \n<p>There\u2019s money in pizza. Fulham Shore, the company that operates the Franco Manca chain as well as the Real Greek group, looks set to accept a cash takeover offer from a Japanese restaurant business called Toridoll Holdings. The offer values Fulham Shore at just shy of \u00a395m and would benefit chairman and co-founder David Page to the tune of \u00a311m. Franco Manca, which started with one site in Brixton market in 2008, now has just over 70 outlets across the UK (<a href=\"http://francomanca.co.uk/\">francomanca.co.uk</a>).</p> \n<p>We started with news of closures so let\u2019s finish with news of an opening. Josh and Victoria Overington, who closed their York restaurant Le Cochon Aveugle late last year, have announced the opening in June of My\u0301se, which is pronounced meez and comes from the Anglo Saxon for \u201ceating at the table\u201d. It\u2019s a restaurant with rooms in the North Yorkshire village of Hovingham and as with so many ambitious new openings at the moment, will offer an evening tasting menu. It will cost \u00a3110 a head and will include a duck liver and walnut pie, baked scallop with sea urchin, and goats milk ice-cream with caramelised honey and raspberry juice.</p> \n<p><em>Email Jay at jay.rayner@observer.co.uk or follow him on Twitter <a href=\"https://twitter.com/jayrayner1?ref_src=twsrc%5Egoogle%7Ctwcamp%5Eserp%7Ctwgr%5Eauthor\">@jayrayner1</a></em></p>"}}]}], "totalBodyBlocks": 1}, "rights": {"syndicatable": "true", "subscriptionDatabases": "true", "developerCommunity": "true"}, "section": {"id": "food", "webTitle": "Food", "webUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/food", "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/food", "editions": [{"id": "food", "webTitle": "Food", "webUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/food", "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/food", "code": "default"}]}, "isHosted": false, "pillarId": "pillar/lifestyle", "pillarName": "Lifestyle"}, {"id": "tv-and-radio/2023/apr/16/tv-tonight-danny-dyer-gazza-and-chris-eubanks-bizarre-reality-series", "type": "article", "sectionId": "tv-and-radio", "sectionName": "Television & radio", "webPublicationDate": "2023-04-16T05:00:02Z", "webTitle": "TV tonight: Danny Dyer, Gazza and Chris Eubank\u2019s bizarre reality series", "webUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/tv-and-radio/2023/apr/16/tv-tonight-danny-dyer-gazza-and-chris-eubanks-bizarre-reality-series", "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/tv-and-radio/2023/apr/16/tv-tonight-danny-dyer-gazza-and-chris-eubanks-bizarre-reality-series", "fields": {"headline": "TV tonight: Danny Dyer, Gazza and Chris Eubank\u2019s bizarre reality series", "standfirst": "<p>Scared of the Dark sticks a group of celebrities in a pitch black room for eight days. Send Paul Gascoigne in there, along with a handful of other celebrities \u2013 including blind comedian Chris McCausland, who admits he has a slight advantage for once \u2013 and you\u2019ve got this bonkers new reality series, which airs daily for five days. Host Danny Dyer sets the contestants tasks to do in the dark during their stay, driving one of them to nearly throw up and leave within 24 hours. Meanwhile, Eubank is doing everyone\u2019s head in with his philosophising. As Gazza puts it: \u201cEight days like this? Jesus.\u201d <strong>Hollie Richardson</strong></p> <h2>The Hunt for Raoul Moat</h2> <h2>9pm, ITV1</h2> <p>Matt Stokoe stars in this three-part drama, stripped across consecutive evenings, about the 2010 hunt for ex-bouncer Moat. Quite rightly, its main focus is Moat\u2019s victims \u2013 as we begin, Samantha Stobbart is striking up a new romance but is concerned about the lurking presence of her abusive former partner. <strong>Phil Harrison</strong></p> <h2>Our Changing Planet</h2> <h2>7pm, BBC One</h2> <p>Here\u2019s another run for this constructively polemical series that attempts to highlight humanity\u2019s impact on the natural world and explore a few fixes. In this opening episode, a coral reef is recorded in order to track the teeming life it harbours. This week, Deborah Meaden, Jay Blades, Judi Love and Mike Wozniak are attempting cheesecakes, vegan fancies and baked renderings of their first crushes. What could possibly go wrong? <strong>PH</strong></p> <h2>Great Expectations</h2> <h2>9pm, BBC One</h2> <p>\u201cHave you tried opium yet?\u201d Steven Knight\u2019s enjoyably down-and-dirty Dickens adaptation continues with our out-of-sorts hero hitting the rum hard while he runs dockside errands for spice magnate Mr Drummle. Pip\u2019s anguish only increases when Estella crashes back into his life in the worst possible way: as bride-to-be for his unctuous boss. <strong>Graeme Virtue</strong></p> <h2>Last Woman on Earth With Sara Pascoe</h2> <h2>9pm, BBC Two</h2> <p>Pascoe takes on the most endangered jobs in Denmark this week. In a crash course in construction, the comedian dabbles in the dying art of seaweed thatching. ARTIST STUDIO &amp; SANAM PICTURES.</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Lee Jung-jae, of Squid Game fame, directs and writes as well as stars in this passion project. An intelligent espionage thriller set in 80s South Korea, it skilfully uses the febrile political situation of a military dictatorship facing pro-democracy demonstrations and threats from North Korea to give historical depth to the search for a mole in the security services. Lee plays foreign unit chief Park; Jung Woo-sung is Kim, head of the domestic unit. One of them is the mole. Suspicion switches between them as we are entertainingly strung along. <strong>Simon Wardell</strong></p> <p><strong>The Outlaw Josey Wales, 9pm, ITV4</strong></p> <p>Clint Eastwood has often had fun forcing his lone gunman characters into ad-hoc family groupings \u2013 the joke being that the monosyllabic grouch finds he actually likes, even needs, company. Close-up. Relaxed mood concept in the historical italian city.2A6JD3X Glass of rose wine in man's hand against a blue sky background. Close-up. Relaxed mood concept in the historical italian city.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Pick and choose: Italy boasts at least 500 grape varieties, leading to hundreds of different types of wine.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Davide Zanin/Alamy</span> </figcaption> </figure>", "body": "<p><strong>Castello di Verduno Verduno Doc Basadone, Piedmont, Italy 2020 (\u00a318.67, <a href=\"https://www.justerinis.com/\">justerinis.com</a>) </strong>Of all the many reasons to love Italian wine, one of the most compelling is the sheer, dazzling array of grape varieties the country\u2019s winemakers have at their disposal. The Italian wine specialist Ian d\u2019Agata discusses about 500 in his marvellously detailed Native Wine Grapes of Italy \u2013 and that doesn\u2019t even cover the many non-natives, such as chardonnay, cabernet sauvignon, pinot noir and gewurztraminer, that can and do make some tremendous (and recognisably Italian) wines in various parts of the peninsula. A handful of Italian varieties have achieved international fame: pinot grigio; the sangiovese of chianti, brunello di montalcino and others in Tuscany and elsewhere in Central Italy; the nebbiolo of Piedmont\u2019s barolo and barbaresco. Others are barely known beyond their very particular local area, among them the vanishingly rare pelavegra, from the village of Verduno in barolo country, which makes delightfully delicate, strawberry-scented, finely black pepper-sprinkled reds such as Castello di Verduno\u2019s.</p> <p><strong>Contesa Pecorino Colline Pescaresi, Abruzzo, Italy 2021 (\u00a39.99, or \u00a37.99 as part of a mixed case of six, <a href=\"https://www.majestic.co.uk/\">majestic.co.uk</a>) </strong>As is the case in much of France, many Italian grape varieties are hidden from view, with producers traditionally preferring to label their wines by place rather than grape, as is the modern, New World-led way. Varieties such as another from Piedmont, Gavi\u2019s cortese \u2013 which is responsible for the pristine, subtly lemon-scented raciness of the Piona Bricco delle Farfalle Gavi del Comune di Gavi 2021 (\u00a315.99, or \u00a313.99 to members, nakendwines.com) \u2013 or the garganega of soave in the Veneto \u2013 the base for the soft pear, scented white flowers and almonds of Inama Soave Classico 2021 (\u00a315.99, or \u00a312.99, as part of a mixed case of six, <a href=\"https://www.majestic.co.uk/\">majestic.co.uk</a>) \u2013 are as a consequence rather less well known than they deserve to be. Elsewhere in Italy, however, the variety\u2019s the star, not least pecorino, which makes gently incisive, citrussy whites such as the Contesa, which, I reckon pairs better with simply grilled white fish than its namesake Italian sheep\u2019s cheese.</p> <p><strong>Maree d\u2019Ione Nero di Troia, Puglia, Italy 2022 (\u00a38.99, <a href=\"https://www.waitrose.com/\">Waitrose</a>) </strong>Partly because of the sheer range, but also thanks to the fact that so few of them have been planted to any great extent (or with much success) outside Italy itself, it\u2019s easy even for the most experienced and adventurous wine drinker to feel a sense of discovery with Italian grape varieties. Certainly, it\u2019s hard to get bored when you can, without taking very much trouble, find a different, delicious Italian wine, made from a different grape variety, for every day of the year. Recent candidates I\u2019ve enjoyed include a bottle from the very far north, Manni Nossing Kerner 2021 (\u00a323.95, <a href=\"https://www.leaandsandeman.co.uk/\">leaandsandeman.co.uk</a>), a startlingly, spring-water-pure example of kerner, a German grape variety that has made a home for itself in the province of Alto-Adige, while Maree d\u2019Ione Nero di Troia gives start billing to nero di troia, a red grape that usually falls behind primitivo or negroamaro in the local Puglian pecking order, but here makes a typically ripe, warming, darkly plummy, good-value southern red.</p> <p><em>Follow David Williams on Twitter <a href=\"https://twitter.com/daveydaibach?lang=en\" title=\"\">@Daveydaibach</a></em></p>", "newspaperPageNumber": "27", "wordcount": "521", "commentable": "false", "firstPublicationDate": "2023-04-16T05:00:02Z", "isInappropriateForSponsorship": "false", "isPremoderated": "false", "lastModified": "2023-04-16T05:00:02Z", "newspaperEditionDate": "2023-04-16T00:00:00Z", "productionOffice": "UK", "publication": "The Observer", "shortUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/p/nmbxz", "shouldHideAdverts": "false", "showInRelatedContent": "true", "thumbnail": "https://media.guim.co.uk/82ea0e1d4975cd7bf5c4134a337907535aeef3f4/0_482_3850_2311/500.jpg", "legallySensitive": "false", "lang": "en", "isLive": "true", "bodyText": "Castello di Verduno Verduno Doc Basadone, Piedmont, Italy 2020 (\u00a318.67, justerinis.com) Of all the many reasons to love Italian wine, one of the most compelling is the sheer, dazzling array of grape varieties the country\u2019s winemakers have at their disposal. Relaxed mood concept in the historical italian city.", "mediaId": "82ea0e1d4975cd7bf5c4134a337907535aeef3f4", "mediaApiUri": "https://api.media.gutools.co.uk/images/82ea0e1d4975cd7bf5c4134a337907535aeef3f4", "suppliersReference": "2A6JD3X", "imageType": "Photograph", "role": "showcase"}}]}, "body": [{"id": "389c4a11-2460-4224-9262-931864c9f00a", "bodyHtml": "<p><strong>Castello di Verduno Verduno Doc Basadone, Piedmont, Italy 2020 (\u00a318.67, <a href=\"https://www.justerinis.com/\">justerinis.com</a>) </strong>Of all the many reasons to love Italian wine, one of the most compelling is the sheer, dazzling array of grape varieties the country\u2019s winemakers have at their disposal. The Italian wine specialist Ian d\u2019Agata discusses about 500 in his marvellously detailed Native Wine Grapes of Italy \u2013 and that doesn\u2019t even cover the many non-natives, such as chardonnay, cabernet sauvignon, pinot noir and gewurztraminer, that can and do make some tremendous (and recognisably Italian) wines in various parts of the peninsula. A handful of Italian varieties have achieved international fame: pinot grigio; the sangiovese of chianti, brunello di montalcino and others in Tuscany and elsewhere in Central Italy; the nebbiolo of Piedmont\u2019s barolo and barbaresco. Others are barely known beyond their very particular local area, among them the vanishingly rare pelavegra, from the village of Verduno in barolo country, which makes delightfully delicate, strawberry-scented, finely black pepper-sprinkled reds such as Castello di Verduno\u2019s.</p> <p><strong>Contesa Pecorino Colline Pescaresi, Abruzzo, Italy 2021 (\u00a39.99, or \u00a37.99 as part of a mixed case of six, <a href=\"https://www.majestic.co.uk/\">majestic.co.uk</a>) </strong>As is the case in much of France, many Italian grape varieties are hidden from view, with producers traditionally preferring to label their wines by place rather than grape, as is the modern, New World-led way. Varieties such as another from Piedmont, Gavi\u2019s cortese \u2013 which is responsible for the pristine, subtly lemon-scented raciness of the Piona Bricco delle Farfalle Gavi del Comune di Gavi 2021 (\u00a315.99, or \u00a313.99 to members, nakendwines.com) \u2013 or the garganega of soave in the Veneto \u2013 the base for the soft pear, scented white flowers and almonds of Inama Soave Classico 2021 (\u00a315.99, or \u00a312.99, as part of a mixed case of six, <a href=\"https://www.majestic.co.uk/\">majestic.co.uk</a>) \u2013 are as a consequence rather less well known than they deserve to be. Elsewhere in Italy, however, the variety\u2019s the star, not least pecorino, which makes gently incisive, citrussy whites such as the Contesa, which, I reckon pairs better with simply grilled white fish than its namesake Italian sheep\u2019s cheese.</p> <p><strong>Maree d\u2019Ione Nero di Troia, Puglia, Italy 2022 (\u00a38.99, <a href=\"https://www.waitrose.com/\">Waitrose</a>) </strong>Partly because of the sheer range, but also thanks to the fact that so few of them have been planted to any great extent (or with much success) outside Italy itself, it\u2019s easy even for the most experienced and adventurous wine drinker to feel a sense of discovery with Italian grape varieties. Certainly, it\u2019s hard to get bored when you can, without taking very much trouble, find a different, delicious Italian wine, made from a different grape variety, for every day of the year. Recent candidates I\u2019ve enjoyed include a bottle from the very far north, Manni Nossing Kerner 2021 (\u00a323.95, <a href=\"https://www.leaandsandeman.co.uk/\">leaandsandeman.co.uk</a>), a startlingly, spring-water-pure example of kerner, a German grape variety that has made a home for itself in the province of Alto-Adige, while Maree d\u2019Ione Nero di Troia gives start billing to nero di troia, a red grape that usually falls behind primitivo or negroamaro in the local Puglian pecking order, but here makes a typically ripe, warming, darkly plummy, good-value southern red.</p> <p><em>Follow David Williams on Twitter <a href=\"https://twitter.com/daveydaibach?lang=en\" title=\"\">@Daveydaibach</a></em></p>", "bodyTextSummary": "Castello di Verduno Verduno Doc Basadone, Piedmont, Italy 2020 (\u00a318.67, justerinis.com) Of all the many reasons to love Italian wine, one of the most compelling is the sheer, dazzling array of grape varieties the country\u2019s winemakers have at their disposal. The Italian wine specialist Ian d\u2019Agata discusses about 500 in his marvellously detailed Native Wine Grapes of Italy \u2013 and that doesn\u2019t even cover the many non-natives, such as chardonnay, cabernet sauvignon, pinot noir and gewurztraminer, that can and do make some tremendous (and recognisably Italian) wines in various parts of the peninsula. Varieties such as another from Piedmont, Gavi\u2019s cortese \u2013 which is responsible for the pristine, subtly lemon-scented raciness of the Piona Bricco delle Farfalle Gavi del Comune di Gavi 2021 (\u00a315.99, or \u00a313.99 to members, nakendwines.com) \u2013 or the garganega of soave in the Veneto \u2013 the base for the soft pear, scented white flowers and almonds of Inama Soave Classico 2021 (\u00a315.99, or \u00a312.99, as part of a mixed case of six, majestic.co.uk) \u2013 are as a consequence rather less well known than they deserve to be. Certainly, it\u2019s hard to get bored when you can, without taking very much trouble, find a different, delicious Italian wine, made from a different grape variety, for every day of the year.
