"Illustration: Mathilde Vogt/The Guardian", "source": "The Guardian", "photographer": "Mathilde Vogt", "alt": "Illustration of a woman being weighed down under a roll of bills", "mediaId": "07f4e06053b681bd2d490f5671c4faf6e5bffc98", "mediaApiUri": "https://api.media.gutools.co.uk/images/07f4e06053b681bd2d490f5671c4faf6e5bffc98", "suppliersReference": "2023 04 THE GUARDIAN_Mathilde-Vogt (2)", "imageType": "Illustration", "role": "showcase"}}]}, "body": [{"id": "6435f71d8f0885230b2d8f91", "bodyHtml": "<p>On 27 January, I was just under six weeks pregnant. My fertility app \u2013 one of several pinned on my phone\u2019s home screen, I am reluctant to admit \u2013 told me that the embryo growing inside me was the size of a green pea.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/apr/15/abortion-republicans-election-supreme-court\">\u2018They created this\u2019: are Republicans willing to lose elections to retain their abortion stance?</a> </p> </aside>  <p>That morning, I felt both elated and nervous. Between Zoom calls and spurts of distracted writing, I thought about spilling the beans to my sister, but resisted. After two miscarriages, I was wary of sharing the news too early.</p> <p>As noon approached, I started to feel some pangs in my abdomen. At first, I didn\u2019t overthink it: every piece of reproductive literature out there will tell you that cramps, and even light bleeding, are normal during early pregnancy. They subside.</p> <p>For me, they only got worse. The spotting became heavy bleeding, while mild cramps turned into agonizing stabs of pain on my right side.</p> <p>\u201cI think you need to come home,\u201d I texted my husband, who was at work. This was not a normal miscarriage.</p> <p>Mercifully, the nearest emergency department was only a 10-minute drive away. There, the nurse immediately suspected what an ultrasound later confirmed. The pregnancy had been ectopic, meaning it had implanted outside the uterus \u2013 in my case, near the opening of my right fallopian tube, which burst.</p> <p>Because it ruptured so early, my surrounding organs would probably not be damaged. But the pregnancy, as with <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/article/ectopic-pregnancy-symptoms-treatment.html\">all ectopic pregnancies</a>, was not viable. It would need to be surgically aborted, and fast, to stop internal bleeding and prevent infection, both of which can be life-threatening.</p> <p>In simpler terms, I was going to have an abortion and it was going to save my life.</p> <p>I live in California, where access to legal abortion is not in jeopardy, top-quality medical care is plentiful, and lawmakers understand that <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/us-news/2019/nov/29/ohio-extreme-abortion-bill-reimplant-ectopic-pregnancy\">an ectopic pregnancy cannot be re-implanted</a> into the uterus to develop into a healthy baby.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/us-news/2023/apr/13/florida-democrat-lauren-book-fight-six-week-abortion-ban\">\u2018It\u2019s a scary time\u2019: Florida Democrat vows to keep fighting six-week abortion ban</a> </p> </aside>  <p>My ability to receive the abortion I needed was never in question. But I was not exempt from the fact that in the US, receiving abortion care \u2013 or any medical care \u2013 can be extremely expensive. At the end of it all, I was billed over $55,000, a number that felt unfathomable for someone who grew up in Canada, where under a publicly funded health system, an emergency abortion (<a href=\"https://nafcanada.org/abortion-coverage-region/\">or any abortion</a>, for that matter) would yield no cost to an insured patient.</p> <p>***</p> <p>As I was wheeled off to the OR, I was overwhelmed with relief that I did not live in one of the 13 states that have banned abortion since the US supreme court <a href=\"https://reproductiverights.org/case/scotus-mississippi-abortion-ban/\">ruled to overturn Roe v Wade</a> last June. But as the surgeon explained how they would make a tiny incision in my abdomen to remove my mangled tube and the embryo attached to it, I found myself imagining alternate realities \u2013 and was not comforted.</p> <p>Aborting an ectopic pregnancy is necessary to protect the life of the mother. But in states with abortion bans across the country, exemption clauses that are supposed to reflect those complexities are <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2023/01/21/us/abortion-ban-exceptions.html\">failing</a> to protect women who need care, often because wording is too vague to ensure that a doctor will not be <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2022/09/10/us/abortion-bans-medical-care-women.html\">prosecuted</a> for doing their job.</p> <p>In Michigan, a woman received a <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/health/2022/07/16/abortion-miscarriage-ectopic-pregnancy-care/\">life-saving abortion for an ectopic pregnancy</a> after she was denied one in her home state. Another woman in Tennessee was told that the only way she could legally terminate her rare <a href=\"https://www.propublica.org/article/tennessee-abortion-ban-doctors-ectopic-pregnancy\">cesarean scar ectopic pregnancy</a> was with a hysterectomy, which would effectively sterilize her. Unable to travel out of state, she was forced to continue the pregnancy, putting her life in grave danger and risking severe complications for the baby. In California, I retained rights that had been stripped from those women, and that protected me from those unthinkable abuses.</p> <p>However, my state did not shield me from other brutal features of American healthcare. I received the first bill about a week after the procedure. I was sitting on the couch, wearing the green sweatsuit I had lived in for seven days, with my abdomen still wrapped in gauze, when an email notified me that the surgeon\u2019s charges had come through: $5,922. About 10 days after that, the hospital\u2019s bill arrived, listing additional services adding up to $49,209.20.</p> <p>I was shocked. I hadn\u2019t even stayed the night, yet, after my private health insurance plan paid its part, I owed over $8,000 for a procedure without which I might have died.</p> <p>And as it turns out, I\u2019m not alone. One New York woman was charged <a href=\"https://khn.org/news/article/ectopic-pregnancy-new-york-abortion-law/\">$80,000</a> for services related to the termination of an ectopic pregnancy \u2013 again, in a state with liberal abortion laws.</p> <p>Across the board, Americans spend more money per capita on healthcare (<a href=\"https://www.pgpf.org/blog/2022/07/how-does-the-us-healthcare-system-compare-to-other-countries\">$12,318 a head in 2021</a>, to be exact) than their peers in other developed nations, yet experience <a href=\"https://www.commonwealthfund.org/publications/issue-briefs/2020/jan/us-health-care-global-perspective-2019\">worse health outcomes</a>. This can be attributed to several factors. For one, US healthcare is <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5394555/\">profit-motivated</a>, with little stopping providers and pharmaceutical companies from gouging patients. But moreover, its model is made up of a series of disconnected and underregulated systems, leading to <a href=\"https://www.harvardmagazine.com/2020/05/feature-forum-costliest-health-care\">high administrative costs</a> that are passed on to patients.</p> <p>Of course, high costs aren\u2019t exclusive to abortion care. At the hospital I visited, an emergency appendectomy would, in theory, cost the same as my surgery. But a gender bias is baked into the system, especially when it comes to reproductive care.</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--showcase\"> <blockquote> <p>Women pay significantly more for healthcare than men between their 20s and 40s, largely due to reproductive care and even when they have adequate coverage</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p><a href=\"https://www.hsph.harvard.edu/news/hsph-in-the-news/calculating-cost-womens-reproductive-health-care/\">Studies</a> have shown that women pay <a href=\"https://www.healthsystemtracker.org/chart-collection/health-expenditures-vary-across-population/#Average%20individual%20health%20spending,%202019%C2%A0\">significantly more</a> for healthcare than men between their 20s and 40s, largely due to reproductive care and even when they have adequate coverage. This is despite the fact that covering the costs of sexual and reproductive care is known to not only benefit patients\u2019 health, but also save the overall system <a href=\"https://www.guttmacher.org/report/adding-it-costs-and-benefits-investing-sexual-and-reproductive-health-2014\">a good deal of money</a> \u2013 as encouraging patients to seek early or preventive care typically reduces complications and lowers costs down the line.</p> <p>And abortion bans are making it much worse: not only do they force many women to travel long distances for care, which is expensive, but the extra time required to get care also makes it more complex and, as a result, costlier.</p> <p>***</p> <p>Even in non-emergency situations, abortions in the US are expensive. Typically, where abortion care is legal, it costs between several hundred and several thousand dollars. At a non-profit like Planned Parenthood, abortion costs range from $550-800. At a hospital or private clinic, price tags can be much higher.</p> <p>\u201cCost for an abortion has always been a big hurdle for people who are trying to just get the care that they need,\u201d said Dailienis Garcia, senior director of abortion access, funding and navigation for Planned Parenthood. She explained that even in states that are abortion-friendly, requirements pushed by actors hostile to abortion, like multiple in-person appointments or medically unnecessary ultrasounds, have historically driven up prices.</p> <p>Among the remaining states with unrestricted access, just <a href=\"https://www.kff.org/medicaid/state-indicator/abortion-under-medicaid/?currentTimeframe=0&amp;sortModel=%7B%22colId%22:%22Location%22,%22sort%22:%22asc%22%7D\">16</a> cover abortion through state Medicaid programs, an enduring legacy of the <a href=\"https://www.plannedparenthoodaction.org/issues/abortion/federal-and-state-bans-and-restrictions-abortion/hyde-amendment\">1976 Hyde Amendment</a> that blocked the use of federal Medicaid funding for abortion coverage. As a result, many low-income patients have to pay out of pocket.</p> <p>\u201cThe impact \u2026 is felt largely by Black, Latino and Indigenous people, immigrants, people living with low incomes and people in rural areas,\u201d Garcia said. \u201cWealthy individuals will always be able to access abortions.\u201d</p> <p>That disparity in access extends to other aspects of reproductive care, too. For instance, although <a href=\"https://www.npr.org/sections/health-shots/2021/07/21/1018483557/contraception-is-free-to-women-except-when-its-not\">federal law requires</a> insurance plans to cover contraception, patients pay high fees for birth control pills or IUD insertions. Fertility care \u2013including fertility testing and <a href=\"https://calmatters.org/health/2022/05/fertility-treatment-costs-california/\">assisted reproductive technology</a> like IUI or IVF \u2013 is rarely covered by insurance plans and out-of-pocket charges in the US often reach into the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/us-news/2018/jul/30/ivf-fertility-treatment-lets-talk-about-costs-insurance-coverage\">tens of thousands of dollars</a>. (In Europe, by way of contrast, <a href=\"https://www.europarl.europa.eu/cmsdata/226090/No_12_September_2016_in_vitro_fertilisation.pdf\">most countries heavily subsidize fertility treatments</a>.) In all of these realms, people with lower incomes <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/lifeandstyle/2018/nov/28/who-can-afford-ivf-treatments-fertility-class\">face barriers to care</a> that do not exist for the wealthy in America.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"ebd9d18f2ad5de4b325f9de9aed0072b53284aae\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/ebd9d18f2ad5de4b325f9de9aed0072b53284aae/0_0_1500_900/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Woman sitting at table looking at hospital bill. \" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u2018In all of these realms, people with lower incomes face barriers to care that do not exist for the wealthy in America.\u2019</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Illustration: Mathilde Vogt/The Guardian</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>But reproductive care doesn\u2019t need to \u2013 and shouldn\u2019t \u2013 cost as much as it does. Across the border, in Canada, physician and hospital services cost a fraction of what they do here in the US.</p> <p>For a straightforward abortion that takes place before 14 weeks of gestation, a physician in Ontario can bill <a href=\"https://www.health.gov.on.ca/en/pro/programs/ohip/sob/\">C$112.40</a> (US$83). In California, <a href=\"https://files.medi-cal.ca.gov/rates/rates_download.aspx\">prices start at $250</a> for patients insured by Medicaid.</p> <p>Dr Alisha Olsthoorn, an OB-GYN at Toronto\u2019s Mount Sinai hospital, said that she will typically bill C$307 (about US$225) for a laparoscopic salpingectomy \u2013 the same surgery that cost me $5,900 in Berkeley.</p> <p>\u201cIf it\u2019s the middle of the night, I\u2019d bill more,\u201d said Olsthoorn, explaining that she would add a 75% surcharge for an after-hours surgery.</p> <p>At Scarborough Health Network, also in Toronto, hospital costs for an ectopic pregnancy needing a surgical abortion average C$5,608.00 and include an overnight stay (which was not among the amenities provided in my $49k hospital bill).</p> <p>And even those costs are covered by the government \u2013 not the patient.</p> <p>So why was it that at Sutter, my hospital charges were nearly 10 times that amount?</p> <p>\u201cThey basically make it up,\u201d said Karen Pollitz, senior fellow and co-director of the program on patient and consumer protections in health insurance for Kaiser Family Foundation, who added that \u201cbilled amounts are whatever the doc and the hospital feel like billing you\u201d.</p> <p>As a result, fees in the US can be not only <a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/hospital-prices-arbitrary-healthcare-medical-bills-insurance-11635428943\">arbitrary</a>, but also grossly inflated, and rarely reflect the actual costs of services \u2013 even when certain hospitals are ostensibly <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2023/01/25/podcasts/the-daily/nonprofit-hospitals-investigation.html\">non-profit</a>.</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--inline\"> <blockquote> <p>When you have to deal with this is often when you\u2019re least equipped to do so because you\u2019re going through a scary health crisis.</p> <footer> <cite>Karen Pollitz</cite> </footer> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>Pollitz went on to say that while insurance groups are supposed to act as fiscal gatekeepers that keep tabs on how much a hospital charges subscribers, unfair billing practices often go unnoticed. Most often, patients must shoulder the burden of either paying or fighting for charges to be lowered \u2013 something that takes time and energy.</p> <p>\u201cWhen you have to deal with this is often when you\u2019re least equipped to do so because you\u2019re going through a scary health crisis \u2026 you\u2019re sick or sad because you lost a pregnancy,\u201d said Pollitz. \u201cIt\u2019s just not nice to do to sick people.\u201d</p> <p><strong>***</strong></p> <p>Since 1 January 2021, hospitals across the country have had to comply with the federal government\u2019s <a href=\"https://www.cms.gov/hospital-price-transparencynew%20york%20tim\">hospital price transparency</a> rule, which requires they make their pricing for services offered publicly available. The rule has exposed just <a href=\"https://www.healthsystemtracker.org/brief/price-transparency-and-variation-in-u-s-health-services/\">how widely costs for a single procedure can vary</a> from place to place, insurer to insurer and patient to patient. The <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2021/08/22/upshot/hospital-prices.html\">costs for an MRI at Mass General, for example, range from $877 to over $4,000</a>, depending on a patient\u2019s insurance plan.</p> <p>After digesting the scale of the charges I had received from Sutter Health, a hospital behemoth that <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/10/03/health/sutter-hospitals-medical-bills.html\">has been accused</a> of inflating costs in the past, I began to dig. My hospital listed \u201cAll services for surgery related to an ectopic pregnancy\u201d as ranging from $7,500 and nearly $37,000 for people with Blue Shield insurance.</p> <p>When I asked Pollitz how that squares with my $50,000 in hospital charges, she explained that without any federal oversight, hospitals have no incentive to stick to the charges they post online.</p> <p>\u201cThey\u2019re just a horrible mess,\u201d Pollitz said of the transparency data, explaining that hospitals do not clarify whether listed prices are \u201ccomprehensive\u201d or \u201cpartial\u201d. Indeed, when I called to contest the cost, a Sutter billing representative said that the \u201call services\u201d price listed on their website \u201cmay just be part of the procedure\u201d and probably didn\u2019t include \u201cpharmacy, layups, IVs, emergency room and anesthesia\u201d charges. </p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"f45b130b16b777e6b172d12e98c07bc962ecfa08\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/f45b130b16b777e6b172d12e98c07bc962ecfa08/0_0_5472_3648/1000.jpg\" alt=\"People participate in a pro-choice rally in Washington DC in January 2023.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"667\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">People participate in a pro-choice rally in Washington DC in January 2023.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Aurora Samperio/NurPhoto/Rex/Shutterstock</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>In a statement to the Guardian, Sutter Health seemed to suggest it was impossible to set prices in advance for emergencies. \u201cSutter and commercial health insurance companies typically do not have set negotiated rates for services provided in emergency care settings. Generally, this is because emergency services and individual patient circumstances can vary widely,\u201d said a spokesperson. \u201cWe always listen to patient feedback and continually review our processes to find ways to improve the patient experience and make the billing process more clear.\u201d</p> <p>But patients do have options for recourse when bills are unjustly high. A person can ask the hospital for charges to be reviewed and for clear descriptions of items listed as \u201cmiscellaneous\u201d. (Just note that it doesn\u2019t always work. After formally reviewing my bill, Sutter notified me that the charges were, in their eyes, accurate.)</p> <p>Contacting one\u2019s insurance plan is another important step, said Pollitz, as lower overall charges also benefit insurers, who are usually responsible for paying a percentage of the bill. But filing a grievance can be frustrating, time-consuming and re-traumatizing. When I called my insurance plan\u2019s customer service line to inquire about the charges, I was told it was my job to research costs before getting surgery, and that it wasn\u2019t their fault that I had failed to do so.</p> <p>\u201cDid you get the price for your surgery approved in advance?\u201d a representative from Blue Shield California asked me over the phone about my emergency surgical abortion.</p> <p>The question rang as absurd. Reproductive care is not predictable and if a patient delays seeking emergency care because they are nervous about costs, their condition could become more dangerous and expensive. Prohibitive costs, therefore, not only discourage people from seeking care, but also help contribute to the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/mar/20/us-maternal-mortality-rate-climbing-health-crisis#:~:text=America%20is%20in%20a,of%20maternal%20causes%20that%20year.\">US\u2019s exceptionally high maternal mortality rates</a>.</p> <p>\u201cNo, I didn\u2019t plan to have an ectopic pregnancy,\u201d I told the representative.</p> <p>\u201cThen this charge is correct,\u201d she responded. If only I had been able to plan the moment my tube would rupture, I could have saved thousands of dollars.</p>", "bodyTextSummary": "On 27 January, I was just under six weeks pregnant. My fertility app \u2013 one of several pinned on my phone\u2019s home screen, I am reluctant to admit \u2013 told me that the embryo growing inside me was the size of a green pea.\nThat morning, I felt both elated and nervous. Between Zoom calls and spurts of distracted writing, I thought about spilling the beans to my sister, but resisted. After two miscarriages, I was wary of sharing the news too early. As noon approached, I started to feel some pangs in my abdomen. At first, I didn\u2019t overthink it: every piece of reproductive literature out there will tell you that cramps, and even light bleeding, are normal during early pregnancy. They subside. For me, they only got worse. The spotting became heavy bleeding, while mild cramps turned into agonizing stabs of pain on my right side. \u201cI think you need to come home,\u201d I texted my husband, who was at work. This was not a normal miscarriage. Mercifully, the nearest emergency department was only a 10-minute drive away. There, the nurse immediately suspected what an ultrasound later confirmed. The pregnancy had been ectopic, meaning it had implanted outside the uterus \u2013 in my case, near the opening of my right fallopian tube, which burst. Because it ruptured so early, my surrounding organs would probably not be damaged. But the pregnancy, as with all ectopic pregnancies, was not viable. It would need to be surgically aborted, and fast, to stop internal bleeding and prevent infection, both of which can be life-threatening. In simpler terms, I was going to have an abortion and it was going to save my life. I live in California, where access to legal abortion is not in jeopardy, top-quality medical care is plentiful, and lawmakers understand that an ectopic pregnancy cannot be re-implanted into the uterus to develop into a healthy baby.\nMy ability to receive the abortion I needed was never in question. But I was not exempt from the fact that in the US, receiving abortion care \u2013 or any medical care \u2013 can be extremely expensive. At the end of it all, I was billed over $55,000, a number that felt unfathomable for someone who grew up in Canada, where under a publicly funded health system, an emergency abortion (or any abortion, for that matter) would yield no cost to an insured patient. *** As I was wheeled off to the OR, I was overwhelmed with relief that I did not live in one of the 13 states that have banned abortion since the US supreme court ruled to overturn Roe v Wade last June. But as the surgeon explained how they would make a tiny incision in my abdomen to remove my mangled tube and the embryo attached to it, I found myself imagining alternate realities \u2013 and was not comforted. Aborting an ectopic pregnancy is necessary to protect the life of the mother. But in states with abortion bans across the country, exemption clauses that are supposed to reflect those complexities are failing to protect women who need care, often because wording is too vague to ensure that a doctor will not be prosecuted for doing their job. In Michigan, a woman received a life-saving abortion for an ectopic pregnancy after she was denied one in her home state. Another woman in Tennessee was told that the only way she could legally terminate her rare cesarean scar ectopic pregnancy was with a hysterectomy, which would effectively sterilize her. Unable to travel out of state, she was forced to continue the pregnancy, putting her life in grave danger and risking severe complications for the baby. In California, I retained rights that had been stripped from those women, and that protected me from those unthinkable abuses. However, my state did not shield me from other brutal features of American healthcare. I received the first bill about a week after the procedure. I was sitting on the couch, wearing the green sweatsuit I had lived in for seven days, with my abdomen still wrapped in gauze, when an email notified me that the surgeon\u2019s charges had come through: $5,922. About 10 days after that, the hospital\u2019s bill arrived, listing additional services adding up to $49,209.20. I was shocked. I hadn\u2019t even stayed the night, yet, after my private health insurance plan paid its part, I owed over $8,000 for a procedure without which I might have died. And as it turns out, I\u2019m not alone. One New York woman was charged $80,000 for services related to the termination of an ectopic pregnancy \u2013 again, in a state with liberal abortion laws. Across the board, Americans spend more money per capita on healthcare ($12,318 a head in 2021, to be exact) than their peers in other developed nations, yet experience worse health outcomes. This can be attributed to several factors. For one, US healthcare is profit-motivated, with little stopping providers and pharmaceutical companies from gouging patients. But moreover, its model is made up of a series of disconnected and underregulated systems, leading to high administrative costs that are passed on to patients. Of course, high costs aren\u2019t exclusive to abortion care. At the hospital I visited, an emergency appendectomy would, in theory, cost the same as my surgery. But a gender bias is baked into the system, especially when it comes to reproductive care.\nStudies have shown that women pay significantly more for healthcare than men between their 20s and 40s, largely due to reproductive care and even when they have adequate coverage. This is despite the fact that covering the costs of sexual and reproductive care is known to not only benefit patients\u2019 health, but also save the overall system a good deal of money \u2013 as encouraging patients to seek early or preventive care typically reduces complications and lowers costs down the line. And abortion bans are making it much worse: not only do they force many women to travel long distances for care, which is expensive, but the extra time required to get care also makes it more complex and, as a result, costlier. *** Even in non-emergency situations, abortions in the US are expensive. Typically, where abortion care is legal, it costs between several hundred and several thousand dollars. At a non-profit like Planned Parenthood, abortion costs range from $550-800. At a hospital or private clinic, price tags can be much higher. \u201cCost for an abortion has always been a big hurdle for people who are trying to just get the care that they need,\u201d said Dailienis Garcia, senior director of abortion access, funding and navigation for Planned Parenthood. She explained that even in states that are abortion-friendly, requirements pushed by actors hostile to abortion, like multiple in-person appointments or medically unnecessary ultrasounds, have historically driven up prices. Among the remaining states with unrestricted access, just 16 cover abortion through state Medicaid programs, an enduring legacy of the 1976 Hyde Amendment that blocked the use of federal Medicaid funding for abortion coverage. As a result, many low-income patients have to pay out of pocket. \u201cThe impact \u2026 is felt largely by Black, Latino and Indigenous people, immigrants, people living with low incomes and people in rural areas,\u201d Garcia said. \u201cWealthy individuals will always be able to access abortions.\u201d That disparity in access extends to other aspects of reproductive care, too. For instance, although federal law requires insurance plans to cover contraception, patients pay high fees for birth control pills or IUD insertions. Fertility care \u2013including fertility testing and assisted reproductive technology like IUI or IVF \u2013 is rarely covered by insurance plans and out-of-pocket charges in the US often reach into the tens of thousands of dollars. (In Europe, by way of contrast, most countries heavily subsidize fertility treatments.) In all of these realms, people with lower incomes face barriers to care that do not exist for the wealthy in America.\nBut reproductive care doesn\u2019t need to \u2013 and shouldn\u2019t \u2013 cost as much as it does. Across the border, in Canada, physician and hospital services cost a fraction of what they do here in the US. For a straightforward abortion that takes place before 14 weeks of gestation, a physician in Ontario can bill C$112.40 (US$83). In California, prices start at $250 for patients insured by Medicaid. Dr Alisha Olsthoorn, an OB-GYN at Toronto\u2019s Mount Sinai hospital, said that she will typically bill C$307 (about US$225) for a laparoscopic salpingectomy \u2013 the same surgery that cost me $5,900 in Berkeley. \u201cIf it\u2019s the middle of the night, I\u2019d bill more,\u201d said Olsthoorn, explaining that she would add a 75% surcharge for an after-hours surgery. At Scarborough Health Network, also in Toronto, hospital costs for an ectopic pregnancy needing a surgical abortion average C$5,608.00 and include an overnight stay (which was not among the amenities provided in my $49k hospital bill). And even those costs are covered by the government \u2013 not the patient. So why was it that at Sutter, my hospital charges were nearly 10 times that amount? \u201cThey basically make it up,\u201d said Karen Pollitz, senior fellow and co-director of the program on patient and consumer protections in health insurance for Kaiser Family Foundation, who added that \u201cbilled amounts are whatever the doc and the hospital feel like billing you\u201d. As a result, fees in the US can be not only arbitrary, but also grossly inflated, and rarely reflect the actual costs of services \u2013 even when certain hospitals are ostensibly non-profit.\nPollitz went on to say that while insurance groups are supposed to act as fiscal gatekeepers that keep tabs on how much a hospital charges subscribers, unfair billing practices often go unnoticed. Most often, patients must shoulder the burden of either paying or fighting for charges to be lowered \u2013 something that takes time and energy. \u201cWhen you have to deal with this is often when you\u2019re least equipped to do so because you\u2019re going through a scary health crisis \u2026 you\u2019re sick or sad because you lost a pregnancy,\u201d said Pollitz. \u201cIt\u2019s just not nice to do to sick people.\u201d *** Since 1 January 2021, hospitals across the country have had to comply with the federal government\u2019s hospital price transparency rule, which requires they make their pricing for services offered publicly available. The rule has exposed just how widely costs for a single procedure can vary from place to place, insurer to insurer and patient to patient. The costs for an MRI at Mass General, for example, range from $877 to over $4,000, depending on a patient\u2019s insurance plan. After digesting the scale of the charges I had received from Sutter Health, a hospital behemoth that has been accused of inflating costs in the past, I began to dig. My hospital listed \u201cAll services for surgery related to an ectopic pregnancy\u201d as ranging from $7,500 and nearly $37,000 for people with Blue Shield insurance. When I asked Pollitz how that squares with my $50,000 in hospital charges, she explained that without any federal oversight, hospitals have no incentive to stick to the charges they post online. \u201cThey\u2019re just a horrible mess,\u201d Pollitz said of the transparency data, explaining that hospitals do not clarify whether listed prices are \u201ccomprehensive\u201d or \u201cpartial\u201d. Indeed, when I called to contest the cost, a Sutter billing representative said that the \u201call services\u201d price listed on their website \u201cmay just be part of the procedure\u201d and probably didn\u2019t include \u201cpharmacy, layups, IVs, emergency room and anesthesia\u201d charges.\nIn a statement to the Guardian, Sutter Health seemed to suggest it was impossible to set prices in advance for emergencies. \u201cSutter and commercial health insurance companies typically do not have set negotiated rates for services provided in emergency care settings. Generally, this is because emergency services and individual patient circumstances can vary widely,\u201d said a spokesperson. \u201cWe always listen to patient feedback and continually review our processes to find ways to improve the patient experience and make the billing process more clear.\u201d But patients do have options for recourse when bills are unjustly high. A person can ask the hospital for charges to be reviewed and for clear descriptions of items listed as \u201cmiscellaneous\u201d. (Just note that it doesn\u2019t always work. After formally reviewing my bill, Sutter notified me that the charges were, in their eyes, accurate.) Contacting one\u2019s insurance plan is another important step, said Pollitz, as lower overall charges also benefit insurers, who are usually responsible for paying a percentage of the bill. But filing a grievance can be frustrating, time-consuming and re-traumatizing. When I called my insurance plan\u2019s customer service line to inquire about the charges, I was told it was my job to research costs before getting surgery, and that it wasn\u2019t their fault that I had failed to do so. \u201cDid you get the price for your surgery approved in advance?\u201d a representative from Blue Shield California asked me over the phone about my emergency surgical abortion. The question rang as absurd. Reproductive care is not predictable and if a patient delays seeking emergency care because they are nervous about costs, their condition could become more dangerous and expensive. Prohibitive costs, therefore, not only discourage people from seeking care, but also help contribute to the US\u2019s exceptionally high maternal mortality rates. \u201cNo, I didn\u2019t plan to have an ectopic pregnancy,\u201d I told the representative. \u201cThen this charge is correct,\u201d she responded. If only I had been able to plan the moment my tube would rupture, I could have saved thousands of dollars.", "attributes": {}, "published": true, "createdDate": "2023-04-16T05:00:02Z", "firstPublishedDate": "2023-04-16T05:00:02Z", "publishedDate": "2023-04-17T17:32:14Z", "lastModifiedDate": "2023-04-17T17:32:14Z", "contributors": [], "elements": [{"type": "text", "assets": [], "textTypeData": {"html": "<p>On 27 January, I was just under six weeks pregnant. My fertility app \u2013 one of several pinned on my phone\u2019s home screen, I am reluctant to admit \u2013 told me that the embryo growing inside me was the size of a green pea.</p>"}}, {"type": "rich-link", "assets": [], "richLinkTypeData": {"url": "https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/apr/15/abortion-republicans-election-supreme-court", "originalUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/world/2023/apr/15/abortion-republicans-election-supreme-court", "linkText": "\u2018They created this\u2019: are Republicans willing to lose elections to retain their abortion stance?", "linkPrefix": "Related: ", "role": "thumbnail"}}, {"type": "text", "assets": [], "textTypeData": {"html": "<p>That morning, I felt both elated and nervous. Between Zoom calls and spurts of distracted writing, I thought about spilling the beans to my sister, but resisted. After two miscarriages, I was wary of sharing the news too early.</p> \n<p>As noon approached, I started to feel some pangs in my abdomen. At first, I didn\u2019t overthink it: every piece of reproductive literature out there will tell you that cramps, and even light bleeding, are normal during early pregnancy. They subside.</p> \n<p>For me, they only got worse. The spotting became heavy bleeding, while mild cramps turned into agonizing stabs of pain on my right side.</p> \n<p>\u201cI think you need to come home,\u201d I texted my husband, who was at work. This was not a normal miscarriage.</p> \n<p>Mercifully, the nearest emergency department was only a 10-minute drive away. There, the nurse immediately suspected what an ultrasound later confirmed. The pregnancy had been ectopic, meaning it had implanted outside the uterus \u2013 in my case, near the opening of my right fallopian tube, which burst.</p> \n<p>Because it ruptured so early, my surrounding organs would probably not be damaged. But the pregnancy, as with <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/article/ectopic-pregnancy-symptoms-treatment.html\">all ectopic pregnancies</a>, was not viable. It would need to be surgically aborted, and fast, to stop internal bleeding and prevent infection, both of which can be life-threatening.</p> \n<p>In simpler terms, I was going to have an abortion and it was going to save my life.</p> \n<p>I live in California, where access to legal abortion is not in jeopardy, top-quality medical care is plentiful, and lawmakers understand that <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/us-news/2019/nov/29/ohio-extreme-abortion-bill-reimplant-ectopic-pregnancy\">an ectopic pregnancy cannot be re-implanted</a> into the uterus to develop into a healthy baby.</p>"}}, {"type": "rich-link", "assets": [], "richLinkTypeData": {"url": "https://www.theguardian.com/us-news/2023/apr/13/florida-democrat-lauren-book-fight-six-week-abortion-ban", "originalUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/us-news/2023/apr/13/florida-democrat-lauren-book-fight-six-week-abortion-ban", "linkText": "\u2018It\u2019s a scary time\u2019: Florida Democrat vows to keep fighting six-week abortion ban", "linkPrefix": "Related: ", "role": "thumbnail"}}, {"type": "text", "assets": [], "textTypeData": {"html": "<p>My ability to receive the abortion I needed was never in question. But I was not exempt from the fact that in the US, receiving abortion care \u2013 or any medical care \u2013 can be extremely expensive. At the end of it all, I was billed over $55,000, a number that felt unfathomable for someone who grew up in Canada, where under a publicly funded health system, an emergency abortion (<a href=\"https://nafcanada.org/abortion-coverage-region/\">or any abortion</a>, for that matter) would yield no cost to an insured patient.</p> \n<p>***</p> \n<p>As I was wheeled off to the OR, I was overwhelmed with relief that I did not live in one of the 13 states that have banned abortion since the US supreme court <a href=\"https://reproductiverights.org/case/scotus-mississippi-abortion-ban/\">ruled to overturn Roe v Wade</a> last June. But as the surgeon explained how they would make a tiny incision in my abdomen to remove my mangled tube and the embryo attached to it, I found myself imagining alternate realities \u2013 and was not comforted.</p> \n<p>Aborting an ectopic pregnancy is necessary to protect the life of the mother. But in states with abortion bans across the country, exemption clauses that are supposed to reflect those complexities are <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2023/01/21/us/abortion-ban-exceptions.html\">failing</a> to protect women who need care, often because wording is too vague to ensure that a doctor will not be <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2022/09/10/us/abortion-bans-medical-care-women.html\">prosecuted</a> for doing their job.</p> \n<p>In Michigan, a woman received a <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/health/2022/07/16/abortion-miscarriage-ectopic-pregnancy-care/\">life-saving abortion for an ectopic pregnancy</a> after she was denied one in her home state. Another woman in Tennessee was told that the only way she could legally terminate her rare <a href=\"https://www.propublica.org/article/tennessee-abortion-ban-doctors-ectopic-pregnancy\">cesarean scar ectopic pregnancy</a> was with a hysterectomy, which would effectively sterilize her. Unable to travel out of state, she was forced to continue the pregnancy, putting her life in grave danger and risking severe complications for the baby. In California, I retained rights that had been stripped from those women, and that protected me from those unthinkable abuses.</p> \n<p>However, my state did not shield me from other brutal features of American healthcare. I received the first bill about a week after the procedure. I was sitting on the couch, wearing the green sweatsuit I had lived in for seven days, with my abdomen still wrapped in gauze, when an email notified me that the surgeon\u2019s charges had come through: $5,922. About 10 days after that, the hospital\u2019s bill arrived, listing additional services adding up to $49,209.20.</p> \n<p>I was shocked. I hadn\u2019t even stayed the night, yet, after my private health insurance plan paid its part, I owed over $8,000 for a procedure without which I might have died.</p> \n<p>And as it turns out, I\u2019m not alone. One New York woman was charged <a href=\"https://khn.org/news/article/ectopic-pregnancy-new-york-abortion-law/\">$80,000</a> for services related to the termination of an ectopic pregnancy \u2013 again, in a state with liberal abortion laws.</p> \n<p>Across the board, Americans spend more money per capita on healthcare (<a href=\"https://www.pgpf.org/blog/2022/07/how-does-the-us-healthcare-system-compare-to-other-countries\">$12,318 a head in 2021</a>, to be exact) than their peers in other developed nations, yet experience <a href=\"https://www.commonwealthfund.org/publications/issue-briefs/2020/jan/us-health-care-global-perspective-2019\">worse health outcomes</a>. This can be attributed to several factors. For one, US healthcare is <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5394555/\">profit-motivated</a>, with little stopping providers and pharmaceutical companies from gouging patients. But moreover, its model is made up of a series of disconnected and underregulated systems, leading to <a href=\"https://www.harvardmagazine.com/2020/05/feature-forum-costliest-health-care\">high administrative costs</a> that are passed on to patients.</p> \n<p>Of course, high costs aren\u2019t exclusive to abortion care. At the hospital I visited, an emergency appendectomy would, in theory, cost the same as my surgery. But a gender bias is baked into the system, especially when it comes to reproductive care.</p>"}}, {"type": "pullquote", "assets": [], "pullquoteTypeData": {"html": "Women pay significantly more for healthcare than men between their 20s and 40s, largely due to reproductive care and even when they have adequate coverage", "role": "showcase"}}, {"type": "text", "assets": [], "textTypeData": {"html": "<p><a href=\"https://www.hsph.harvard.edu/news/hsph-in-the-news/calculating-cost-womens-reproductive-health-care/\">Studies</a> have shown that women pay <a href=\"https://www.healthsystemtracker.org/chart-collection/health-expenditures-vary-across-population/#Average%20individual%20health%20spending,%202019%C2%A0\">significantly more</a> for healthcare than men between their 20s and 40s, largely due to reproductive care and even when they have adequate coverage. This is despite the fact that covering the costs of sexual and reproductive care is known to not only benefit patients\u2019 health, but also save the overall system <a href=\"https://www.guttmacher.org/report/adding-it-costs-and-benefits-investing-sexual-and-reproductive-health-2014\">a good deal of money</a> \u2013 as encouraging patients to seek early or preventive care typically reduces complications and lowers costs down the line.</p> \n<p>And abortion bans are making it much worse: not only do they force many women to travel long distances for care, which is expensive, but the extra time required to get care also makes it more complex and, as a result, costlier.</p> \n<p>***</p> \n<p>Even in non-emergency situations, abortions in the US are expensive. {"html": "<p>In a statement to the Guardian, Sutter Health seemed to suggest it was impossible to set prices in advance for emergencies. \u201cSutter and commercial health insurance companies typically do not have set negotiated rates for services provided in emergency care settings. Generally, this is because emergency services and individual patient circumstances can vary widely,\u201d said a spokesperson. \u201cWe always listen to patient feedback and continually review our processes to find ways to improve the patient experience and make the billing process more clear.\u201d</p> \n<p>But patients do have options for recourse when bills are unjustly high. A person can ask the hospital for charges to be reviewed and for clear descriptions of items listed as \u201cmiscellaneous\u201d. (Just note that it doesn\u2019t always work. After formally reviewing my bill, Sutter notified me that the charges were, in their eyes, accurate.)</p> \n<p>Contacting one\u2019s insurance plan is another important step, said Pollitz, as lower overall charges also benefit insurers, who are usually responsible for paying a percentage of the bill. But filing a grievance can be frustrating, time-consuming and re-traumatizing. When I called my insurance plan\u2019s customer service line to inquire about the charges, I was told it was my job to research costs before getting surgery, and that it wasn\u2019t their fault that I had failed to do so.</p> \n<p>\u201cDid you get the price for your surgery approved in advance?\u201d a representative from Blue Shield California asked me over the phone about my emergency surgical abortion.</p> \n<p>The question rang as absurd. Reproductive care is not predictable and if a patient delays seeking emergency care because they are nervous about costs, their condition could become more dangerous and expensive. Prohibitive costs, therefore, not only discourage people from seeking care, but also help contribute to the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2023/mar/20/us-maternal-mortality-rate-climbing-health-crisis#:~:text=America%20is%20in%20a,of%20maternal%20causes%20that%20year.\">US\u2019s exceptionally high maternal mortality rates</a>.</p> \n<p>\u201cNo, I didn\u2019t plan to have an ectopic pregnancy,\u201d I told the representative.</p> \n<p>\u201cThen this charge is correct,\u201d she responded. Tadpole tails are wriggling in the ponds. Our broad beans have burst through, happy and healthy, short rows of crimson-flowering and an old-school hanging variety new to us.</p> <p>We have replanted some of the extravagant, luxuriant poppy leaf. Dotted through the plot now, here and there. The last of the old hazel poles are up and climbing Franchi yellow and blue beans, plus Cherokee Trail of Tears have been sown at their base.</p> <p>Our summer planting starts with the structures or at least a clearer idea of which hazel wigwams will go where. New sweetpeas will arrive any day. The seedlings are currently sprouting in <a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/508184829230229/photos/a.2737279329654090/4859618164086852/?type=3\">Jane Scotter\u2019s Fern Verrow greenhouse</a> to be dropped off when they are grown. We will be sowing morning glory from the <a href=\"https://seedcooperative.org.uk/collections/annual-flowers/products/flower-ipomoearubro-coerulea\">Seed Co-operative</a> when the sweet peas tire.</p> <p>I make new seed rows parallel with the path and sow dill tetra (I love the leaf, though I\u2019m also obsessed with its yellow flower). The other row is a spring salad mix to be eaten young.</p> <p>I add a longer strip of coriander at a right angle to these. Sown from last year\u2019s saved seed. It is another herb flower I cannot resist since I first saw it shimmer in a vegetable garden in northern India.</p> <p>I scatter burgundy amaranth in among the pathside calendula. It seems we grow more flowers than before. "https://content.guardianapis.com/uk-news/2023/apr/16/our-identity-is-being-stolen-survivors-plead-for-halt-to-dramas-about-grenfell", "fields": {"headline": "\u2018Our identity is being stolen\u2019: survivors plead for halt to dramas about Grenfell", "standfirst": "<p>Residents and members of bereaved families call on BBC and National Theatre to scrap productions</p>", "trailText": "Residents and members of bereaved families call on BBC and National Theatre to scrap productions", "byline": "Mark Townsend and Vanessa Thorpe", "main": "<figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"4078cc491d24eda900d14bbd7df216620433201e\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/4078cc491d24eda900d14bbd7df216620433201e/0_283_5976_3586/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Doves being released in north Kensington to commemorate the first anniversary of the fire in 2018.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Doves being released in north Kensington to commemorate the first anniversary of the fire in 2018.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Sean Smith/The Guardian</span> </figcaption> </figure>", "body": "<p>The BBC and the National Theatre are facing rising hostility over two high-profile productions about the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/grenfell-tower-fire\" title=\"\">Grenfell Tower fire disaster,</a> with survivors and bereaved family members urging both institutions to drop their plans.</p> <p>Some have even threatened to disrupt BBC attempts to film near the tower for its series, <em>Grenfell</em>, which will dramatise the tragic events around the night of 14 June 2017 in west London, when 72 people died as a result of the blaze. Others claim the National Theatre is stealing their experiences, despite its play being based on survivors\u2019 first-hand accounts. A petition calling on the BBC to scrap its series has so far attracted more than 50,000 signatures.</p> <p>Nabil Choucair, who lost his mother, sister, brother-in-law and three nieces in the fire, said: \u201cOur identity is being stolen. They don\u2019t listen to us \u2013 and now they want to do a play? A series? Who asked for this?\u201d</p> <p>Both the upcoming play and the planned TV drama are to be based on lengthy periods of research conducted locally and will be constructed from the testimony of many of those closest to the tragedy.</p> <p>But the strength of local opposition exposes how raw feelings remain around ownership of the truth about the fire, and the high level of suspicion about even well-intended interventions from national institutions.</p> <p>Maryam Adam, who escaped from the burning tower while three months pregnant, said she felt blindsided by the plans. \u201cWho are they talking to? Who gives them permission to do these things and then tell us after? Before you do this sort of thing, you should get our permission, because this is our pain, our story,\u201d said Adam, who gave <a href=\"https://www.grenfelltowerinquiry.org.uk/evidence/maryam-yusuf-adam-read-evidence\" title=\"\">evidence</a> to the public inquiry into the disaster.</p> <p>David O\u2019Connell, a leaseholder on the Lancaster West estate, which includes Grenfell, voiced concerns that both productions were coming too soon to be seen as definitive. The inquiry into the tragedy closed last year, and its eagerly awaited final report, likely to run into thousands of pages, is yet to be published.</p> <p>O\u2019Connell said: \u201cThe BBC series is useless unless it shows something new and revelatory. The proper way you tell the story happens 20 years after, when all the hidden stuff is out.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"612662a24fc63c8da50e698fa06825e0551245bd\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/612662a24fc63c8da50e698fa06825e0551245bd/0_67_3500_2100/1000.jpg\" alt=\"The remains of Grenfell Tower completely shrouded in white tarpaulins with a green heart logo at the top of the covering, seen from the air in its surroundings\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Grenfell Tower in 2018.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Rick Findler/PA</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>\u201cThere\u2019s no way people want to be part of this BBC series. It\u2019s a fictionalised version of my lived experience. For us it\u2019s repetitive and triggering.\u201d</p> <p>Frank, the next of kin of two people who died in the fire, said that the way in which some people had learned about the planned BBC series and National Theatre production had infuriated many. \u201cThe BBC series was announced through a newspaper report, posted in one of our WhatsApp groups, and the play was a letter in the mail.\u201d</p> <p>He warned that the backlash would hamper any attempts to film by the BBC in the area of north Kensington where the remains of Grenfell still stand. \u201cIt will be very hard for the BBC to do any filming here without cooperation \u2013 and we won\u2019t cooperate because people are very angry here.\u201d</p> <p>Two acclaimed writers are working on scripts for the story of the fire. Gillian Slovo, the South African writer and daughter of the anti-apartheid politician Joe Slovo, will be working on a verbatim play based on interviews conducted with survivors.</p> <p>Announcing this stage production, called <em>Grenfell: In the Words of Survivors</em>, the theatre said Slovo had spent five years \u201cgaining the confidence of community members and recording their accounts\u201d. Opening in July, it will be told in three parts and include a filmed final section in which survivors and the bereaved speak. \u201cGrenfell was something the National Theatre needed to address on the national stage. It was our responsibility to make sure their voices were heard,\u201d Slovo has said.</p> <p>The story will be told for TV by the screenwriter and film-maker Peter Kosminsky, who adapted and directed <em>Wolf Hall</em> for television. Kosminsky and his collaborator, producer Ahmed Peerbux, are making a three-part drama, but it is a long-term project and not likely to be broadcast for at least two years. \u201cAhmed and I started working on this series a few days after the fire,\u201d Kosminsky told the <em>Observer</em>. \u201cHe had been volunteering at the scene, along with his wife.\u201d</p> <p>Kosminsky, who has worked in television for 43 years, regards this drama as his most challenging so far. \u201cWe have spoken to a great number of people off camera from every group affected, from the bereaved to volunteers and those who worked in hospitals. In fact, the actual fire will take up a fairly small part of the drama,\u201d he said. \u201cWe will begin months before and will cover the botched refurbishments. And we will go on long after the fire, well into the woeful responses to the disaster.\u201d</p> <p>Kosminsky\u2019s script will use multiple perspectives, he said. The team attended each day of the public inquiry.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--supporting\" data-media-id=\"2d489db84c9aad1757b5edf0546e3ed0261fc688\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/2d489db84c9aad1757b5edf0546e3ed0261fc688/0_210_3505_4379/800.jpg\" alt=\"Portrait of Steve McQueen wearing a black shirt\" width=\"800\" height=\"1000\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Steve McQueen\u2019s silent film about the aftermath of Grenfell has been praised by local residents.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Frazer Harrison/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>\u201cOur drama certainly is not going to be the story of one or two people trapped on the 17th floor, or something like that,\u201d said Kosminsky, adding: \u201cI have never worked on anything that has gone into something as deeply and fully. We want to understand what has occurred and to make a small contribution to making sure that nothing like this can ever happen again in this country. It was a terrible tragedy and it was avoidable.\u201d</p> <p>Not every resident is opposed to the productions. One survivor, Hanan Wahabi, spoke out in support of the play: \u201cWith each year that goes by our pain doesn\u2019t stop, but we must keep our stories alive and at the forefront of people\u2019s minds. When people watch this play, I have no doubt that they will want to do something, whether through word of mouth or through action.\u201d Wahabi lost her home in the tower, and her brother and his entire family died in the fire.</p> <p>\u201cThe domino effect of this is the power. The National Theatre is supporting us by taking our stories beyond our borders of north Kensington,\u201d she added.</p> <p>The hostile response from some residents contrasts with reaction to Steve McQueen\u2019s acclaimed silent film on Grenfell, showing at the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/2023/apr/05/steve-mcqueen-grenfell-review-serpentine-gallery-london\" title=\"\">Serpentine Gallery</a> this month. \u201cMcQueen was seen as a local. He did it with sensitivity and for the right reasons,\u201d said O\u2019Connell.</p> <p>Maria, who has lived on the west London estate containing Grenfell for 21 years, also felt the play and drama series were being produced prematurely. With the inquiry report yet to be published, criminal prosecutions in connection with the fire may not begin until 2024. Maria said: \u201cIt\u2019s still very sensitive, and the guilty people are not even behind bars.\u201d</p> <p>A BBC spokesperson said: \u201cWe are extremely mindful of the impact that a project of this nature can have on those whose lives have been personally affected, and this programme will be made with the utmost sensitivity and respect. It will draw on over five years of research to ask not only how this terrible tragedy was able to occur, but also what we can learn to ensure that the events at Grenfell can never happen again elsewhere.\u201d</p>", "newspaperPageNumber": "5", "wordcount": "1199", "firstPublicationDate": "2023-04-16T05:00:01Z", "isInappropriateForSponsorship": "false", "isPremoderated": "false", "lastModified": "2023-04-16T05:00:01Z", "newspaperEditionDate": "2023-04-16T00:00:00Z", "productionOffice": "UK", "publication": "The Observer", "shortUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/p/nn5nn", "shouldHideAdverts": "false", "showInRelatedContent": "true", "thumbnail": "https://media.guim.co.uk/4078cc491d24eda900d14bbd7df216620433201e/0_283_5976_3586/500.jpg", "legallySensitive": "false", "lang": "en", "isLive": "true", "bodyText": "The BBC and the National Theatre are facing rising hostility over two high-profile productions about the Grenfell Tower fire disaster, with survivors and bereaved family members urging both institutions to drop their plans. Some have even threatened to disrupt BBC attempts to film near the tower for its series, Grenfell, which will dramatise the tragic events around the night of 14 June 2017 in west London, when 72 people died as a result of the blaze. Others claim the National Theatre is stealing their experiences, despite its play being based on survivors\u2019 first-hand accounts. A petition calling on the BBC to scrap its series has so far attracted more than 50,000 signatures. Nabil Choucair, who lost his mother, sister, brother-in-law and three nieces in the fire, said: \u201cOur identity is being stolen. They don\u2019t listen to us \u2013 and now they want to do a play? A series? Who asked for this?\u201d Both the upcoming play and the planned TV drama are to be based on lengthy periods of research conducted locally and will be constructed from the testimony of many of those closest to the tragedy. But the strength of local opposition exposes how raw feelings remain around ownership of the truth about the fire, and the high level of suspicion about even well-intended interventions from national institutions. Maryam Adam, who escaped from the burning tower while three months pregnant, said she felt blindsided by the plans. \u201cWho are they talking to? Who gives them permission to do these things and then tell us after? Before you do this sort of thing, you should get our permission, because this is our pain, our story,\u201d said Adam, who gave evidence to the public inquiry into the disaster. David O\u2019Connell, a leaseholder on the Lancaster West estate, which includes Grenfell, voiced concerns that both productions were coming too soon to be seen as definitive. The inquiry into the tragedy closed last year, and its eagerly awaited final report, likely to run into thousands of pages, is yet to be published. O\u2019Connell said: \u201cThe BBC series is useless unless it shows something new and revelatory. The proper way you tell the story happens 20 years after, when all the hidden stuff is out. \u201cThere\u2019s no way people want to be part of this BBC series. It\u2019s a fictionalised version of my lived experience. For us it\u2019s repetitive and triggering.\u201d Frank, the next of kin of two people who died in the fire, said that the way in which some people had learned about the planned BBC series and National Theatre production had infuriated many. \u201cThe BBC series was announced through a newspaper report, posted in one of our WhatsApp groups, and the play was a letter in the mail.\u201d He warned that the backlash would hamper any attempts to film by the BBC in the area of north Kensington where the remains of Grenfell still stand. \u201cIt will be very hard for the BBC to do any filming here without cooperation \u2013 and we won\u2019t cooperate because people are very angry here.\u201d Two acclaimed writers are working on scripts for the story of the fire. Gillian Slovo, the South African writer and daughter of the anti-apartheid politician Joe Slovo, will be working on a verbatim play based on interviews conducted with survivors. In fact, the actual fire will take up a fairly small part of the drama,\u201d he said. \u201cWe will begin months before and will cover the botched refurbishments. And we will go on long after the fire, well into the woeful responses to the disaster.\u201d Kosminsky\u2019s script will use multiple perspectives, he said. The team attended each day of the public inquiry. \u201cOur drama certainly is not going to be the story of one or two people trapped on the 17th floor, or something like that,\u201d said Kosminsky, adding: \u201cI have never worked on anything that has gone into something as deeply and fully. We want to understand what has occurred and to make a small contribution to making sure that nothing like this can ever happen again in this country. It was a terrible tragedy and it was avoidable.\u201d Not every resident is opposed to the productions. One survivor, Hanan Wahabi, spoke out in support of the play: \u201cWith each year that goes by our pain doesn\u2019t stop, but we must keep our stories alive and at the forefront of people\u2019s minds. When people watch this play, I have no doubt that they will want to do something, whether through word of mouth or through action.\u201d Wahabi lost her home in the tower, and her brother and his entire family died in the fire. \u201cThe domino effect of this is the power. The National Theatre is supporting us by taking our stories beyond our borders of north Kensington,\u201d she added. The hostile response from some residents contrasts with reaction to Steve McQueen\u2019s acclaimed silent film on Grenfell, showing at the Serpentine Gallery this month. \u201cMcQueen was seen as a local. the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/grenfell-tower-fire\" title=\"\">Grenfell Tower fire disaster,</a> with survivors and bereaved family members urging both institutions to drop their A petition calling on the BBC to scrap its series has so far attracted more than 50,000 signatures.</p> <p>Nabil Choucair, who lost his mother, sister, brother-in-law and three nieces in the fire, said: \u201cOur identity is being stolen. They don\u2019t listen to us \u2013 and now they want to do a play? A series? Who asked for this?\u201d</p> <p>Both the upcoming play and the planned TV drama are to be based on lengthy periods of research conducted locally and will be constructed from the testimony of many of those closest to the tragedy.</p> <p>But the strength of local opposition exposes how raw feelings remain around ownership of the truth about the fire, and the high level of suspicion about even well-intended interventions from national institutions.</p> <p>Maryam Adam, who escaped from the burning tower while three months pregnant, said she felt blindsided by the plans. \u201cWho are they talking to? Who gives them permission to do these things and then tell us after? Before you do this sort of thing, you should get our permission, because this is our pain, our story,\u201d said Adam, who gave <a href=\"https://www.grenfelltowerinquiry.org.uk/evidence/maryam-yusuf-adam-read-evidence\" title=\"\">evidence</a> to the public inquiry into the disaster.</p> <p>David O\u2019Connell, a leaseholder on the Lancaster West estate, which includes Grenfell, voiced concerns that both productions were coming too soon to be seen as definitive. The inquiry into the tragedy closed last year, and its eagerly awaited final report, likely to run into thousands of pages, is yet to be published.</p> <p>O\u2019Connell said: \u201cThe BBC series is useless unless it shows something new and revelatory. The proper way you tell the story happens 20 years after, when all the hidden stuff is out.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"612662a24fc63c8da50e698fa06825e0551245bd\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/612662a24fc63c8da50e698fa06825e0551245bd/0_67_3500_2100/1000.jpg\" alt=\"The remains of Grenfell Tower completely shrouded in white tarpaulins with a green heart logo at the top of the covering, seen from the air in its surroundings\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Grenfell Tower in 2018.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Rick Findler/PA</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>\u201cThere\u2019s no way people want to be part of this BBC series. It\u2019s a fictionalised version of my lived experience. For us it\u2019s repetitive and triggering.\u201d</p> <p>Frank, the next of kin of two people who died in the fire, said that the way in which some people had learned about the planned BBC series and National Theatre production had infuriated many. \u201cThe BBC series was announced through a newspaper report, posted in one of our WhatsApp groups, and the play was a letter in the mail.\u201d</p> <p>He warned that the backlash would hamper any attempts to film by the BBC in the area of north Kensington where the remains of Grenfell still stand. \u201cIt will be very hard for the BBC to do any filming here without cooperation \u2013 and we won\u2019t cooperate because people are very angry here.\u201d</p> <p>Two acclaimed writers are working on scripts for the story of the fire. Gillian Slovo, the South African writer and daughter of the anti-apartheid politician Joe Slovo, will be working on a verbatim play based on interviews conducted with survivors.</p> <p>Announcing this stage production, called <em>Grenfell: In the Words of Survivors</em>, the theatre said Slovo had spent five years \u201cgaining the confidence of community members and recording their accounts\u201d. Opening in July, it will be told in three parts and include a filmed final section in which survivors and the bereaved speak. \u201cGrenfell was something the National Theatre needed to address on the national stage. It was our responsibility to make sure their voices were heard,\u201d Slovo has said.</p> <p>The story will be told for TV by the screenwriter and film-maker Peter Kosminsky, who adapted and directed <em>Wolf Hall</em> for television. Kosminsky and his collaborator, producer Ahmed Peerbux, are making a three-part drama, but it is a long-term project and not likely to be broadcast for at least two years. \u201cAhmed and I started working on this series a few days after the fire,\u201d Kosminsky told the <em>Observer</em>. \u201cHe had been volunteering at the scene, along with his wife.\u201d</p> <p>Kosminsky, who has worked in television for 43 years, regards this drama as his most challenging so far. \u201cWe have spoken to a great number of people off camera from every group affected, from the bereaved to volunteers and those who worked in hospitals. In fact, the actual fire will take up a fairly small part of the drama,\u201d he said. \u201cWe will begin months before and will cover the botched refurbishments. And we will go on long after the fire, well into the woeful responses to the disaster.\u201d</p> <p>Kosminsky\u2019s script will use multiple perspectives, he said. The team attended each day of the public inquiry.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--supporting\" data-media-id=\"2d489db84c9aad1757b5edf0546e3ed0261fc688\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/2d489db84c9aad1757b5edf0546e3ed0261fc688/0_210_3505_4379/800.jpg\" alt=\"Portrait of Steve McQueen wearing a black shirt\" width=\"800\" height=\"1000\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Steve McQueen\u2019s silent film about the aftermath of Grenfell has been praised by local residents.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Frazer Harrison/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>\u201cOur drama certainly is not going to be the story of one or two people trapped on the 17th floor, or something like that,\u201d said Kosminsky, adding: \u201cI have never worked on anything that has gone into something as deeply and fully. We want to understand what has occurred and to make a small contribution to making sure that nothing like this can ever happen again in this country. It was a terrible tragedy and it was avoidable.\u201d</p> <p>Not every resident is opposed to the productions. One survivor, Hanan Wahabi, spoke out in support of the play: \u201cWith each year that goes by our pain doesn\u2019t stop, but we must keep our stories alive and at the forefront of people\u2019s minds. When people watch this play, I have no doubt that they will want to do something, whether through word of mouth or through action.\u201d Wahabi lost her home in the tower, and her brother and his entire family died in the fire.</p> <p>\u201cThe domino effect of this is the power. The National Theatre is supporting us by taking our stories beyond our borders of north Kensington,\u201d she added.</p> <p>The hostile response from some residents contrasts with reaction to Steve McQueen\u2019s acclaimed silent film on Grenfell, showing at the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/2023/apr/05/steve-mcqueen-grenfell-review-serpentine-gallery-london\" title=\"\">Serpentine Gallery</a> this month. \u201cMcQueen was seen as a local. He did it with sensitivity and for the right reasons,\u201d said O\u2019Connell.</p> <p>Maria, who has lived on the west London estate containing Grenfell for 21 years, also felt the play and drama series were being produced prematurely. With the inquiry report yet to be published, criminal prosecutions in connection with the fire may not begin until 2024. Maria said: \u201cIt\u2019s still very sensitive, and the guilty people are not even behind bars.\u201d</p> <p>A BBC spokesperson said: \u201cWe are extremely mindful of the impact that a project of this nature can have on those whose lives have been personally affected, and this programme will be made with the utmost sensitivity and respect. It will draw on over five years of research to ask not only how this terrible tragedy was able to occur, but also what we can learn to ensure that the events at Grenfell can never happen again elsewhere.\u201d</p>", "bodyTextSummary": "The BBC and the National Theatre are facing rising hostility over two high-profile productions about the Grenfell Tower fire disaster, with survivors and bereaved family members urging both institutions to drop their plans. Some have even threatened to disrupt BBC attempts to film near the tower for its series, Grenfell, which will dramatise the tragic events around the night of 14 June 2017 in west London, when 72 people died as a result of the blaze. Others claim the National Theatre is stealing their experiences, despite its play being based on survivors\u2019 first-hand accounts. A petition calling on the BBC to scrap its series has so far attracted more than 50,000 signatures. Nabil Choucair, who lost his mother, sister, brother-in-law and three nieces in the fire, said: \u201cOur identity is being stolen. They don\u2019t listen to us \u2013 and now they want to do a play? A series? Who asked for this?\u201d Both the upcoming play and the planned TV drama are to be based on lengthy periods of research conducted locally and will be constructed from the testimony of many of those closest to the tragedy. But the strength of local opposition exposes how raw feelings remain around ownership of the truth about the fire, and the high level of suspicion about even well-intended interventions from national institutions. Maryam Adam, who escaped from the burning tower while three months pregnant, said she felt blindsided by the plans. \u201cWho are they talking to? Who gives them permission to do these things and then tell us after? Before you do this sort of thing, you should get our permission, because this is our pain, our story,\u201d said Adam, who gave evidence to the public inquiry into the disaster. David O\u2019Connell, a leaseholder on the Lancaster West estate, which includes Grenfell, voiced concerns that both productions were coming too soon to be seen as definitive. The inquiry into the tragedy closed last year, and its eagerly awaited final report, likely to run into thousands of pages, is yet to be published. O\u2019Connell said: \u201cThe BBC series is useless unless it shows something new and revelatory. The proper way you tell the story happens 20 years after, when all the hidden stuff is out.\n\u201cThere\u2019s no way people want to be part of this BBC series. It\u2019s a fictionalised version of my lived experience. For us it\u2019s repetitive and triggering.\u201d Frank, the next of kin of two people who died in the fire, said that the way in which some people had learned about the planned BBC series and National Theatre production had infuriated many. \u201cThe BBC series was announced through a newspaper report, posted in one of our WhatsApp groups, and the play was a letter in the mail.\u201d He warned that the backlash would hamper any attempts to film by the BBC in the area of north Kensington where the remains of Grenfell still stand. \u201cIt will be very hard for the BBC to do any filming here without cooperation \u2013 and we won\u2019t cooperate because people are very angry here.\u201d Two acclaimed writers are working on scripts for the story of the fire. Gillian Slovo, the South African writer and daughter of the anti-apartheid politician Joe Slovo, will be working on a verbatim play based on interviews conducted with survivors. Announcing this stage production, called Grenfell: In the Words of Survivors, the theatre said Slovo had spent five years \u201cgaining the confidence of community members and recording their accounts\u201d. Opening in July, it will be told in three parts and include a filmed final section in which survivors and the bereaved speak. \u201cGrenfell was something the National Theatre needed to address on the national stage. It was our responsibility to make sure their voices were heard,\u201d Slovo has said. The story will be told for TV by the screenwriter and film-maker Peter Kosminsky, who adapted and directed Wolf Hall for television. Kosminsky and his collaborator, producer Ahmed Peerbux, are making a three-part drama, but it is a long-term project and not likely to be broadcast for at least two years. \u201cAhmed and I started working on this series a few days after the fire,\u201d Kosminsky told the Observer. \u201cHe had been volunteering at the scene, along with his wife.\u201d Kosminsky, who has worked in television for 43 years, regards this drama as his most challenging so far. \u201cWe have spoken to a great number of people off camera from every group affected, from the bereaved to volunteers and those who worked in hospitals. In fact, the actual fire will take up a fairly small part of the drama,\u201d he said. \u201cWe will begin months before and will cover the botched refurbishments. And we will go on long after the fire, well into the woeful responses to the disaster.\u201d Kosminsky\u2019s script will use multiple perspectives, he said. The team attended each day of the public inquiry.\n\u201cOur drama certainly is not going to be the story of one or two people trapped on the 17th floor, or something like that,\u201d said Kosminsky, adding: \u201cI have never worked on anything that has gone into something as deeply and fully. We want to understand what has occurred and to make a small contribution to making sure that nothing like this can ever happen again in this country. It was a terrible tragedy and it was avoidable.\u201d Not every resident is opposed to the productions. One survivor, Hanan Wahabi, spoke out in support of the play: \u201cWith each year that goes by our pain doesn\u2019t stop, but we must keep our stories alive and at the forefront of people\u2019s minds. When people watch this play, I have no doubt that they will want to do something, whether through word of mouth or through action.\u201d Wahabi lost her home in the tower, and her brother and his entire family died in the fire. \u201cThe domino effect of this is the power. The National Theatre is supporting us by taking our stories beyond our borders of north Kensington,\u201d she added. The hostile response from some residents contrasts with reaction to Steve McQueen\u2019s acclaimed silent film on Grenfell, showing at the Serpentine Gallery this month. \u201cMcQueen was seen as a local. He did it with sensitivity and for the right reasons,\u201d said O\u2019Connell. Maria, who has lived on the west London estate containing Grenfell for 21 years, also felt the play and drama series were being produced prematurely. With the inquiry report yet to be published, criminal prosecutions in connection with the fire may not begin until 2024. Maria said: \u201cIt\u2019s still very sensitive, and the guilty people are not even behind bars.\u201d A BBC spokesperson said: \u201cWe are extremely mindful of the impact that a project of this nature can have on those whose lives have been personally affected, and this programme will be made with the utmost sensitivity and respect.
