has called for a federal takeover of child protection, saying foster parents have told her Indigenous children are being put back into the hands of abusers.</p> <p>Nampijinpa Price levelled serious accusations against the Northern Territory\u2019s child protection agency on Sunday as she reiterated her opposition to enshrining an Indigenous voice in the constitution.</p> <p>\u201cThe only sort of referendum I would support right now is if we put the lives and the responsibility of children into the federal arena,\u201d she told the ABC.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/07/at-least-three-senior-liberals-pushed-back-against-indigenous-voice-opposition-in-shadow-cabinet-meeting\">At least three senior Liberals pushed back against Indigenous voice opposition in shadow cabinet meeting</a> </p> </aside>  <p>\u201cThat\u2019s a referendum I could get behind because I think we absolutely need a review of how that is, or isn\u2019t, working across the board.\u201d</p> <p>Asked whether that meant the federal government would run child protection, Nampijinpa Price said: \u201cAbsolutely. If it\u2019s actually doing it from the perspective of upholding the human rights of our children, regardless of racial heritage, in this country, then that\u2019s something I would support.\u201d</p> <p>The Coalition has been riven by divisions in the wake of the Liberal party\u2019s decision earlier this month to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/05/peter-dutton-confirms-liberals-will-oppose-indigenous-voice-to-parliament\">actively oppose enshrining an Indigenous voice</a> in the constitution.</p> <p>The shadow minister for Indigenous Australians, Julian Leeser, <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/13/julian-leeser-says-liberal-proposal-for-symbolic-indigenous-constitutional-recognition-not-enough\">quit the frontbench</a> and the former minister Ken Wyatt <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/06/former-indigenous-affairs-minister-ken-wyatt-quits-liberals-over-partys-voice-opposition\">resigned from the Liberal party</a> after the opposition leader, Peter Dutton, vowed to campaign for \u2018no\u2019 in the referendum to be held between October and December.</p> <ul> <li><p><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\">Sign up for </a><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\">email newsletters for your daily news roundup</a></strong></p></li> </ul> <p>Over the past few days the NT police minister and prominent Aboriginal Territorians have <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/13/report-it-to-police-aboriginal-territorians-react-angrily-to-peter-duttons-claims-of-alice-springs-violence\">reacted angrily to Dutton\u2019s claims</a> that young Indigenous children \u201care being sexually assaulted still on a regular basis\u201d in Alice Springs, saying he should report any wrongdoing to police for further investigation.</p> <p>The NT police minister, Kate Worden, said Nampijinpa Price\u2019s claim that children were being returned to abusive homes was incorrect.</p> <p>She said her department had heard \u201ccrickets\u201d from the senator and Dutton who had not made any reports about cases of child abuse they had referenced in media conferences.</p> <p>\u201cYou can\u2019t just claim these things and then walk away \u2026 she needs to come forward and make declarations [to police] that support that view,\u201d Worden told reporters in Darwin.</p> <p>\u201cThe kinship care system has gone a long way in making sure that we can develop relationships within communities and find safe places for young people within family and culture, but never at the expense of the safety of the child.\u201d</p> <p>Worden said the territory\u2019s child protection system did not place children back into communities or homes where abusive perpetrators lived.</p> <p>\u201cThe current statements that are being made around our child protection system are incorrect,\u201d she said.</p> <p>\u201cEverybody has a role to play in child safety \u2026 we\u2019re all in this together. None of us have our heads buried in the sand and it\u2019s really important that we don\u2019t get destabilised.\u201d</p> <p>The minister said many child protection workers in the department would feel \u201cdemonised\u201d by Nampijinpa Price\u2019s comments.</p> <p>On Sunday the assistant minister for Indigenous Australians, Malarndirri McCarthy, called for an end to \u201cthe political blame game and the political football that Alice Springs has become\u201d. McCarthy reiterated that it was mandatory to refer any sexual assault accusations to police.</p> <p>Nampijinpa Price, who was with Dutton during the opposition leader\u2019s visit to Alice Springs last week, was asked repeatedly by the ABC Insiders host David Speers whether the claims had been reported to police.</p> <p>\u201cLook, David, we\u2019re not stupid. Peter Dutton is a former policeman, he knows what needs to be done,\u201d the Warlpiri-Celtic woman said on Sunday.</p> <p>Nampijinpa Price alleged that the child protection agency \u2013 Territory Families, Housing and Communities \u2013 was prioritising keeping Indigenous children with their family or community over safety considerations.</p> <p>She said Territory Families \u201chas known about these circumstances\u201d and she was assuming that the agency had reported the incidents to police. She said she also knew foster parents had \u201cdone the due diligence and they have done the reporting in these circumstances\u201d.</p> <p>Asked whether she was making the serious accusation that \u201cTerritory Families are putting kids in the hands of abusers\u201d, Nampijinpa Price replied: \u201cThat\u2019s right.\u201d</p> <p>\u201cAnd this is what foster parents have been telling me \u2013 and I have no reason to believe that these concerned parents are making these stories up, especially when I\u2019ve heard them over and over again.\u201d</p> <p>The NT\u2019s Care and Protection of Children Act states that in any decision involving a child, \u201cthe best interests of the child are the paramount concern\u201d. Territory Families, Housing and Communities was contacted for a response.</p> <p>Nampijinpa Price said there was a \u201csense of shame or denial around Indigenous kids\u201d, who were \u201cnot given the same reverence as someone like Grace Tame is given when they want to be heard, when they want their voices to be heard\u201d.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/mar/06/calls-to-increase-allowances-for-indigenous-carers-to-keep-children-connected-with-culture\">Calls to increase allowances for Indigenous carers to keep children connected with culture</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Nampijinpa Price, who has been <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2023/apr/15/jacinta-price-kerrynne-liddle-opposition-portfolio-indigenous-australians\">tipped as being in line for a potential promotion after the departure of Leeser</a> from the frontbench, said this would be \u201ca decision for Mr Dutton and for the leadership\u201d.</p> <p>During an interview with Sky News earlier on Sunday, McCarthy said it would be no surprise if Nampijinpa Price was promoted \u201cbecause we know what we\u2019re getting in terms of her position with the voice\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cI think what I\u2019d say to all Australians is this: be watchful of the way people conduct themselves throughout this whole campaign,\u201d McCarthy, a NT Labor senator and Yanyuwa Garrawa woman, said.</p> <p>McCarthy said she was Nampijinpa Price has called for a federal takeover of child protection, saying foster parents have told her Indigenous children are being put back into the hands of abusers. Nampijinpa Price levelled serious accusations against the Northern Territory\u2019s child protection agency on Sunday as she reiterated her opposition to enshrining an Indigenous voice in the constitution. \u201cThe only sort of referendum I would support right now is if we put the lives and the responsibility of children into the federal arena,\u201d she told the ABC. \u201cThat\u2019s a referendum I could get behind because I think we absolutely need a review of how that is, or isn\u2019t, working across the board.\u201d Asked whether that meant the federal government would run child protection, Nampijinpa Price said: \u201cAbsolutely. If it\u2019s actually doing it from the perspective of upholding the human rights of our children, regardless of racial heritage, in this country, then that\u2019s something I would support.\u201d The Coalition has been riven by divisions in the wake of the Liberal party\u2019s decision earlier this month to actively oppose enshrining an Indigenous voice in the constitution. The shadow minister for Indigenous Australians, Julian Leeser, quit the frontbench and the former minister Ken Wyatt resigned from the Liberal party after the opposition leader, Peter Dutton, vowed to campaign for \u2018no\u2019 in the referendum to be held between October and December. Sign up for Guardian Australia\u2019s free morning and afternoon email newsletters for your daily news roundup Over the past few days the NT police minister and prominent Aboriginal Territorians have reacted angrily to Dutton\u2019s claims that young Indigenous children \u201care being sexually assaulted still on a regular basis\u201d in Alice Springs, saying he should report any wrongdoing to police for further investigation. The NT police minister, Kate Worden, said Nampijinpa Price\u2019s claim that children were being returned to abusive homes was incorrect. She said her department had heard \u201ccrickets\u201d from the senator and Dutton who had not made any reports about cases of child abuse they had referenced in media conferences. \u201cYou can\u2019t just claim these things and then walk away \u2026 she needs to come forward and make declarations [to police] that support that view,\u201d Worden told reporters in Darwin. \u201cThe kinship care system has gone a long way in making sure that we can develop relationships within communities and find safe places for young people within family and culture, but never at the expense of the safety of the child.\u201d Worden said the territory\u2019s child protection system did not place children back into communities or homes where abusive perpetrators lived. \u201cThe current statements that are being made around our child protection system are incorrect,\u201d she said. \u201cEverybody has a role to play in child safety \u2026 we\u2019re all in this together. None of us have our heads buried in the sand and it\u2019s really important that we don\u2019t get destabilised.\u201d The minister said many child protection workers in the department would feel \u201cdemonised\u201d by Nampijinpa Price\u2019s comments. On Sunday the assistant minister for Indigenous Australians, Malarndirri McCarthy, called for an end to \u201cthe political blame game and the political football that Alice Springs has become\u201d. McCarthy reiterated that it was mandatory to refer any sexual assault accusations to police. Nampijinpa Price, who was with Dutton during the opposition leader\u2019s visit to Alice Springs last week, was asked repeatedly by the ABC Insiders host David Speers whether the claims had been reported to police. \u201cLook, David, we\u2019re not stupid. Peter Dutton is a former policeman, he knows what needs to be done,\u201d the Warlpiri-Celtic woman said on Sunday. Nampijinpa Price alleged that the child protection agency \u2013 Territory Families, Housing and Communities \u2013 was prioritising keeping Indigenous children with their family or community over safety considerations. She said Territory Families \u201chas known about these circumstances\u201d and she was assuming that the agency had reported the incidents to police. She said she also knew foster parents had \u201cdone the due diligence and they have done the reporting in these circumstances\u201d. Asked whether she was making the serious accusation that \u201cTerritory Families are putting kids in the hands of abusers\u201d, Nampijinpa Price replied: \u201cThat\u2019s right.\u201d \u201cAnd this is what foster parents have been telling me \u2013 and I have no reason to believe that these concerned parents are making these stories up, especially when I\u2019ve heard them over and over again.\u201d The NT\u2019s Care and Protection of Children Act states that in any decision involving a child, \u201cthe best interests of the child are the paramount concern\u201d. Territory Families, Housing and Communities was contacted for a response. Nampijinpa Price said there was a \u201csense of shame or denial around Indigenous kids\u201d, who were \u201cnot given the same reverence as someone like Grace Tame is given when they want to be heard, when they want their voices to be heard\u201d. Nampijinpa Price, who has been tipped as being in line for a potential promotion after the departure of Leeser from the frontbench, said this would be \u201ca decision for Mr Dutton and for the leadership\u201d. During an interview with Sky News earlier on Sunday, McCarthy said it would be no surprise if Nampijinpa Price was promoted \u201cbecause we know what we\u2019re getting in terms of her position with the voice\u201d. \u201cI think what I\u2019d say to all Australians is this: be watchful of the way people conduct themselves throughout this whole campaign,\u201d McCarthy, a NT Labor senator and Yanyuwa Garrawa woman, said. Nampijinpa Price levelled serious accusations against the Northern Territory\u2019s child protection agency on Sunday as she reiterated her opposition to enshrining an Indigenous voice in the constitution. \u201cThe only sort of referendum I would support right now is if we put the lives and the responsibility of children into the federal arena,\u201d she told the ABC.\n\u201cThat\u2019s a referendum I could get behind because I think we absolutely need a review of how that is, or isn\u2019t, working across the board.\u201d Asked whether that meant the federal government would run child protection, Nampijinpa Price said: \u201cAbsolutely. If it\u2019s actually doing it from the perspective of upholding the human rights of our children, regardless of racial heritage, in this country, then that\u2019s something I would support.\u201d The Coalition has been riven by divisions in the wake of the Liberal party\u2019s decision earlier this month to actively oppose enshrining an Indigenous voice in the constitution. The shadow minister for Indigenous Australians, Julian Leeser, quit the frontbench and the former minister Ken Wyatt resigned from the Liberal party after the opposition leader, Peter Dutton, vowed to campaign for \u2018no\u2019 in the referendum to be held between October and December. Sign up for Guardian Australia\u2019s free morning and afternoon email newsletters for your daily news roundup Over the past few days the NT police minister and prominent Aboriginal Territorians have reacted angrily to Dutton\u2019s claims that young Indigenous children \u201care being sexually assaulted still on a regular basis\u201d in Alice Springs, saying he should report any wrongdoing to police for further investigation. The NT police minister, Kate Worden, said Nampijinpa Price\u2019s claim that children were being returned to abusive homes was incorrect. She said her department had heard \u201ccrickets\u201d from the senator and Dutton who had not made any reports about cases of child abuse they had referenced in media conferences. \u201cYou can\u2019t just claim these things and then walk away \u2026 she needs to come forward and make declarations [to police] that support that view,\u201d Worden told reporters in Darwin. \u201cThe kinship care system has gone a long way in making sure that we can develop relationships within communities and find safe places for young people within family and culture, but never at the expense of the safety of the child.\u201d Worden said the territory\u2019s child protection system did not place children back into communities or homes where abusive perpetrators lived. \u201cThe current statements that are being made around our child protection system are incorrect,\u201d she said. \u201cEverybody has a role to play in child safety \u2026 we\u2019re all in this together. None of us have our heads buried in the sand and it\u2019s really important that we don\u2019t get destabilised.\u201d The minister said many child protection workers in the department would feel \u201cdemonised\u201d by Nampijinpa Price\u2019s comments. On Sunday the assistant minister for Indigenous Australians, Malarndirri McCarthy, called for an end to \u201cthe political blame game and the political football that Alice Springs has become\u201d. McCarthy reiterated that it was mandatory to refer any sexual assault accusations to police. Nampijinpa Price, who was with Dutton during the opposition leader\u2019s visit to Alice Springs last week, was asked repeatedly by the ABC Insiders host David Speers whether the claims had been reported to police. \u201cLook, David, we\u2019re not stupid. Peter Dutton is a former policeman, he knows what needs to be done,\u201d the Warlpiri-Celtic woman said on Sunday. Nampijinpa Price alleged that the child protection agency \u2013 Territory Families, Housing and Communities \u2013 was prioritising keeping Indigenous children with their family or community over safety considerations. She said Territory Families \u201chas known about these circumstances\u201d and she was assuming that the agency had reported the incidents to police. She said she also knew foster parents had \u201cdone the due diligence and they have done the reporting in these circumstances\u201d. Asked whether she was making the serious accusation that \u201cTerritory Families are putting kids in the hands of abusers\u201d, Nampijinpa Price replied: \u201cThat\u2019s right.\u201d \u201cAnd this is what foster parents have been telling me \u2013 and I have no reason to believe that these concerned parents are making these stories up, especially when I\u2019ve heard them over and over again.\u201d The NT\u2019s Care and Protection of Children Act states that in any decision involving a child, \u201cthe best interests of the child are the paramount concern\u201d. "developerCommunity": "true"}, "section": {"id": "world", "webTitle": "World news", "webUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/world", "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/world", "editions": [{"id": "world", "webTitle": "World news", "webUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/world", "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/world", "code": "default"}]}, "isHosted": false, "pillarId": "pillar/news", "pillarName": "News"}, {"id": "lifeandstyle/2023/apr/16/i-feel-privileged-to-have-them-why-you-should-befriend-a-resident-possum", "type": "article", "sectionId": "lifeandstyle", "sectionName": "Life and style", "webPublicationDate": "2023-04-16T00:00:03Z", "webTitle": "\u2018I feel privileged to have them\u2019: why you should befriend a resident possum", "webUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/lifeandstyle/2023/apr/16/i-feel-privileged-to-have-them-why-you-should-befriend-a-resident-possum", "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/lifeandstyle/2023/apr/16/i-feel-privileged-to-have-them-why-you-should-befriend-a-resident-possum", "fields": {"headline": "\u2018I feel privileged to have them\u2019: why you should befriend a resident possum", "standfirst": "<p>While some consider them pests, <strong>Bronwen Scott</strong> has an affinity with the coppery brushtail that\u2019s taken up lodgings in her roof \u2013 and she\u2019s not alone</p>", "trailText": "While many consider them pests, <strong>Bronwen Scott</strong> has an affinity with the coppery brushtail possum that\u2019s taken up lodgings in her roof", "byline": "Bronwen Scott", "main": "<figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"1065c0bd368bcd385c233bf9993ba3f6ea15e015\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/1065c0bd368bcd385c233bf9993ba3f6ea15e015/0_0_4524_3216/1000.jpg\" alt=\"An Australian brush tail possum climbs a tree against a Melbourne cityscape.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"711\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Is this arboreal life: a common brushtail possum climbs a tree in Melbourne\u2019s Flagstaff Gardens. Common brushtails and ringtails survive in parks, gardens and along riverbanks.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: David Crosling/AAP</span> </figcaption> </figure>", "body": "<p>At last light, a silhouette detaches itself from the shadows on the roof. It is round and fuzzy, with a tail curled into a comma at the tip. It clambers on to the power line and trundles along until it reaches the trees. Parking its fluffy backside on a branch, it starts on the ripening fruit that will never make it to my kitchen.</p> <p>My guest is a coppery brushtail possum, the blinged-up tropical cousin of the common brushtail possum so familiar in cities. Although I rarely see it, the possum makes itself known. The heavy footfall shifting between march and gallop. The scrabbling as it squeezes into impossibly small spaces. And the staccato hisses and growls when it runs into a rival. Brushies might tolerate humans in their territories, but they draw the line at other possums.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"ee325124cbe7a80ffe803a4cc2f2913c5486068c\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/ee325124cbe7a80ffe803a4cc2f2913c5486068c/0_0_5500_3660/1000.jpg\" alt=\"A coppery brushtail climbs a tree branch at night.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"665\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Hello possum: the coppery brushtail.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: robertharding/Alamy</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>As cities expand and urban areas join up like colonies of mould in a petri dish, wildlife habitat is declining. But coppery brushtail possums and common ringtail possums survive in parks and gardens and in the green ribbons along riverbanks. Roof spaces and garages are substitutes for tree hollows. Fruit trees, vegetable gardens and bird feeders supplement their natural diet. There are new dangers to face \u2013 possums must deal with dogs, cats and traffic \u2013 but still they persist.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/sport/2022/oct/28/like-a-thoroughbred-race-horse-inside-the-high-speed-high-stakes-world-of-pigeon-racing\">\u2018Like a thoroughbred race horse\u2019: inside the high-speed, high-stakes world of pigeon racing</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Harry Saddler, a nature writer based in Melbourne, shared his house with a family of common ringtails. When the old metal grille dislodged from an air vent in the wall, possums found the space to their liking.</p> <p>\u201cI first noticed them when I saw leaves sticking out of the air vent,\u201d Saddler says. \u201cI could tell the number of possums in residence by the amount of sunlight coming through.\u201d</p> <p>At one point, two adult and two young possums lived in these cosy lodgings. Only pinpricks of light made it past them.</p> <p>They are polite house guests. Unlike the boisterous and pugnacious brushtails, ringtails tend to keep to themselves.</p> <p>While brushtails and ringtails are the most commonly encountered possums in cities, they are not the only species present. Krefft\u2019s gliders and sugar gliders often live in well-vegetated locations. Even where numerous, they can be difficult to spot.</p> <p>\u201cThey\u2019re fast, small and well-camouflaged,\u201d Saddler says.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--supporting\" data-media-id=\"eceabf865c491b8f5603bfb887c92dd2bd0cccb9\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/eceabf865c491b8f5603bfb887c92dd2bd0cccb9/0_0_3342_5100/655.jpg\" alt=\"A common brushtail possum peeks out of a nest box.\" width=\"655\" height=\"1000\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u2018Nest boxes are appreciated\u2019: a common brushtail possum checks in.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Auscape/Universal Images Group/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>In the absence of tree hollows, these little possums will adopt alternative accommodation. A nest box project in a Melbourne park was a near instant success.</p> <p>\u201cWithin three weeks of installing nest boxes, gliders had moved in.\u201d</p> <p>Mammal expert Dr Stephen Jackson of the Australian Museum, also suggests nest boxes as a good way to encourage possums.</p> <p>\u201cIn residential areas, possums live where they can,\u201d Jackson says. \u201cNest boxes are appreciated. They need to be high enough off the ground and to have the right-sized entrances to avoid predation.</p> <p>\u201cBut also plant trees and shrubs that possums will use for food and which will eventually provide real tree hollows.\u201d</p> <p>The presence of urban possums can have a positive impact on other wildlife. The powerful owl \u2013 Australia\u2019s largest owl and one of its rarest \u2013 preys on ringtail possums. The abundance of ringtails in Melbourne and other east coast cities supports a small population of these birds.</p> <p>\u201cWhen I was a kid, the powerful owl was an almost mythical bird of wet gullies,\u201d Saddler says. \u201cNow it\u2019s out and about in the suburbs of Australia\u2019s largest cities.\u201d</p> <p>Of the 30 or so possum species in Australia, only a few live close to us. Even in rural areas, encountering other species is a rarity. They might be around, but most possums conduct their lives in darkness and avoid people.</p> <p>Jennifer Browne is an enthusiastic birdwatcher and avid gardener. Her property on the Atherton Tablelands in Queensland is planted with native trees that attract wildlife. Green ringtail possums sometimes visit.</p> <p>With their grey-green fur and quiet demeanour, these possums blend in among the lush vegetation. Seeing them requires luck or determination and often both.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"7e32e6e546155e50dd4f795f95a12cfbb68516bd\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/7e32e6e546155e50dd4f795f95a12cfbb68516bd/0_0_4860_3761/1000.jpg\" alt=\"A green ringtail possum on a shrub. \" width=\"1000\" height=\"774\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">The elusive green ringtail possum.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Minden Pictures/Alamy</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>\u201cThey are very different from the coppery brushtails,\u201d Browne says. \u201cThey stay away from the house. There\u2019s no rampaging across the roof, no brawling on the back deck.</p> <p>\u201cI feel privileged to have them living so close.\u201d</p> <p>I feel the same way about the brushtail in my ceiling.</p> <p>Just before first light, it will retrace its path along the power line, its outline rounder and its movement slower, and then wriggle back under the corrugated iron. I never see the return journey, but I hear the possum making little sounds above my head. They could be snores \u2026 or complaints about the noisy neighbour downstairs.</p> <h2>How to build a possum-friendly nest box </h2> <p>Possum nest boxes are widely available <a href=\"https://www.wildlifevictoria.org.au/wildlife-information/buy-a-possum-box\">ready-made</a>, but local wildlife organisations and government departments offer detailed <a href=\"https://wildlife.org.au/how-to-build-a-nest-box/\">instructions</a> on <a href=\"https://www.environment.nsw.gov.au/research-and-publications/publications-search/guide-to-making-a-possum-house\">building your own</a>, tailored to the species in your area.</p> <p><strong>Size:</strong> Boxes should be deep and roomy with an overhang or baffle to protect the interior from rain. The diameter of the entrance is important: 100-120mm for brushtails, 60-80mm for ringtails and 30-50mm for smaller gliders.</p> <p><strong>Location:</strong> Possum nest boxes should be attached to trees and located in protected spots, away from wind and direct sunlight. Height depends on the species. For brushtails and ringtails, about 3 to 5 metres off the ground is adequate. Sugar gliders and their relatives spend their time in the tree tops so prefer their nest boxes at about 4 to 8 metres from the ground.</p>", "wordcount": "890", "commentCloseDate": "2023-04-19T00:00:03Z", "commentable": "true", "firstPublicationDate": "2023-04-16T00:00:03Z", "isInappropriateForSponsorship": "false", "isPremoderated": "false", "lastModified": "2023-04-16T00:21:51Z", "productionOffice": "AUS", "publication": "theguardian.com", "shortUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/p/nm9tq", "shouldHideAdverts": "false", "showInRelatedContent": "true", "thumbnail": "https://media.guim.co.uk/1065c0bd368bcd385c233bf9993ba3f6ea15e015/0_309_4524_2714/500.jpg", "legallySensitive": "false", "lang": "en", "isLive": "true", "bodyText": "At last light, a silhouette detaches itself from the shadows on the roof. It is round and fuzzy, with a tail curled into a comma at the tip. It clambers on to the power line and trundles along until it reaches the trees. Parking its fluffy backside on a branch, it starts on the ripening fruit that will never make it to my kitchen. My guest is a coppery brushtail possum, the blinged-up tropical cousin of the common brushtail possum so familiar in cities. Although I rarely see it, the possum makes itself known. The heavy footfall shifting between march and gallop. The scrabbling as it squeezes into impossibly small spaces. And the staccato hisses and growls when it runs into a rival. Brushies might tolerate humans in their territories, but they draw the line at other possums. As cities expand and urban areas join up like colonies of mould in a petri dish, wildlife habitat is declining. But coppery brushtail possums and common ringtail possums survive in parks and gardens and in the green ribbons along riverbanks. Roof spaces and garages are substitutes for tree hollows. Fruit trees, vegetable gardens and bird feeders supplement their natural diet. There are new dangers to face \u2013 possums must deal with dogs, cats and traffic \u2013 but still they persist. Harry Saddler, a nature writer based in Melbourne, shared his house with a family of common ringtails. When the old metal grille dislodged from an air vent in the wall, possums found the space to their liking. \u201cI first noticed them when I saw leaves sticking out of the air vent,\u201d Saddler says. \u201cI could tell the number of possums in residence by the amount of sunlight coming through.\u201d At one point, two adult and two young possums lived in these cosy lodgings. Only pinpricks of light made it past them. They are polite house guests. Unlike the boisterous and pugnacious brushtails, ringtails tend to keep to themselves. While brushtails and ringtails are the most commonly encountered possums in cities, they are not the only species present. Krefft\u2019s gliders and sugar gliders often live in well-vegetated locations. Even where numerous, they can be difficult to spot. \u201cThey\u2019re fast, small and well-camouflaged,\u201d Saddler says. In the absence of tree hollows, these little possums will adopt alternative accommodation. A nest box project in a Melbourne park was a near instant success. \u201cWithin three weeks of installing nest boxes, gliders had moved in.\u201d Mammal expert Dr Stephen Jackson of the Australian Museum, also suggests nest boxes as a good way to encourage possums. \u201cIn residential areas, possums live where they can,\u201d Jackson says. \u201cNest boxes are appreciated. They need to be high enough off the ground and to have the right-sized entrances to avoid predation. \u201cBut also plant trees and shrubs that possums will use for food and which will eventually provide real tree hollows.\u201d The presence of urban possums can have a positive impact on other wildlife. The powerful owl \u2013 Australia\u2019s largest owl and one of its rarest \u2013 preys on ringtail possums. The abundance of ringtails in Melbourne and other east coast cities supports a small population of these birds. \u201cWhen I was a kid, the powerful owl was an almost mythical bird of wet gullies,\u201d Saddler says. \u201cNow it\u2019s out and about in the suburbs of Australia\u2019s largest cities.\u201d Of the 30 or so possum species in Australia, only a few live close to us. Even in rural areas, encountering other species is a rarity. They might be around, but most possums conduct their lives in darkness and avoid people. Jennifer Browne is an enthusiastic birdwatcher and avid gardener. Her property on the Atherton Tablelands in Queensland is planted with native trees that attract wildlife. Green ringtail possums sometimes visit. With their grey-green fur and quiet demeanour, these possums blend in among the lush vegetation. Seeing them requires luck or determination and often both. \u201cThey are very different from the coppery brushtails,\u201d Browne says. \u201cThey stay away from the house. There\u2019s no rampaging across the roof, no brawling on the back deck. \u201cI feel privileged to have them living so close.\u201d I feel the same way about the brushtail in my ceiling. Just before first light, it will retrace its path along the power line, its outline rounder and its movement slower, and then wriggle back under the corrugated iron. I never see the return journey, but I hear the possum making little sounds above my head. They could be snores \u2026 or complaints about the noisy neighbour downstairs. How to build a possum-friendly nest box Possum nest boxes are widely available ready-made, but local wildlife organisations and government departments offer detailed instructions on building your own, tailored to the species in your area. Size: Boxes should be deep and roomy with an overhang or baffle to protect the interior from rain. The diameter of the entrance is important: 100-120mm for brushtails, 60-80mm for ringtails and 30-50mm for smaller gliders. Location: Possum nest boxes should be attached to trees and located in protected spots, away from wind and direct sunlight. Height depends on the species. For brushtails and ringtails, about 3 to 5 metres off the ground is adequate. Sugar gliders and their relatives spend their time in the tree tops so prefer their nest boxes at about 4 to 8 metres from the ground.", "charCount": "5334", "shouldHideReaderRevenue": "false", How to build a possum-friendly nest box Possum nest boxes are widely available ready-made, but local wildlife organisations and government departments offer detailed instructions on building your own, tailored to the species in your area. Size: Boxes should be deep and roomy with an overhang or baffle to protect the interior from rain. The diameter of the entrance is important: 100-120mm for brushtails, 60-80mm for ringtails and 30-50mm for smaller gliders. Industry groups such as the Electric Vehicle Council have warned Australia\u2019s status \u201cas the world\u2019s dumping ground for dated, high-emission vehicles will be cemented if the Albanese government does not move swiftly to catch up with new fuel efficiency standards\u201d as announced by the Biden administration this week. \u201cThis shift in position from the US is of monumental consequence to Australia,\u201d the council\u2019s CEO, Behyad Jafari, said in a statement. \u201cCar companies will now be racing to meet the more stringent standards set in the US, Europe, China, and even New Zealand. \u201cWe know the federal government has been slowly working on a new EV policy,\u201d Jafari said. \u201cThis move in the US means the buzzer has sounded. The time for talk is done, we know the action we need to take, so let\u2019s introduce strong new fuel efficiency standards now. \u201cThe US first introduced fuel efficiency standards into law in the 1970s and has been strengthening them since then,\u201d Jafari said. \u201cIn 2023, Australia has a discussion paper about them. It\u2019s ridiculous.\u201d Industry members have also expressed concern the strategy, once announced, will require further public feedback, with the result that changes to emissions standards low-emissions vehicles under $60,000, compared with 26 available in the UK.</p> <p>Impetus has only gathered internationally since then, with the US becoming the latest country to flag much stricter emissions standards. Industry sources say the federal climate change and energy minister, Chris Bowen, will release the strategy ahead of an event in western Sydney on Wednesday. \u201cWe\u2019re expecting something quite ambitious,\u201d said one of those briefed. Bowen\u2019s office declined to confirm the timing or details of the strategy, saying only it \u201cwill be launched soon.\u201d The plans will \u201cprovide a nationally consistent, comprehensive framework to looking at supply, demand and infrastructure needs for cleaner and cheaper vehicles\u201d, a spokesperson said.\nAustralia is the only OECD nation to not have, or be in the process of developing, fuel efficiency standards. Bowen said last August that Australian consumers could choose between just eight low-emissions vehicles under $60,000, compared with 26 available in the UK. Impetus has only gathered internationally since then, with the US becoming the latest country to flag much stricter emissions standards. Under proposed changes by the US environment protection agency unveiled this week, the share of electric vehicles in the world\u2019s second-largest car market could soar from about 6% last year to 66% by 2032. \u201cMotor vehicle emissions contribute to ozone, particulate matter and air toxins, which are linked with premature death and other serious health impacts, including respiratory illness, cardiovascular problems, and cancer,\u201d the US EPA said, while explaining the need for the new standards. Sign up for a weekly email featuring our best reads \u201cIn addition, there is consensus that the effects of climate change represent a rapidly growing threat to human health and the environment \u2026 caused by greenhouse gas emissions from human activity, including motor vehicle transportation.\u201d Asked whether Australia could reach EV sales of two out of every three purchases, Bowen said he believed \u201cAustralia can catch up with the rest of the world\u201d. \u201cYou look at the United Kingdom, United States, other countries are way ahead of Australia\u2019s electric vehicle sales,\u201d he said on Thursday. \u201cThat is partly a supply issue because we don\u2019t require low emissions vehicles to be sent to Australia because we don\u2019t have fuel efficiency standards,\u201d Bowen said. \u201cIt\u2019s partly because the previous government talked down electric vehicles. \u201cBattery electric vehicle sales are 2.5 times higher in the first quarter of this year, than [the same time] last year,\u201d he said. The government said it had received more than 500 submissions to the strategy\u2019s consultation paper. They represent more than 1,500 individuals and more than 200 organisations.\nIndustry groups such as the Electric Vehicle Council have warned Australia\u2019s status \u201cas the world\u2019s dumping ground for dated, high-emission vehicles will be cemented if the Albanese government does not move swiftly to catch up with new fuel efficiency standards\u201d as announced by the Biden administration this week. \u201cThis shift in position from the US is of monumental consequence to Australia,\u201d the council\u2019s CEO, Behyad Jafari, said in a statement. \u201cCar companies will now be racing to meet the more stringent standards set in the US, Europe, China, and even New Zealand. \u201cWe know the federal government has been slowly working on a new EV policy,\u201d Jafari said. \u201cThis move in the US means the buzzer has sounded. The time for talk is done, we know the action we need to take, so let\u2019s introduce strong new fuel efficiency standards now. \u201cThe US first introduced fuel efficiency standards into law in the 1970s and has been strengthening them since then,\u201d Jafari said. \u201cIn 2023, Australia has a discussion paper about them. the Tote has needed saving \u2013 but Shane Hilton plans on it being the last.</p> <p>Hilton and his partner, Leanne, are hoping to secure the legendary live music venue\u2019s future with a funding model that could keep other struggling venues out of the hands of developers.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/music/2023/apr/14/we-need-to-break-down-their-power-why-australian-music-is-facing-an-existential-threat\">\u2018We need to break down their power\u2019: why Australian music is facing an \u2018existential threat\u2019</a> </p> </aside>  <p>\u201c<a href=\"https://www.pozible.com/project/the-last-chance-to-save-the-tote\">The Last Chance to Save the Tote</a>\u201d is being called the biggest crowdfunding campaign in live music history and hailed as a model for preserving other band rooms, which are coming increasingly under threat in the age of Spotify and gastro-pubs.</p> <p>They just need $3m to do it.</p> <p>Hilton is so committed to saving the venue, he has even started tattooing onto his body the names of donors who chip in what he calls a \u201clife membership\u201d of $1000.</p> <p>The campaign has raised almost $1.5m, but the pair need to double it before 6 May. The current owners, Jon Perring and Sam Crupi, then have to accept the offer.</p> <ul> <li><p><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\">Sign up for Guardian Australia\u2019s free morning and afternoon</a></strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\"> </a><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/australia-news/2022/oct/29/email-newsletters-guardian-australia-best-daily-news-emails-newsletter-free-sign-up-inbox-subscribe-headlines?CMP=copyembed\">email newsletters for your daily news roundup</a></strong></p></li> </ul> <p>The Tote is listed for sale through commercial property agents Miglic Dean with a price tag of $6-$6.6m. While the listing spruiks the hotel\u2019s rich history of four decades \u201csupporting Melbourne\u2019s live music scene\u201d, it is also marketed as an opportunity for \u201ca mixed use development incorporating the hotel as its centrepiece\u201d.</p> <p>In their Pozible campaign, Shane and Leanne \u2013 who also co-own Melbourne\u2019s Last Chance rock\u2019n\u2019roll bar \u2013 claim the hotel risks being \u201cturned into apartments, or worse one of those fucking awful redeveloped tissue box pubs run to make as much money as possible with no dirty rock &amp; roll in sight\u201d.</p> <p>If they can buy the Tote, Hilton said they will set up a charitable trust to manage the venue, which they say would be the first of its kind in Australia.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"9f933b2ebcc1e5db6c973097e3c94f3648cefc44\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/9f933b2ebcc1e5db6c973097e3c94f3648cefc44/0_0_5184_3888/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Shane Hilton and his partner Leanne outside The Tote. Melbourne. Australia\" width=\"1000\" height=\"750\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Shane Hilton and his partner Leanne outside The Tote. Melbourne. Australia</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Shane Hilton</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>\u201cWe\u2019re going to put it into a trust, and then eventually what will happen is we will hand the building over to an independent charitable organisation who will own the Tote for the rest of time,\u201d Hilton said.</p> <p>Melbourne is a cornerstone of Australia\u2019s live music scene \u2013 last year, five out of the six Australian acts at Glastonbury were from Victoria.</p> <p>But venues have struggled since the end of the pandemic lockdowns, with Jimmy Hornet and Colour both closing.</p> <p>The Curtin was saved when the new owner agreed to sign a 10-year lease, and Footscray\u2019s Hotel Westwood is for sale.</p> <p>Hilton said without drastic intervention, closures will keep happening.</p> <p>\u201cLive music venues and pubs are on prime development land,\u201d he said. \u201cSo many of them have been turned into apartments. These spaces are disappearing.</p> <p>\u201cWe think it\u2019s a model that can be used in other venues to protect spaces. It\u2019s something governments could look at and fine-tune.</p> <p>\u201cThese spaces are important, they\u2019re community spaces. It\u2019s not the walls, it\u2019s what\u2019s happens within them.\u201d</p> <p>On top of the fundraiser, the pair have contributed what they could. While he cannot say how much the offer was, it came under the asking price of $6.6m.</p> <p>Hilton said: \u201cWe put an offer in, that we could basically afford.</p> <p>\u201cWe put it in with the hope that the campaign would make it to $3m.\u201d</p> <p>So far, in terms of his tattoo offer to life membership donors, he has over 70 tattoos.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"c35771532742824467bfac2ed8aa405a14e8e2e6\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/c35771532742824467bfac2ed8aa405a14e8e2e6/0_100_3000_1802/1000.jpg\" alt=\"A tattoo artist tattoos a person\u2019s foot\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">As part of the campaign, Hilton is getting the names of life members tattooed on him.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Photoyunist</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>\u201cI\u2019ve got over 650 still to be tattooed on me,\u201d Hilton said. \u201cI\u2019ve got plenty of room, I\u2019ve only got one whole leg, a thigh and my buttocks.</p> <p>\u201cEven if we fail, it\u2019ll be a reminder of how strong the community is.\u201d</p> <p>Pozible\u2019s Alan Crabbe said the campaign was the biggest in live music history.</p> <p>\u201cWith over 5200 supporters, I cannot think of any campaign in the music industry internationally that has garnered so much support,\u201d Crabbe said.</p> <p>\u201cOverwhelmingly, many supporters view this venue as a Melbourne institution, that must be preserved for future generations to experience "true", "bodyText": "This is not the first time that Collingwood\u2019s the Tote has needed saving \u2013 but Shane Hilton plans on it being the last. Hilton and his partner, Leanne, are hoping to secure the legendary live music venue\u2019s future with a funding model that could keep other struggling venues out of the hands of developers. \u201cThe Last Chance to Save the Tote\u201d is being called the biggest crowdfunding campaign in live music history and hailed as a model for preserving other band rooms, which are coming increasingly under threat in the age of Spotify and gastro-pubs. They just need $3m to do it. Hilton is so committed to saving the venue, he has even started tattooing onto his body the names of donors who chip in what he calls a \u201clife membership\u201d of $1000. The campaign has raised almost $1.5m, but the pair need to double it before 6 May. The current owners, Jon Perring and Sam Crupi, then have to accept the offer. Sign up for Guardian Australia\u2019s free morning and afternoon email newsletters for your daily news roundup The Tote is listed for sale through commercial property agents Miglic Dean with a price tag of $6-$6.6m. While the listing spruiks the hotel\u2019s rich history of four decades \u201csupporting Melbourne\u2019s live music scene\u201d, it is also marketed as an opportunity for \u201ca mixed use development incorporating the hotel as its centrepiece\u201d. In their Pozible campaign, Shane and Leanne \u2013 who also co-own Melbourne\u2019s Last Chance rock\u2019n\u2019roll bar \u2013 claim the hotel risks being \u201cturned into apartments, or worse one of those fucking awful redeveloped tissue box pubs run to make as much money as possible with no dirty rock &amp; roll in sight\u201d. If they can buy the Tote, Hilton said they will set up a charitable trust to manage the venue, which they say would be the first of its kind in Australia. \u201cWe\u2019re going to put it into a trust, and then eventually what will happen is we will hand the building over to an independent charitable organisation who will own the Tote for the rest of time,\u201d Hilton said. Melbourne is a cornerstone of Australia\u2019s live music scene \u2013 last year, five out of the six Australian acts at Glastonbury were from Victoria. But venues have struggled since the end of the pandemic lockdowns, with Jimmy Hornet and Colour both closing. The Curtin was saved when the new owner agreed to sign a 10-year lease, and Footscray\u2019s Hotel Westwood is for sale. Hilton said without drastic intervention, closures will keep happening. \u201cLive music venues and pubs are on prime development land,\u201d he said. \u201cSo many of them have been turned into apartments. These spaces are disappearing. \u201cWe think it\u2019s a model that can be used in other venues to protect spaces. It\u2019s something governments could look at and fine-tune. \u201cThese spaces are important, they\u2019re community spaces. It\u2019s not the walls, it\u2019s what\u2019s happens within them.\u201d On top of the fundraiser, the pair have contributed what they could. While he cannot say how much the offer was, it came under the asking price of $6.6m. Hilton said: \u201cWe put an offer in, that we could basically afford. \u201cWe put it in with the hope that the campaign would make it to $3m.\u201d So far, in terms of his tattoo offer to life membership donors, he has over 70 tattoos. \u201cI\u2019ve got over 650 still to be tattooed on me,\u201d Hilton said. \u201cI\u2019ve got plenty of room, I\u2019ve only got one whole leg, a thigh and my buttocks. \u201cEven if we fail, it\u2019ll be a reminder of how strong the community is.\u201d Pozible\u2019s Alan Crabbe said the campaign was the biggest in live music history. \u201cWith over 5200 supporters, I cannot think of any campaign in the music industry internationally that has garnered so much support,\u201d Crabbe said. \u201cOverwhelmingly, many supporters view this venue as a It\u2019s something governments could look at and fine-tune.</p> <p>\u201cThese spaces are important, they\u2019re community spaces. It\u2019s not the walls, it\u2019s what\u2019s happens within them.\u201d</p> <p>On top of the fundraiser, the pair have contributed what they could. While he cannot say how much the offer was, it came under the asking price of $6.6m.</p> <p>Hilton said: \u201cWe put an offer in, that we could basically afford.</p> <p>\u201cWe put it in with the hope that the campaign would make it to $3m.\u201d</p> <p>So far, in terms of his tattoo offer to life membership donors, he has over 70 tattoos.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"c35771532742824467bfac2ed8aa405a14e8e2e6\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/c35771532742824467bfac2ed8aa405a14e8e2e6/0_100_3000_1802/1000.jpg\" alt=\"A tattoo artist tattoos a person\u2019s foot\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">As part of the campaign, Hilton is getting the names of life members tattooed on him.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Photoyunist</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>\u201cI\u2019ve got over 650 still to be tattooed on me,\u201d Hilton said. \u201cI\u2019ve got plenty of room, I\u2019ve only got one whole leg, a thigh and my buttocks.</p> <p>\u201cEven if we fail, it\u2019ll be a reminder of how strong the community is.\u201d</p> <p>Pozible\u2019s Alan Crabbe said the campaign was the biggest in live music history.</p> <p>\u201cWith over 5200 supporters, I cannot think of any campaign in the music industry internationally that has garnered so much support,\u201d Crabbe said.</p> <p>\u201cOverwhelmingly, many supporters view this venue as a Melbourne institution, that must be preserved for future generations to experience \u2013 as they did.\u201d</p> <p>The current co-owners did not reply to questions before deadline, but said previously they would look \u201cfavourably at proposals that ensure a live music component\u201d.</p>", "bodyTextSummary": "This is not the first time that Collingwood\u2019s the Tote has needed saving \u2013 but Shane Hilton plans on it being the last. Hilton and his partner, Leanne, are hoping to secure the legendary live music venue\u2019s future with a funding model that could keep other struggling venues out of the hands of developers.\n\u201cThe Last Chance to Save the Tote\u201d is being called the biggest crowdfunding campaign in live music history and hailed as a model for preserving other band rooms, which are coming increasingly under threat in the age of Spotify and gastro-pubs. They just need $3m to do it. Hilton is so committed to saving the venue, he has even started tattooing onto his body the names of donors who chip in what he calls a \u201clife membership\u201d of $1000. The campaign has raised almost $1.5m, but the pair need to double it before 6 May. The current owners, Jon Perring and Sam Crupi, then have to accept the offer. Sign up for Guardian Australia\u2019s free morning and afternoon email newsletters for your daily news roundup The Tote is listed for sale through commercial property agents Miglic Dean with a price tag of $6-$6.6m. While the listing spruiks the hotel\u2019s rich history of four decades \u201csupporting Melbourne\u2019s live music scene\u201d, it is also marketed as an opportunity for \u201ca mixed use development incorporating the hotel as its centrepiece\u201d. In their Pozible campaign, Shane and Leanne \u2013 who also co-own Melbourne\u2019s Last Chance rock\u2019n\u2019roll bar \u2013 claim the hotel risks being \u201cturned into apartments, or worse one of those fucking awful redeveloped tissue box pubs run to make as much money as possible with no dirty rock & roll in sight\u201d. If they can buy the Tote, Hilton said they will set up a charitable trust to manage the venue, which they say would be the first of its kind in Australia.\n\u201cWe\u2019re going to put it into a trust, and then eventually what will happen is we will hand the building over to an independent charitable organisation who will own the Tote for the rest of time,\u201d Hilton said. Melbourne is a cornerstone of Australia\u2019s live music scene \u2013 last year, five out of the six Australian acts at Glastonbury were from Victoria. But venues have struggled since the end of the pandemic lockdowns, with Jimmy Hornet and Colour both closing. The Curtin was saved when the new owner agreed to sign a 10-year lease, and Footscray\u2019s Hotel Westwood is for sale. Hilton said without drastic intervention, closures will keep happening. \u201cLive music venues and pubs are on prime development land,\u201d he said. \u201cSo many of them have been turned into apartments. These spaces are disappearing. \u201cWe think it\u2019s a model that can be used in other venues to protect spaces. It\u2019s something governments could look at and fine-tune. \u201cThese spaces are important, they\u2019re community spaces. It\u2019s not the walls, it\u2019s what\u2019s happens within them.\u201d On top of the fundraiser, the pair have contributed what they could. While he cannot say how much the offer was, it came under the asking price of $6.6m. Hilton said: \u201cWe put an offer in, that we could basically afford. \u201cWe put it in with the hope that the campaign would make it to $3m.\u201d So far, in terms of his tattoo offer to life membership donors, he has over 70 tattoos.\n\u201cI\u2019ve got over 650 still to be tattooed on me,\u201d Hilton said. \u201cI\u2019ve got plenty of room, I\u2019ve only got one whole leg, a thigh and my buttocks. \u201cEven if we fail, it\u2019ll be a reminder of how strong the community is.\u201d Pozible\u2019s Alan Crabbe said the campaign was the biggest in live music history. \u201cWith over 5200 supporters, I cannot think of any campaign in the music industry internationally that has garnered so much support,\u201d Crabbe said. \u201cOverwhelmingly, many supporters view this venue as a Melbourne institution, that must be preserved for future generations to experience \u2013 as they did.\u201d The current co-owners did not reply to questions before deadline, but said previously
